THE RED PAGE.
Literature and Tobacco.
TO say that literature is closely associated
with tobacco is a feeble and unworthy
periphrasis. Shall vye not rather acclaim
their identity, since their association is
too intimate and subtle ever to be recounted.
Tobacco is the rolling’ cloud, literature the emerg-
lng lightning. Is there a novelist among us who
does not smoke? A journalist? And, .saving
Mr. Swinburne, a poet ? But it will be said that
literature was produced during thousands of
years by non-smokers like Homer, Virgil, Dante,
and Chaucer. There is no gainsaying it. Admit
that certain people figured on an ancient Egyptian
tombstone were not smoking, but onlv blowing
glass. Concede that no tobacco pipe ever found
not even one which was plucked from the jaws of
an ancient Milesian in a tumulus at Kildare, can
boast a greater antiquity than Elizabeth. Yet
the Greeks are known to have inhaled the smoke
of dried coltsfoot to cure chest troubles, and the
lhracians and Scythians are reported to have
enjoyed the smoke of certain fragrant nlants and
of hemp-seed. They had no tobacco,‘but they
may have had what we have lost ? What of that
rare
Nepenthes which the wife of Thone
In. Egypt gave to Jove-born Helena ?
The ages give and take away. Horace knew not
tea, the author of Beowulf was a stranger to
bananas ; and we, also, are born too early for
delights yet unimagined by the epicure.
Is not literature itself a great narcotic? A few
days ago three bookmen were discussing the
question whether books really influence men in
their lives and conduct. Two maintained that
the moral influence of literature is a mere delu-
Sl °n- The third indignantly met their denial;
and the wrangle was only settled on the basis that
literature is at least a, great narcotic.that it
soothes and liberates the native energies of the
mind. What are those companions of our waiting
and agitated hours; what are those precious
fragments of verse and prose which we brin»
forth at odd moments, chanting them to the wind
or to the rumble of a train, amid the racket and
suspense of business ; what are these but the in-,
stmctively selected narcotic grains of literature ;
and what is our sensuous enjoyment of them if it
is not comparable to the paradisaical calm and
aloofness of the smoker ? Is it for any other result
that one murmurs a passage like this from
Hyperion:
His palace bright
Bastion’d with pyramids of glowing gold,
And touch’d with shade of bronzed obelisks,
Glared a blood-red through all its thousand coilrts.
Arches, and domes, and fiery galleries ;
And all its curtains of Aurorian clouds
flushed angerly ; while sometimes eagle’s wings
C nuee.n before by gods or wondering men,
Harken’d the place ; and neighing steeds were heard,
A of heard before by god# or wondering men ?
Or wherein, but in its simple power to empty the
nnnd of worry alid fill it with vast spaces and
cadences, dwells the magic of Milton’s
Where the great vision of the guarded mount
Looks towards Namancos and Bayona’s hold ?
By long use and habit these unattached peace-
makers and anaesthetists present themselves
silently, swiftly, when the care of little things and
the unhappy frustrations of the hour become too
much for us. In . all fine poetry and prose there is
secreted a narcotic principle that never dies -that
pervades its very fragments. In a few discon-
nected words, whose context is forgotten, whose
meaning when taken alone is nothing, this blessed
influence will survive; There is a passage in one
of Mr. Francis Thompson’s poems which begins :
On Ararat there grew a vine. . . .
Often and often above the clamour of the street,
and the never-so-wise charming of our industrious
singers, we have enjoyed the vastness and cadence
of those dissevered words; “On Ararat there
grew a vine.”
It was the narcotic in that blessed word Meso-
potamia that drugged the old lady into peace of
mind for so many years. We office heard a bishop
preach a sing-song sermon on the text “In the
valley of Achor, by the way of the sea.” And
still, after infinite removal and lapse of time, we
see the light fading out of the great eastern win-
dow of that northern cathedral church, and recall
the warmth and the seclusion and the morocco
scent of the hymn-books, as the voice repeats,
th f ! I v : ,n nd JTI in ? offc nasal undulations—“ln

Byron won to it when he wrote the stanza:
A king- sate on the rocky brow

And ships by thousands lay below
And men in nations. Ail were his :
■He counted them at break of day,
nut when the sun set, where were they?

.It,is one of the regrets of Biography that Dr.
Johnson did not smoke. His case was peculiar.
He approved, but refrained. In this he seems to
have been influenced by the general reaction
against tobacco which set in with the eighteenth
century. bnuff alone was sanctioned by the
elect.... . Dr Johnson rebelled against this law,
yet kept it. He said: “Smoking has gone out.
lo be sure, it is a shocking tiling, blowing smoke
out of our mouths into other people’s mouths,
eyes, And noses, and having the same thing done
t° us. Yet I cannot account why a thing which
requires so little exertion, and yet preserves the
mind trom total vacuity, should have gorip out.
Every man has something by which he calms
himself: beating with his feet or so.”
It was not social convention that kept Professor
Huxley from smoking for forty years. For that
period, he told a sectional meeting of the British
Association, tobacco had been a deadly poison to
him. ‘As a medical student I tried to smoke,
but at every attempt tobacco stretched me upon
tile floor. On entering the Navy I again triecl to
smoke, and again met with defeat. I hated
tobacco. I could have almost lent my support to
any institution that had for its object the execu-
tion of-smokers. A few years ago I was in Brit-
tany with some friends. It was a miserable,
drenching day, and they looked so happy and
comfortable with their pipes that I thought I
would try a cigar. I did so. I smoked that
cigar ; it was delicious. From that moment I
was a changed man, and now I feel that smoking
m moderation is a comforting and laudable prac-
tice, and productive of good. There is no more
harm in a pipe than there is in a cup of tea.”
A grain of nicotine, we have been told by our
severer counsellers, kills a cat. But “ a grain of
theme,” it is said, with happy adaptation, “killsa
frog instantly ” When Pope Pius IX. received a
certain Englishman of high rank in private audi-
ence, he offered him a cigar. “You will find this
very good,” he said. “ Thank you, your Holiness
but lam not addicted to this vice.” “It isn’t a
vice, cuttingly replied the Holy Father, himself
or , Probably you would be.” George

with the Creation. —From The Academy.
A New Religion.
THE Hau-haus will look askance at C. P. W.
Longdill, a land and insurance agent of
Gisborne, M.L., who has invented “A
Aew Religion”—“for circulation among
adults only.” The new religion is exposed in a
70 pp. pamphlet, recently printed at Auckland,
which has this for the author’s last word—
Hence it will be seen that the only way to regenerate
mankind, and to restore them to perfect manhood and
womanhood, is by having a season each leap-year for
physical love, and to lead continent lives during the in-
tervening periods.
The author claims the leap-year idea as his own
exclusively—Longdill’s Leap-Year Love-Season
(patent applied for). The spectacle of the New
.Kchgiou.s swain waiting until he reaches a year
divisible by 4 before crooking his finger to a
n> mph, and murmuring “ Long dilly, dilly, come
and be loved ! is not without its peculiar charm ;
yet one can understand that there may still be a
ceitain human unrest, all too human unrest,
among votaries when the Gregorian calendar
misses leap-year.(as in 1900), thus necessitating a
close season of eight years.
The religion of Leap-Year Love is buttressed'by
an analogy with • “ the Ajave (A. Americaia),”
which ‘ only flowers once in from ten to seven-
teen years, according to climate, and then dies”
having waited, apparently, a trifle too long. A
considerable if vague support appears to be
inferred from the fact that during the breeding
season the male stickleback (Gasterosteus
luminus) ” is “‘beautiful beyond description.’
lhe back and eyes of the female are simply
brown ; the belly white. The eyes of the male,
on the other hand, are of the most, splendid
green colour .... and the whole fish ap-
pears as though it . was somewhat translucent,
and glowed with an internal incandescence.” It
is important that ‘‘the males of Plagiostonious
fishes . .. . are provided with claspers which serve
to retain the females,” and that “during the
season of love a musky odour is emitted by the
submaxillary glands of the crocodile, and pervades
their haunts.”
Answering his own question, “Why Leap
Year? the author affirms his belief that “it has
been ordained by God from the beginning, that
from this epoch, this shall be the year of love and
marriage of the human race for all time . .
Further,
There is no doubt, therefore, that as all. animals
ana plants have their love seasons, which vary in dis-
tance apart in harmony with the requirements or habits
of the various species, Leap Year is the love season of the
human race. Otherwise how did the custom of woman
proposing during leap year originate ?
This argument is unanswerable ; and we may
learn from its modern cogency how much our
morals owe to the march of civilisation, —
since before 1582, when leap-year was introduced
to the calendar, myriads of human beings must
. have perished miserably in their lusts, lacking the
criterion of love-seasons supplied by Pope Gregory
Xlll,—who plainly “builded better than he
knew.’’ A corroborative argument (which Mr.
Longdill does not adduce) may be found in the
old Scottish custom of leaping over a broom-stick
at the ceremony of marriage. C. P. W. Long-
dill is a humble land and insurance agent ; yet
the greatest religions of the world have had the
lowliest beginnings; and Gisborne, M.L., may
still become the goal of pilgrimages as pious and
as numerous_as those to Jerusalem and to Mecca,
Will .future ages witness a new crusade to win the
Holy Sepulchre of G. P. W. Longdill from infidel
Maorilanders ? or will rapt and believing Maori-
landers jealously exclude infidel Australians from
some holy Caaba on the site of a sacred land and
insurance office ?
These things are beyond our ken. Meanwhile,
Mr. Longdill proceeds slowly, and records that
Rams seem to make love to the ewes by tapping them
with tfie fore-leg in the brisket. ...
“ Rams seem to make love to the ewes by tap-
ping them with the fore-leg in the brisket. . . .”
Marvellous Maoriland !
S. A. Rosa writes of the Federal Treasurer’s
English:
We can hardly wonder that’the London Athenaeum
exclaims against what it terms (however erroneously)
“the Australian ‘will’ tor ‘shall’” when, we find so
eminent an Australian statesman as George Turner mis-
using the words. In delivering his first Federal Budget
he said ( Hansard p. 5678, col. 1): “ There are only a few
items with which I will trouble the House.” The will
should have been shall. As Mr. Turner was stating that
he intended to trouble the House he should have in-
dicated, by the use of, “ shall,” that he was compelled to
do so by the force of circumstances. In reply to an
interjection from Mr. Reid, Mr. Turner said, “ Yes, I will
giv.e the details of. it in a minute or two.” This will
should also have been shall, which, besides being better
English, would have implied that Sir George was impelled
by a sense of duty, or of respect for the House, to give
“ the details ” (of the new Federal expenditure). Only
when the speaker desires to express his absolute mental
independence should will be used in the first person.
(There are a few exceptions, which prove the rule). When
there is any outward compulsion, even of obligation,
shall should be used. Mr. Turner’s “ I will trouble
the House ” was as ungrammatical as the drowning Irish-
man’s exclamation, “ I will be drowned and nobody shall
save me.”
“I propose,” said Mr. Turner ( Hansard , p. 5692, col. 2),
“ toask for authority to borrow £1,000,000.” We propose
to ask for a thing-when we-offer to ask for it if some other
person, or persons, first approve of our asking for it; but
asikfr. Turner was announcing that it was his purpose to
ask for the authority to borrow the £1,000,000, he should
The use of “ lot ” in Mr. Turner’s phrase—“ We i
taking over a lot of their [the States’] Treasury work ’
is improper. “Lot,” in this sense, is rightly c.ondeninec
as a slipshod colloquialism. “ Deal ” would have been i
less objectionable word, or “ much’’—which latter wore
would probably have expressed exactly Mr. Turne
meaning.
In the following sentence Mr. Turner ungrammatical
uses the adjective “ such ” instead of the adverb “ so
“ I want to see the accounts of the Treasury kept in sue
a simple manner that any honourable member requirir
information may always be able to get it without dif
culty.” ( Hansard , p. 5674, col. 2.) “Such” applies
the nature of a thing; “so,” to the degree. “Such
simple manner ” means “ a simple manner of a particula
nature ” ; but “ so simple a manner ” means a manner o
so great a degree of simplicity that certain results wi
follow. Therefore, Mr. Turner should have said, “in
simple a manner that,” etc.
“ VVe can,” said Mr. Turner, have less drills, which wi
mean less pay.” The first “less” should be “fewer.
Tlie ungrammatical use of an adjective of quantity
bulk for an adjective of quantity in number has been
exposed and condemned by nearly every English gram
marian.
Mr. Turner has evidently forgotten that the word “cir
cumstanees ” means “ conditions that surround.” Other
wise he surely would not speak of being “ under these
circumstances ” ( Hansard , page 5674, column 1). In the
circumstances, Mr. Turner !
Mr. Turner says : “ Those figures have been very care
fully checked, and, I think, are reliable.” When the
course of a sentence is diverted by a change in the tnooc
or tense the nominative should generally be repeated or
another nominative supplied. In the quoted sentence
ii ij ,?- urner should have said, “I think they are.’
Reliable ” is a word of questionable propriety : George
Saintsbury says, “ The mere grammar of style teaches us
not to say reliable where we can say trustivorthy."
Mr. Turner said: “ I shall be only too pleased to help
them [Queensland and Tasmania] to devise some scheme
by which we can give them assistance.” “Only too
pleased” is an absurd colloquialism that one hardly
expects to find in the Budget speech of a Federal Trea
s 4!‘ er - If M>\ Turner will be too pleased, then his pleasure
will be either a nuisance or a danger. The “ only too
pleased ” colloquialism is, when analysed, found to. be
actually as impolite as it is foolish. On page 5691 of the
Commonwealth Hansard Mr, Turner is reported as
saving ; “ If there is anything I can explain to them [the
members], I shall be only too glad to do so.” Did Mr.
Timer lament his (presumed) weakness for becoming
glad? If not, then he should have said, “I shall be
extremely glad.”
Possibly Mr. Turner cannot be expected to excel in
grammar as well as in finance ; yet I may be forgiven for
suggesting that ten minutes each morning, devoted to
assiduous study of the elements of syntax would probably
give ; readers of his next Budget speech occasion for
gratitude.
Frank Wilkinson (who for some time represented
Sydney Telegraph as African war correspondent,
and acquitted himself creditably if not brilliantly)
writes a short history of the N.S.W. Lancer
Regiment and the First Australian Horse, under
the title Australian Cavalry (Angus and
Robertson; 25.) As a literary performance the
book is poor. Strange, slipshod statements
abound: e.g. (p. 2) —
. . . Some such syllogistic fallacy as this :
Disciplined British troops were opposed to undisciplined
burghers;
Undisciplined burghers are hard to. beat; therefore
Discipline is a negligeable quantity.
Reasoning such as this bears its refutation .on its face.
It violates every law ever yet formulated by the logician
for the mystification of the illogical, and contains a self-
evident fallacy in every line. It is no syllogism at all.'
®ht, as constructed by Mr. Wilkinson, with
Mr. Wilkinson’s remarkable comment, it is a
characteristic example of Mr. Wilkinson’s hasty-
nasty writing. Apart from the English,
Australian Cavalry is a collection of military
facts and statistics which will be doubtless in-
teresting to the military persons concerned. The
coloured frontispiece, representing a pinkish Lan-
cer on a reddish horse bounding gallantly over
yellowish grass, is calculated to make sdrvant-
girls weep with admiring rapture.
Alice Eyton writes— .. :
Did you see the Eight-Hour procession march through
the streets of Sydney ? What was the predominant note
of that long, straggling array?—lndifference ! What
was the predominant note of the crowd that looked on ?
—lndifference ! The same people but recently cheered,
and cheered again, at sig'ht of military trappings, at
sound of martial music, at thought of 'the pomp and
gior.v (?) of war ! Now they see the result of bloodless
revolution. They are mute ! No enthusiasm ! Only an
occasional abortive cheer, a jibe at some familiar face, a
sorry witticism, a blank mind.
And the men forming the procession ? They
appeared to me one never-ending line of apathetic,
sunken, lead-coloured faces, pipe in mouth ; with
stunted, or overgrown, slouching figures. You
could count on your two hands, those who looked
thoroughly healthy, who betrayed the slightest idea of
what it meant to be a Man—heir to the achievements of
the Past, the repository of future possibilities. I
am not going to retract my confession of faith in Aus-
tralia. But the signs are ominous. Why should we fall
away from the strength of pioneers ? Why, at very in-
fancy, show signs of decadence ? Have we forgotten that
we must still do battle ?
Fight ! fight ! fight !—but not abroad ! Our Own
Country is our Foe, and our Beloved !
W. M. Whitney, a Sydney writer, some of
whose work has appeared in The Bulletin, will publish
shortly a book of verses to be called When Winds Awake.
Mrs. Junor, widow of the late Charles Junor, desires it
known that she has opened a book-shop in Hunter-street,
Sydney.

WOMEN AND
WARNER’S
SAFE CURE.
The health of women is of supreme im-
portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-
lutely essential to the welfare of the race
that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
SAFE CURE a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation—
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.
Read the following Letters:

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“They Sleep at Sea.”
They sailed away one summer day
With flaunting flags and streamers gay ;
I sent them laughing down the tide
(My heart was young—my heart was wide) —
I knew full well some evening bell
Would ring them home to haven free
With wealth of gold and love for me—
Dear Ships—Dear Ships at Sea !
They sailed to laughing lands afar;
Their streamers kissed the alien star;
Their canvas caught the wintry gale;
They blenched beneath the bitter hail:
And I —l pled with One o’erhead
The while they tossed in agony,
The sport of Ocean’s wanton glee—
Dear Wrecks —Dear Wrecks at Sea !
And now no more my song I sing
Of sails that mock the seagull’s wing ;
For ribs of steel and sturdy keel
Beneath the eddy rock and reel:
And I —l dream that down the stream
I float to where my loved ones be,
With hands outstretched that wait for me—
Dear Dead—Dear Dead at Sea 1
N.Q.
P.L.
Nocturne.
All my hopes reach forward to some exquisite
Forever,
Some fair realm of innocence beyond all earthly
seas,
Sitting here with Dora, while the Night rests on
the river—
Night, with pinions folded, and soft breath that
stirs the trees.
All my life is rounded, and harmonious, and
completer
Than of old I dreamed or hoped my life could ever
be;
And the joys that Love has brought are infinite
and sweeter
Than of old I dreamed that any grace could bring
to me.
For my heart was wearied with a sad, unending,
aching
Hunger for surcease of pain that quiet Death
should bring;
Bowed with bitter care I was, each nerve went
nigh to breaking:
Not for me the splendors and the solace of the
Spring 1
Then dear Dora came, and touched with lissom
fingers lightly
That dark load, which fell from me, and there was
no more pain;
But her calm eyes soothed me, and her lithe arms
drew me whitely,
And I found my youth, my hopes, my very self
again.
Sitting here with Dora, through the shadows come
the glowing
Tones that lovers only catch, the world a world
away !
And our souls are strained to silence, and our
hearts are overflowing :
Night repays with rapture desolation of the day.
Ah ! the lovely laughter of the gentle God that
nests now
In my heart, my master, but my captive self-
confessed !
Ah ! the tender fragrance of the sunny head that
rests now—
Bowed to be the nearer Him—so gladly on my
breast!
“Tell me, dear,” ... I murmur; but her lifted
eyes arrest me—
(Eyes abrim with love-content, Boft as a brooding
bird’s:
In their depths are drowned for aye the terrors
that oppressed me.)
Dora whispers, “Love of mine! why ask me!,
what are words ! ”
Just above the hill-tops a pale shred of moon
comes peeping;
Out away to southward stretch the spacious
breadths of sea;
All the arid world is dumb, and half Earth’s woes
are sleeping;—
Heaven’s awake. And Heaven below holds just
my love and me !
TAS.
FRANK MORTON.
Maoriland now exports frozen Stewart
Island oysters to London. It needs a robust taste
to stand these huge, tough molluscs; but there
once was an English King who liked his a month
old, so perhaps the new industry may succeed —in
London.
Latest medical idea is a European congress
on sea-sickness, now being held at Ostend. Dr.
F. Madeuf, of Paris, is the convener, and the
King of the Belgians (a monarch very fond of a
little trip incog, to any place where the women
are fair) is patron.
“Dram”: Instead of the cumbersome
“ wireless telegram,” I suggest Cliogram. Clio.
from the Greek for a wave. [Why not airgram ?
Also, why not simply air-letter or air-word ?
There are already 25 times more terms in the
British language than any average man knows,
and somebody is always inventing new ones. This
is called “enriching the language,” and it is
already so rich that its own people can’t digest
it.]

Dooleyisms : “ Th’ difference between
Christyan scientists an’ doctors is that Christyan
scientists thinks they’se no such thing as disease,
and doctors thinks there ain’t anything else. It
the Christyan scientists had some science, and the

The latest addition to Maoriland’s cable
tramways, making the fourth passenger cable line
in the province, will shortly be opened at
Wellington. Its total length is 36 chains, and the
total rise 387 feet, giving a steady grade of 1 in. 5£.
This grade is steeper continuously than any of the
three Dunedin lines, though there are sharp
pinches in the Roslyn and Kaikorai routes, while
the Mornington Extension is a hair-lifter (grade
about 1 in 2). The Wellington tram, however,
will be a safer line, as the-centre-rail-brake will be
attached—similar to that in use on the Rimutakas.
In its short length the line pierces three tunnels
and crosses three viaducts ; in fact, it is over and
under all the way. But, though M.L. boasts of
some of the stiffest grades in Australasia, Switzer-
land can go several better, the Glion tramway in
that country having a maximum grade of 1 in If.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 26, 1901.
The Great West.
The report of the N.S.W. Western Lands
Commission raises one question vvhich really
contains all the rest : Is the West an asset
or a liability 1 It is a gruesome tale that the
Commission has to tell. The greater part of
the West is temporarily bankrupt ; it is by
no means certain that it isn’t permanently
bankrupt. It isn’t merely a case of
drought. The additional trouble is that the
West seems to have broken loose, and
become movable instead of fixed, and to be
wandering about casually, and liable to fall
on other parts of the country and over-
whelm them, and no one has any certain idea
how to catch it and chain it up again. When
the pastoralists rushed it, not so very many
years ago, it was a rather dry and decidedly
lonely place, but otherwise it was not so
seriously unlike any other place. There
were no rabbits, no sheep, and only a thin
scattering of herbivorous animals. Nature,
having been practically left alone, had man-
aged, in the course of a million years or so,
to accumulate enough vegetation to hold the
country together. It was a sad-looking
vegetation—a mere scurf of greyish-greenish-
ne.ss in many places—but its roots kept the
Western division from rising up and leaving
for parts unknown.
* * * *
Then the squatter came, and the sheep
came in numbers far ahead of what the
country could bear, and the rabbit came
after the sheep. And the squatter, having
thoughtfully made tanks, these animals were
enabled to live where they might otherwise
have died. They attacked the scanty vegeta-
tion with fury, and used it up. As the
report sadly observes, drought succeeded
drought, and, in the absence of grass, the
stock ate the dismal native herbage out by
the roots. Whether the droughts of the
last few years are the usual condition of
the West —a condition that may be expected
to last for the rest of Time, with only
an occasional accidental break—it is not
easy to say. Pastoral settlement overran
the West during a period which was dotted
over with comparatively good years, but
whether these good years were an extraordi-
nary episode after, perhaps, three centuries
of regular drought, or whether they were
a frequent episode which happened to be
followed in this case by an unusual series of
droughts, local records don’t go far enough
back to show. But the probabilities appear
to be that drought is the normal state of
things. At all events, the West broke loose.
The roots of the feeble herbage which held
it together being eaten out by an unnatural
influx of animals which the country was
wholly unfitted to sustain, the West became
a sandstorm rather than a district. A gale
lifts up an area something like a small Ger-
man principality and throws it into the air
promiscuously. Houses are being buried.
A high fence is silted up by the moving
land-drift at the shortest notice, and another
fence erected on top of it shares the
same fate. A costly tank may be filled up in a
day, and sheep are buried, in addition to the
other modes of dying open to them. The
sandstorm digs out as well as fills in—under-
mines fences, leaves trees tottering high in
the air, balanced on anetwork of exposed roots,
and carries away the soil bodily off homestead
leases and conditional purchases. The pre-
vailing winds are from the west, and so the
western district, having broken away from
its anchorage through man and beast fooling
recklessly with the anchor, is moving east-
ward.
* • * *
Even if a few good seasons arrive the
situation is problematical. If the West is to
become again a settled locality (not settled
by population but permanently settled down
in one place) apparently the native vegeta-
tion—the kind which can hold its own against
drought—must have a chance to recover.
When the white man first went out there the
vegetation had apparently been doing
nothing save recovering since the beginning
of history, with only a moderate amount of
native game to contend against. And even
under these favorable circumstances nothing
so very great in the way of a recovery had
been effected. When the next series of good
seasons arrive—supposing they do arrive in
any definite sense—the upspringing vegeta-
tion will have the sheep and the rabbits to
contend with, and whether the West can
make any real recovery under these condi-
tions is a problem.
* * * *
If anyone could accurately estimate the
losses and gains of the pastoral industry of
the Western district it might be found that
N.S.W. has made something out of it, or,
again, it might not. The great dry land has
swallowed up the capital and labor of innu-
merable pioneers. Not only has all the
natural increase of the last eight years dis-
appeared, but 10,000,000 sheep have
vanished besides. The same country has
partly swallowed up a multitude of banks
and financial institutions, and the capital of
many private Shylocks as well. Its popula-
tion is diminishing ; the people, as well as
the sand, are blowing eastward again. It
has supplied a certain amount of wool and
other produce, many lunatics, a good deal of
poetry, a peculiarly hideous brand of
liquor, and that literary myth commonly
described as Billjim. But if the loss
of time, life, and money is set against
these products it is possible that the
Western plains are rather a liability than an
asset. Certainly, they looked like an asset
at one time, but that is no evidence. While
banks and financial institutions were raking
in 4 per cent, deposits freely, and lending
the money at good rates on western lands,
and while their western mortgages were still
regarded as good security, it appeared as if
the West was making N.S.W. rich. A
valueless mortgage, ora bogus bank-note, both
make the holder feel rich until he finds out
their valuelessness. But a great deal of the
money that was allegedly made in the West
was made by one man buying a property at
£50,000 and selling it to a still more sanguine
individual at £lOO,OOO. And when the pro-
perty finally got down to be hardly worth
accepting as a gift, the West, despite the
money some people made there, may easily
be a minus quantity on the whole.
* m * *
The proposals of the Western Lands Com-
mission are, in the main, that there should
be an effort made to share the loss. The
Government is to give extended leases at a
low rental. The banks and other institu-
tions which hold mortgages over the country
are to agree to write off part of the money
owing to them. Further, there requires to be
a hard and fast agreement against overstock-
ing when a good season comes again—if any
sudden outbreak of vegetation is eaten out
again wholesale as soon as it appears there
is no chance for the exhausted country to
make a genuine recovery. The campaign
against the hopeless, innumerable rabbit
needs to be kept up vigorously, and there is
some talk of planting belts of timber to stop
the westward movement of this wildly
unfixable territory. And these things
appear to be about all that can be done in
the matter. The West is one of the things
commonly described as the “great natural
resources of this new land.” It is
a sorrowful resource just at present,
and apparently there is very little hope
that it will ever be the resource it was once
supposed to be. Any general writing-down
of mortgages will be a severe shock to the
outward appearance of many an institution
which is still “ saving its face ” by reckoning
these assets at their paper value, but it will
not do any real injury. It is a question now
of writing-down most of the West to its real
value, if that can be arrived at, and every-
body concerned must take a hand in the
process if any good is to be done.
A Decaying Industry.
The fire and marine insurance industry is one which
no Australasian Government has yet attempted to
protect and encourage, yet it deserves encourage-
ment and protection quite as much as any other,
and more than many others. When a foreign
firm sends an agent to Australia and starts new
soapworks, it is to be regretted that the soapworks
were not started by a local firm, so that the profits
as well as the wages might remain in the country.
Still, the circumstance has alleviations. It may
cause soap to be made in the country that would
otherwise have been imported—therefore it may
cause a large amount of our money to be expended
here in wages when it would otherwise have gone
to England, or Germany, or America, to pay
wages to people in whom we feel no special interest.
Therefore, while it is best of all to have our soap-
works i n Austral ia owned by Australians, it is better
to have them here owned by foreigners than not
have them here at all. In the first case, the
wages and profits belong to Australia; in the
second case, the wages at least belong to Aus-
tralia ; but the worst case of all is when the soap
is imported, and both the wages and the profits go
abroad. But fire and marine assurance belongs to
a different industrial category. It is a kind of
business which has to be done here almost as a
matter of necessity. The only question is whether
it shall be done by Australian companies, whose
profits are spent here, or by British, German,
Austrian, French, Dutch, Chinese, Japanese, and
miscellaneous companies, whose profits are spent
abroad, so that the departure of these profits will
add a little more to the incredible sum which this
country has to send away for the support of the
multifarious absentee.
* * * •
There was a time when Australian fire and
marine insurance was mostly done by Australian
institutions. It is not so now. When the local
institutions were still mostly small and weak the
huge British companies came and squatted largely
in the land. They bought out some of the local
concerns. In other cases, local concerns died
from pressure of competition, or, as happens
in every country, from sheer mismanagement, and
(largely through the pressure of foreign competi-
tion) no one found it worth while to start new
institutions in their stead. The British offices
that started business in Australia were mostly
large, solid concerns, which had grown up in the
days when there was no foreign competition in
their own land, and with their great resources and
their great English revenues they could tide over
bad years. Many of the local offices, when
they struck a bad year and this heavy foreign
competition simultaneously, went under. The
British competitors brought nothing of any value
to this country that wasn’t here before. They
employed no labor that wouldn’t have been
employed in the same line of business in any case.
They did nothing that wouldn’t have been done
by the local companies in any case. They brought
their name and reputation, and their stationery,
and they took away their profits, and that was the
most of the story.
* * * »
In 1884 there were thirty fire and marine
insurance companies belonging to the Australian
mainland and Maoriland doing business in these
parts. The Tasmanian companies are left out of
the calculation because, until recently, some of
them did not publish revenue accounts, and the
amount of business they did 16 years ago was an
unknown quantity. The figures which follow give
some evidence of the size of the industry in these
old days:

the Australian mainland States and Maoriland:—

There is nominally another New South Wales
company, the City Mutual, still in existence, but
as the London and Lancashire (of England), really
owns it nowadays it can no longer be regarded as
an Australian institution.
* * * *
During the past 16 years, therefore, the fire and
marine insurance companies of ano
Maoriland have decreased from 30 to 14. South
Australia’s four offices are all dead. New South
Wales’ largest surviving concern is mainly run by
a great Queensland firm, but, reckoning it as a
Cornstalk institution, N.S.W. now owns four
companies and Victoria five. Sixteen years ago
the premium income of the purely Australian
companies was £855,275 ; it is now £466,048 this
despite the fact that population has increased
about 40 per cent., and business should have shown
at least some corresponding expansion. And the
insurance companies of all Australia now do a
business that is not quite two-thirds that of the
companies belonging to the one little State
of Maoriland—a place with much less than
one-fourth of Australia’s population ! The
tendency of the Maorilanders to support local
institutions, as distinguished from the tendency
of Australians to rush after anything that is
English, is one of the causes of the remarkable
prosperity of the two bleak islands. During the
last 14 years no new fire or marine insurance com-
pany has started in Australia and survived,
though 17 old ones have died. Sundry new ones,
however (such as the Hull and General, N.S.W.
Assurance, Sydney Mutual, Australian Traders’,
Queensland Mutual and Queensland General),
have started and either perished young for want
of support, or were absorbed by the persistent
Britisher.
* * * •
During the last 10 years, 10 millions of the fire
insurance risks in the city of Sydney alone have
passed into the hands of foreign institutions.
And unless some emphatic steps are taken to
protect the valuable Australian fire and
marine insurance industry, by a licensing
system which will prevent the further
influx of foreign institutions, by a heavy absentee-
tax on extraneous offices, or by some other means,
the extinction of the business seems a very adja-
cent possibility. The matter is one in which the
Labor party in Australian politics takes little in-
terest, yet there is no other party which should be
so deeply interested. There are two ways in
which the working class of any country may come
to be poor, wretched, hopeless, and underpaid.
One is through the wealth of the country being
badly distributed ; the other is through there
being very little wealth in the country to distri-
bute. Everything that tends to draw profits or
interest or rents out of Australia diminishes the
country’s wealth and in a poor country no legisla-
tion, however advanced and intelligent, will
prevent the working classes being ill-paid and
miserable. The sheep that has no wool can’t be
shorn, and the wealth that is absent can’t be taxed
or distributed in wages. For these reasons the
fact that a once-big Australian industry, hav-
ing been run on Freetrade principles, is going
where industries run on Freetrade principles
generally do go, is a matter that the Labor party
can’t afford to neglect. When a country begins,
through any cause, to suffer from the dead weight
of poverty, it is the man at the bottom who feels
the weight most. Also, it is the man at the
bottom who feels it first.
The Plot to Blacken Australia.
“White Australian ” writes from Cairns (Q.) :
It is singular that the Mulgrave Central Mill (Cairns,
Q.) carriers’strike should occur just as the Kanaka Bill
is before the public. It looks like a carefully-engineered
plan to gain public sympathy for the mill-owners against
the tyranny of the White Australian policy. The “ un-
reliability ” theory is supposed to be supported by the
little strike of 20 men, though white labor in the imme-
diate neighborhood has just completed a railway-line
from Port Douglas to Mossman, and the men inside the
Mulgrave mill, whose work is infinitely more oppressive
than the carriers’, did not come out. The events leading
up to the strike should be studied. This season the Mul-
grave co. put Hindoos to do work that hitherto was done
by white men. This the Cairns Labor people objected to,
and the matter was ventilated in the House, and the
Mulgrave mill manager was advised t.Q employ w'hite men
instead of Hindoos. Subsequently, some Bundaberg
“ farmers’ sons ” were “ obtained ”to do this work. Why
the “ farmers’ sons ” were “ obtained ” is a mystery, con-
sidering the numbers of men in search of work around
Cairns. These “ farmer’ sons ” didn’t work a week, and
their leader, who went to Cairns allegedly with White
Australian views, not only changed his views, but was in
such a hurry to make it known that he signed a paper to
that effect, which Brisbane Courier is now booming for
all it is worth. The “ unhealthy climate ” theory is dead
now, thanks to Lord Hopetoun, who publicly states that
he had to go to Cairns to get back his health 1 And
against the argument educed from the Mulgrave carriers’
strike, as to unreliability, is the fact that in Bundaberg
district —hotter than Cairns —the leading mills employ
white labor exclusively for carrier and inside work, at the
moderate pay of 225. 6d. per week of 63 hours ! It is an
open secret that efforts are to be made to evade the tacit
understanding that white labor alone should be employed
in the State-aided central mills, and this affair at the
Mulgrave is the first move of the Black Austral ians.
“ R.P.” on another aspect of the same question :
There is a deliberate effort to foster the erroneous im-
pression in the Southern States that the climate of
Queensland is distressingly hot. You may find travelled
Queenslanders in every town from Townsville and
Charters Towers to Brisbane, who will tell you that they
riever feel the heat in their State as badly as in Sydney or
Melbourne. Speaking for myself, I may say, as the result
of some dozen or more trips to Far Northern Queensland,
that during all months of that year, except December or
January, I have never suffered from the heat as much as
in N.S.W. This applies to all the Eastern country
in which are the sugar districts, and four-fifths
of the population. In the Western districts much
the same climatic conditions prevail as in the corre-
sponding sections of N.S.W. ; but I doubt whether any
Q. western town can equal Bourke in its summer heat
records. The reasons why whites do not at present work
satisfactorily in the cane-fields of Queensland are not
climatical, but economic, moral, and racial; they will not
do “ niggers’ ” work—not because they cannot, but
because their morale for work is destroyed before they
enter a cane-field by the feeling that they are degrading
themselves to do that which the local sentiment —a senti-
ment fostered in every way by the planters and their
press—makes “niggers’” work. The same sentiment
made all unskilled manual labour more or less dis-
graceful to a white man in some of the slave
States of America. If the Hindoo spreads over
the cane-fields of N.S.W., cane-cutting there also will
become niggers’ work, with the same results so far as
white labor is concerned. Remove the nigger from the
scene, and the white men—if paid a reasonable white
man’s wage—will cut cane as reliably on the Johnstone
River as they have ever done on the Richmond.
A Queensland planter (Bundaberg) on the plot:—
Until quite recently, the Queensland Central Mills
have been worked entirely with white labor, but within
the last 12 months there has been a significant agitation
amongst the irreconcilable section of private mill-owners
to make white labor appear “unreliable” in certain
localities. It will be news to many Southerners, who
may have been misled by our Tory press, to know that
the following large mills in Q. have always employed, and
do at present employ, white labor only in manufacturing
operations : —Nerang, Bauple, Isis, Gin-Gin, and Invicta
Central Mills, Huckley and Knockroe (C.S.R. Co.’s mills),
Doolbi, and Bingera private concerns. These are all in
the south. In the north the white-labor mills are : Race-
course, Marion, Pleystowe, Plane Creek, Eton, Proser-
pine and Mossman Central Mills. The Mulgrave Central
Mill is the only one of its class that does not use white
labor exclusively, and this is probably due to the Fiji “ in-
fluence” of the C.S.R. Co.’smillsin the immediate vicinity.
The total manufacturing cost of labor and rations, &c.,
last year at the white labor mills only came to “ £1 Bs.
llfd.” per ton of sugar (vide Sugar Journal, Sept., 1901).
There is not the slightest excuse for colored labor in
sugar manufacture any more than for work connected
with meat works or wool sheds. Barton’s tariff, so far


as sugar is concerned, is the most statesmanlike remedy
ever put forward to solve the vexed colored - labor
question.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Looting of New South
Wales.
The ghost of the old legend about the conspiracy
on the part of the other States to loot N.S.W.
reared its drunken head once more last week.
Said George Reid —the man who claims to have
brought the Commonwealth into existence :
Under this wonderful tariff the people of New South
Wales will pay 36 per cent, of the whole taxation.
Then Sydney Evening News promptly took
up the parable in its leading article of 10th
October:
The great debate in Melbourne upon Mr. Reid’s motion
censuring the Federal tariff has begun, and the two
leaders have already spoken. Mr. Reid made a telling
attack, or an attack which should have been telling, but
for the fact that New South Wales has been selected as
the victim of the tariff framers. Had it been thought
that New South Wales had enough influence to seriously
imperil the tariff, that tariff would not have been so
drawn as to make New South Wales pay 36 per cent, of
the total Australian taxation. The other States, before
they marched to the plunder of New South Wales, pro-
bably made certain, by a careful count of heads, that the
crib was a safe one to crack.
This is the sort of silly and unscrupulous rot
which imposes on the ignorant, and which is
evidently intended to impose on the ignorant.
It most distinctly implies that if N.S.W. pays
36 per cent, of the taxation it will be
grievously wronged in some way. The fact
that N.S.W. has 36 per cent, of the white
population, and therefore should pay 36 per
cent, of the taxation—that there is no looting,
and no cracking of any crib, and nobody march-
ing to the plunder of anybody else—all this
is carefully suppressed. So is the fact that
N.S.W., if it pays 36 per cent, of the taxa-
tion, will get back its corresponding proportion
of the surplus. If the public could possibly
believe that the Foreign-trade papers and the
Foreign-trade politicians of New South Wales
really represented the popular feeling of the
State, then New South Wales would be an object
of scorn, as the big, currish braggart of the Com-
monwealth. Its pot-house arrogance about the
wealth of the “Mother State ” as compared with its
allegedly bankrupt partners ; its pitiable small-
dog whine about the robbery proposed to be per-
petrated on New South Wales when it is asked to
supply its fair share of the Federal revenue
according to population—just as much per head as
its allegedly much poorer brethren ; its lamenta-
tion that the Federal Ministry (in which Victoria
has less representation in proportion to population
than any other State) is run by Victoria ; its con-
stant weeping about how its own big State has
been in some way struck or damaged or wronged
by its very small neighbor down South —all these
unmanly groans and wails are calculated to make
the community feel very sick. If the Foreign-
trade press would try to be a man instead of a
miserable injured Mother Gummidge a lot more
public respect would come its way.
+
The Slaughter of the Children.
The murder record in the British concentration
camps in South Africa, since information about
these horrois first began to be reluctantly supplied
by the Salisbury-Chamberlain Government:
July : 1412 deaths out of 91,940 white persons (mostly
women and children), or at the rate of about 184 per
annum out of every 1000.
August: 1878 deaths out of 107,425 prisoners, or at the
rate of about 210 per annum out of every 1000.
September: 2411 deaths out of 109,418 prisoners, or at
the rate of over 264 per annum out of every 1000.
This is the slaughter of women and children in-
discriminately. But, take the children separately,
for the two months during which separate in-
formation was supplied:
August: 510 deaths out of 22,036 children detained as
prisoners, or at the rate of 278 per annum out of
every 1000.
September: 1964 deaths out of 54,326 child prisoners,
or at the rate of 433 pe.- annum out of every 1000.
It was in July that the Salisbury Government
cheerfully admitted that its brutish Kitchener
had shut out the red-cross nurses from his awful
death-traps, and gave as a reason that things were
so satisfactory that nurses were quite superfluous.
Since then the mortality has gone up in a hideous
fashion. At the present rate the children of the
Boer nation will be practically all killed off inside
three years ! The death-rate among adults and
children is now 22 times that of New South Wales;
it breaks the worst known records of the cholera
or the Black Death ; the prisons of the Inquisition
weren’t worth mentioning by comparison; and even
Alva never managed to establish such a death-
rate during his rule in the Netherlands.
And this is in the cool season of the year ; what
things may be like when the glare of summer
shines down on these loathsome dens is beyond
imagining. Kitchener’s savage policy of de-
stroying private property wholesale, and penning
up women and children, whose homes and food
supplies have been burnt by his orders, to die
of disease, exposure, bad food, and neglect in his
concentration camps is a disgrace from which the
good name of Britain will not recover for cen-
turies. And yet even he finds apologists. Con-
sider this beautiful slab of servile degradation
from a Sydney morning paper ;
The foregoing figures indicate a tremendous infant
mortality. Roughly, they mean an average death-rate of
about 433 per 1000 per annum. “ But,” as a returned
soldier recently stated to an interviewer, “ you cannot
conduct a war on kid-glove principles. It is terrible to
see the suffering. Everybody I saw at the front did his
best to alleviate misery-i-but misery, gaunt and horrible,
is ever present all the same. Measles and fevers never
seem to leave the refugee camps.”
Think of that now ! “ Everybody did his best to
alleviate suffering.” And making war on women
and children, burning their homes, and shutting
them up under conditions bo awful that the
mortality is in a fair way to wipe out the whole
community inside four years is what the Jingo rag
calls alleviating suffering! What would it be like
if it wasn’t alleviated ?

The Proposed Nigger-Labor Upheaval.
The Barton Government’s sugar duties have
aroused a shriek of indignation in Queensland
nigger-labor circles. Probably anything except
unlimited nigger, accompanied, if possible, by an
excise duty on white-man-made sugar, in order to
suppress the objectionable European, would have
made the Brisbane black-labor crowd angry, but
in its present mood the rage of an Orange-and-
Green mob on the anniversary of Aughrim is a
holy calm by comparison. The tariff proposes, in
the first place, that there shall be a duty of £lO
per ton on sugar not made from cane. This is
intended to counterbalance the fact that much
beet sugar which comes here from the continent
of Europe is aided by a State bounty, and
cane sugar made here by white labor without
the aid of a bounty cannot fairly be called
on to compete against this. Incidentally
this duty gives the British empire—which
grows much cane sugar and practically no beet —
a leg up, a fact which should appeal to a
country which has just sent away about two dozen
farm-burning contingents. Cane sugar is to be
taxed £6 a ton. Also there is to be an excise of
£3 a ton on locally-grown sugar, with the proviso
that after Ist July, 1902, a rebate equal to £2 a
ton will be granted on all sugar made by white
labor only, reducing the ■ excise to £1; and,
after Ist January, 1907, a rebate equal to the
whole duty is to be allowed on the white product.
In other words, after next July white-man-made
sugar receives £5 per ton Protection and the
Kanaka and Chinese product only £3; after the
end of 1906 the white product receives £6 per ton
Protection and the black product still only £3.
The Barton Government believ es in protecting
our own nigger against the foreign nigger,
but it believes still more in protecting the
white man against all niggers. It is a
fair and honest device, and shows that the
Federal Government adheres firmly to its White
Australia policy. And Queensland in general,
which sent an almost solid White Australia dele-
gation to the Federal Parliament, will assuredly
stand by the Government—unless it has doubts
that the proceeding isn’t drastic enough. But up
in Queensland the Black Labor remnant (which
runs Brisbane and its daily papers) has thrown its
tall hat to the winds, and torn its frock-coat up
the back, and is talking civil war and red revolu-
tion, and has dreams of sending a Black Labor
army to burn Melbourne, and leave the Barton
Ministry a cinder among the ruins. Its language
is that of South Carolina before the Civil War,
combined with the curse of the gentleman who
held that Carthage should be destroyed. Even if
Queensland was solid for Black Labor it couldn’t
do a quarter of the things that the Kanaka Rem-
nant is talking about; but with Queensland voting
in a huge majority for a White Australia the situa-
tion is supremely ridiculous. This state of things
shows that the White Australia movement was
made none too soon. The United States black
labor problem led to a four-years’ war, and even
that left it unsettled. The Australian problem,
if it had been allowed to grow for an equal length
of time, might have done the same. In fact, but
for the Chinese Restriction Acts and educational
tests, which greatly hindered its growth, it might
have already almost reached the necessary
dimensions.

More Northern Territory Shindykit.
One John Brown, representing the North
Australian Exploration Syndicate (agents, J. S.
Botters— good old Butters— and Co., of Mel-
bourne), advertised recently in the Melbourne
papers that things were going on splendidly in
London with the formation of a large company to
“develop” the property. Then the advt. went
on to furnish particulars of the property to be
developed:
The area secured is, by comparison, equal to a strip of
country stretching from Melbourne to Echuca, varying
from 30 to 40 miles wide ; it reaches from the Gulf of Car-
pentaria to within 20 miles of the telegraph and the route
of the proposed overland railway from Adelaide to Port
Darwin, which has already been built from the southern
end at Adelaide to Oodnadatta, 688 miles, and from the
north at Port Darwin to Pine Creek, 145 J miles.
The total area is 6234 sq. miles, practically four
million acres. The Roper River, one of the
largest in Australia, runs through the whole length
of the property, and has many tributaries, including the
Chambers, the Strangways, the Hodgson, the Elsey, the
Childers, and others ; there are also the Wickham, Lim-
rnen, and Towns rivers further south running direct into
the Gulf. The Roper was surveyed in the Omeo steamer
in 1870 by Messrs. Pickham and M'Lachlan, officers of
the S.A. Govt., and in 1885 in the B. A. Govt, steamer,
Palmerston, commanded by Capt. Carrixoton (now
captain of the Coogee), who checked the previous sur-
veys, and added additional valuable information. This
survey must have cost over £lO,OOO. Copies of the charts
in the hands of the syndicate show that the river is navi-
gable for vessels of 1000 tons for 70 to 8 ) miles close up
to the Leichhardt Falls, and above the Falls by smaller
craft for an additional 40 miles, thus being the great
waterway to the interior of Northern Australia. The
syndicate has exclusive grazing and mineral rights over
the whole area, and all leases (save one) are for a period
of 42 years.
That seems to be the lot. The syndicate has only
got a 42 years’ lien on almost the- best river in
Australia, and practically all the good lands on
both sides of it. And this process of giving away
huge parchment leases, and holding syndicate
meetings in a Melbourne office, and floating com-
panies in London, is called “developing the
Territory.” Unfortunately, that great, neglected
but valuable asset can never be developed by
Butters or Secretary Brown putting advertise-
ments in the Melbourne papers. It wants a
Federally-owned transcontinental railway first of
all—a railway built out of revenue, not loans, so
that having no interest to pay, it can carry pro-
duce very cheaply. It wants a Federal land tax
on something like the S.A. plan—small estates
exempt, with a graduated increase on large ones
till the very largest are liable for about 3d. in the
£, with absentee tax added. It wants this tax
applied to leaseholds as well as freeholds. And
then the small white settler —the genuine agricul-
turist or grazing farmer—should get his land for
next to nothing to induce genuine settlement, so
that the country may yield a revenue, and the
railway may have enough business to make it
pay. With some such policy as that there will be
real prosperity and a real population in the
Territory, and the scalp of Butters’ shindykit
will wave from the highest tree-top. But it is no
earthly use taking oyer the Territory without a
fixed resolve to get rid of the large syndicates—by
resumption, or by taxing them out, but to get rid
of them somehow. It can only be made to pay by
promoting settlement, and the Butters’ clan has
most of the best land already locked up against
settlement.

The Chinese Egg.
By way of a reasonable test of the sincerity of the
Freetrade party, it would be interesting to have it
define its attitude towards that comparatively
recent invader, the Chinese egg. Though the
Chinaman may be objectionable on moral grounds,
the Chinese egg is as moral as any other egg, and
probably as healthy ; and it has to be objected to
for fiscal reasons or not at all. It is a compara-
tively recent institution in N.S. Wales—that
general dumping-ground of the refuse of all
nations. In 1896 the Chow, of China proper and
Hongkong, sent us 11,425 dozen eggs ; in 1897 he
sent 18,794 dozen ; in 1898, 39,102 dozen ; in 1899
he dropped to 31,471 dozen; but in 1900 the
heathen egg supply of New South Wales went up
to 74,365 dozen. In 1896, the Chinaman supplied
less than 3 per cent, of the Cornstalk egg imports ;
while in 1900 lie furnished over 12 per cent. The
reason is obvious enough. The declared value of
the Chinese egg is just about half that of other
imported eggs, and by extra cheapness the Mongol
is wiping the floor with his competitors. Even
after paying freight and all charges, his product
is declared to cost only about half as much landed
in Sydney as the local article costs. Of course
the retail buyer doesn’t get the Chinese egg
visibly cheaper than any other kind, but the
middle-man makes a large profit out of it ; there-
fore, while it is obtainable (and it is obtainable in
steadily increasing quantities) he isn’t likely to.
buy the local product. And the Sydney Tele-
graph is much interested in the local product.
On a Saturday its poultry advertisements often
run to two or three closely-printed columns.
It gets up hen-laying competitions, and
it has a hen-column, and it enjoys a
large circulation among fowl-breeders and fowl-
enthusiasts. There is every reason to believe that
a time will come, if the Freetrade system continues,
when the local fowl won’t be wanted any more by
reason of Asiatic competition and cheap freights,
to say nothing of the great improvement in chil-
ling arrangements and other preservative devices
which now enable the Buddhist fowl to deposit his
egg on the Cornstalk’s doorstep almost as fresh as
that of the purely local bird. In its own small
way, that local bird helps to support innumerable
households, and it can’t be superseded by its
Chinese rival without knocking another prop from
under the local farmer. And the great increase
of Chinese supplies, which threatens to under-
mine an innocent and happy fowl, hasn’t even the
merit of reducing the price to the consumer. Yet
every genuine Freetrader must needs barrack
for the continued free admission of the Chinese
egg, even though its advent doesn’t reduce
prices, and even if it wrecks a valuable local
industry, and knocks out the Telegraph’s
columns of hen advertisements. Will the Free-
trade party, and especially the Freetrade Laborists
who don’t profit by the import business and have
only the satisfaction of seeing another local
industry damaged and paying the same price as
before, make a stand for the free Chow egg ? And
if it won’t, what is the use of having a Freetrade
party? The “Freetrade” Sydney Evening
News actually claims credit for its efforts towards
suppressing the cheap Chinese egg ! Yet it would
be a most excellent thing if the working man
could get eggs at 3d. a dozen to feed his children
on ; it is against the Freetrade policy to tax the
food of the masses. Why do we not find the
Freetrade press barracking for the cheap Chow
egg?

The Secret of Britain’s Greatness.
Britain built up its shipping-trade by its Naviga-
tion laws. For nearly 200 years—the period
during which Britain grew from being a country
which carried on its trade in Dutch ships, to being
the owner of one-third the world’s mercantile
marine—no foreign ship was allowed to trade to
any British colony without special license, no
foreign ship was allowed to bring any goods to a
British port except the goods of its own country ;
and, at one time, no vessel was allowed to trade
to British ports unless the master and the
majority of the crew were British. So Britain
grew till she became the world’s greatest sea-
carrier, and had a grand time. It was not till
well on in Queen Victoria's reign that Britain,
having extinguished her old rivals, abolished the
last remnants of the Navigation Laws. Even
then they were only abolished out of sheer neces-
sity. The last of them to go, in 1854, was the law
which prohibited foreign vessels engaging in the
British coastal trade. At that time Britain had
gone into the coastal trade of so many other coun-
tries that, instead of being afraid of competition
along her own shores, she had become afraid lest
other States should follow her example and shut
her out from theirs. So, by way of establishing
a precedent which would enable her to insist on
retaining free access to the waters of foreign
States she threw her own waters open to all
comers —with a satisfactory conviction that she
had got so solid a hold on the business that nobody
could come to any extent worth mentioning.
King O’Malley’s proposal that Australia, being
in the same situation as Britain when she created
her Navigation Laws, should to some extent follow
Britain’s example should therefore commend itself
to all good loyalists. If Britain’s example isn’t
good enough for us to follow, then the Duke’s visit
was wasted, and all those signed photos might as
well have gone into the ash-bin.

The Patient and the Students.
A correspondent writes to The Bulletin on a
much-vexed question:
When a woman goes to Prince Alfred Hospital (Sydney)
to be treated as an out-door patient for diseases peculiar
to her sex, she’s taken into a room or ward, where a
number of young gentlemen stand around in white coats.
She is placed on a lounge, and surrounded by a screen.
She is covered with a sheet, and the surgeon makes
the necessary examination. When it is concluded
he covers her face with the sheet, and makes some
soothing remark about lying still and being all right,
while as many as four and five students are brought in
one after another to be taught how to make these sort of
examinations. They are made solely for the benefit and
experience of the students, and have anything but a good
effect on the patient, whose permission has never been
asked, and who was quite unaware that she was expected
to submit to the treatment.
The indoor patient is no better off. The outdoor
patient is surrounded by a screen always, and there are
never more than two present at a time ; while the indoor
patient does not always have even the small mercy of a
screen. Each specialist has about 10 or 12 students with
him, and in many cases the presence of a screen would
interfere with their view of the proceedings, so the
feelings of the patient are sacrificed to the interests of the
student. The consequence is that some poor women
who are afflicted with both modesty and poverty
are driven away. They never get over the in-
dignity of that first unauthorised examination, and
they never go back. Then some night they are taken
in a cab to Sydney or St. Vincent’s Hospital incurable.
“ You should have come six months ago ; we can do
nothing for you now! ” says the medico, hopelessly.
It is claimed that if patients didn’t like the treatment
they could object, and no pressure would be put on them
to submit, and they would not be deprived of the use of
the hospital for so doing. But, on the other hand, it is
strongly asserted that patients who refuse to allow
students to practise on them are refused further treat-
ment. And it is generally admitted that neither the
hospital authorities nor the specialists ever inform
patients that they are free to object, nor is the fact
posted up on the walls or the gates. The authorities
claim that the hospital is attached to the University, and
was originally intended as much for a training-ground for
medical students as for the benefit of the sick poor, and
it is therefore taken for granted that all who come there
are willing to be of service to the students. The practice,
it is maintained, is absolutely necessary in the interests of
the public, medical science, and the students. It
would be of no value to students to hire healthy
women for examination : they have to see the disease and
learn to recognise it at sight. There might be plenty of
diseased women available for remuneration, but the sup-
ply would be uncertain, and the effect of having to rely
upon such a supply disastrous to the training of the
students, which already spreads over too much time.
Now I am not convinced by this reasoning. My ob-
jection to the present system of taking things for granted
is that by it many honest and sensitive women are trapped
into suffering what is to them a life-long humiliation. It
is a disgrace to Sydney’s most splendid charitable insti-
tution that its patients are subjected to such cruel indig-
nities. When patients are admitted it should be clearly
explained to them that in return for free, or exceedingly
cheap, treatment they are required to assist in the trainfng
of the students by serving as subjects ; and then there
would be no misunderstanding, and no disgrace to the
hospital.
Acting in the interests of the rotten-butter makers
and vendors, Vic. Leg. Council rejected the Ex-
ported Products Bill. Out of the 48 members, 28
only were present when this particular outrage
was committed. The Bill had been very carefully
considered and greatly improved in the Assembly.
In the form in which it reached the Council it
would have prevented any but wholesome meat
and rabbits being shipped to England ; it would
have stopped the exportation of cart-grease as
butter, and made it an offence for anyone to mark
mixed or milled butter as factory-butter. Most
of the mixed butter is made in the back streets
of Melbourne. It consists of small inferior
parcels of butter sent from all parts of the coun-
try, and butter made from cream sent to Melbourne
by farmers who use the hand-separators. This
cream frequently reaches the city in a stinking
condition, and in a state of fermentation.
Nicholls, M.L.A., speaking on the subject in the
Assembly, declared that he had seen cream landed
in Melbourne full of maggots half-an-inch long.
Butter manufactured from this stuff is doctored
with other butter, put into new, clean cases,
branded with the name of some fictitious factory,
and shipped to London where it does incalculable
harm to the Australia butter-trade. The Exported
Products Bill would have forced the manufacturers
of this horror to label it for what it was. As
usual, not a single reason was advanced in the
Council for throwing out the measure. One mem-
ber after another simply rose up and remarked
literally, or in effect, “This bill is unnecessary.
No one has asked for it.” Leg. Councillors in
Victoria never get beyond those two assertions in
throwing out useful measures.

Some recent controversial letters in the Argus
threw a needed light on the workings of Mel-
bourne Tramways Act, which is one of the least
printed and most easily misunderstood of the Vic.
Statutes. From this correspondence it appears
that sec. 42 of the Act lately referred to in The
Bulletin as a proviso which had “ never been re-
pealed or modified in any way,” became a super-
fluously misleading section when the Melbourne
municipalities elected to build their own lines and
lease them to the co. in accordance with sec. 57.
Secs. 42 and 57 were alternatives, and the latter
was adopted. The corporations seem to have
made the best of the Government's bargain
with the T.C. monopoly when they decided to
form a trust and build their own tramway lines.
It is comforting to know_ that the Tramway co.,
on inquiry, turns out to 'be an honest monopoly
so far as concerns section 42 of its precious Act,
but there is still more comfort in the discovery
that recent scheming for an extension of the
monopoly’s lease of life looks certain to be
defeated. In 1915, or thereabouts, the Melb.
Tramways will become public property if the
public keeps an eye on the schemers meanwhile.

Just as a counterblast to the yells that are being
wired all over the Commonwealth that Queens-
land is in favor of Black Labor, Higgs, the
white-labor Radical senator, topped the poll with
29,452 votes, whereas Cowley, the straight-out
Kanaka candidate, could only poll into tenth
place with 18,265 votes. Not a single declared
Kanaka advocate was elected to the Senate!
Fisher, who represents Wide Bay district, which
includes the great Southern sugar district, beat
Annear, the Kanaka candidate, by 4844 to 3955.
White Australian Bamford, who represents the
Black North, beat Brown, the Kanaka candi-
date, by 3350 to 3133 ; M‘Donald beat the quasi -
Kanaka man in Flinders by 3936 to 2325 ; Page
beat another quasi-black advocate by 2996 to 2609
in Maranoa; and Wilkinson, on the white
ticket, beat the Kanakaphile in Moreton by 2588
to 2086. Yet we are asked to believe
that Queensland wants black - labor. If any
further evidence is needed as to what Queens-
land really voted for, the aggregate vote
polled for straight-out white Australians in the
divisional elections was 32,739, while the more or
less black candidates polled in all only 26,784.
This is the voice of Queensland per medium of
what amounted to a White Australia referendum,
not the voice of the Ministry, the Courier, or the
Q.N. Bank, which latter has sugar securities to
boom or claim compensation for from White
Australia.

Ti-ie latest fake in the way of a protest against the
Federal tariff :
Mr. Kirwan (W.A.) has received a telegram from Kal-
goorlie, from the representatives of capitalists with 80
millions invested in Australian mines, which reads as
follows : “ Consensus of opinion here is that the tariff
will paralyse the mining industry. One immediate effect
has been the stoppage of the expenditure of three-quarters
of a million contemplated by three Kalgoorlie mines.”
This protest starts with a flagrant lie, for there
are not in all Westralia 80 millions of capital
invested in mines. There may be companies with
scrip that professes to represent something like
that amount, but that is quite a different thing.
And the pretence that the Federal tariff inter-
feres with the mining industry is the saddest kind
of an invention. Westralia gets most of its
mining machinery from S.A., Victoria, and
N.S.W. It has hitherto paid duty on all this,
and the Commonwealth tariff makes it all free—■
unless Westralia’s State Parliament exercises the
povver unfortunately granted to it to levy border
duties for five years. The Commonwealth, at all
events, does its share to help the Westralian
mine-owners. It has also made the great
bulk of Westralia’s imported food supplies,
which mostly come from the eastern States, free—
unless the W.A. State Legislature exercises its
temporary power to tax these supplies. Of course,
it is always possible to get some one to send
a telegram saying that a whole community is
shocked and ruined—it can be done for the price
of the telegram and a pint of beer—but the
message that Kirwan received is a very un-
convincing effort in this line.

To-day’s English sentiment in regard to the Boers
merely repeats yesterday’s English sentiment with
regard to the Yankees. Witness Dr. Johnson as
reported by Boswell in April, 1778 :
From this pleasing subject he (Johnson) made a sudden
transition to one upon which he was a violent aggressor ;
for he said, “ I am willing to love all mankind, except an
American;” and, his inflammable corruption bursting
into horrid fire, he breathed out threatenings and
slaughter, calling them “ Rascals—robbers—pirates,”
and exclaiming he’d “ burn and destroy them.”
Miss Seward, looking at him with mild but
steady astonishment, said: “ Sir, this is an instance
that we are always most violent against those whom we
have injured.” He was irritated still more by this delicate
and keen reproach, and roared out another tremendous
volley, which one might fancy could be heard across the
Atlantic. During this tempest I (Boswell) sat in great
uneasiness, lamenting his heat of temper ; till by degrees
I diverted his attention to other topics.
Yet these Americans, like the Boers, were only
fighting for independence. A hundred years
hence the English will admit the Boers were
right, just as to-day they admit the Yankees were
right. In the meantime—fire and slaugV/v.
orphans and burnt homesteads.

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ The Charity Ball.”
Her Majesty’s “ The Casino Girl.”
Bijou “ Tom, Dick and Harry.”

Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
New Building (off
