Town Hall Alice Hollander Concerts.
Exhibition Building... The World’s Fair.
The new American opera company gathered
a great audience at Sydney Criterion on its.
opening night. At the same time it was not a
very enthusiastic audience —especially towards
the end. “Said Pasha” is not an opera in the
ordinary sense of the term. It is a musical
comedy, or rather, a musical variety show, in
which the funny man makes his hair stand up in
terror, by way of the same time-honored joke that
Hiscocks’ blackened Federal Minstrels used to
perpetrate 15 years ago. There is no plot in it,
and no continuity. A series of detached jokes,
some of them none too new, are held together by
a thin thread of music, without any definite con-
sequences. There are the usual two knockabout
adventurers, of whom one violently bosses the
other, after the manner of the Insect and his
mate; but they do nothing remarkable. The
music is respectable, but very few of the items
are likely to clamour after a haunting fashion in
the public’s ear. The loudest laugh arises
after a song about a Sydney tram-conductor,
but it isn’t a durable song, or one which
will stand very much repetition. The thin
little production ends very early; it is short as
well as thin, and when it has departed it is almost
impossible to remember what it was about, for it
really wasn’t about anything. For a piece to be
about nothing is a drawback; for a piece to be about
nothing in no particular kind of way is a still greater
drawback. “ Said Pasha ” calls up memories of
“Evangeline” and “ Morocco Bound,” and other
faint and disastrous shadows of the past. It
hasn’t anything like the resources of the “ Trip
to Chinatown ” or the “ Belle of New York ’’—not
that their resources were very remarkable either.
Yet, to speak severely of the frail little thing is
very like firing a howitzer at a moth, and a
good many remarks that might be made are con-
sequently hung up.
* * * *
The star of the company, Miss Josephine
Stanton, creates a favorable impression, and
deserves to be surrounded by a better and brighter
opera. She is a comely and well-shaped lady,
with a voice that is strong rather than sweet, and
either her singing capacity is only moderate or her
chances of showing what she can do are limited.
But, so far as her opportunities go, she seems to
be a bright and capable actress, and there is a
pleasing vivacity about her, and her limbs cover a
multitude of circumstances. George Kunkel, the
low comedian of the programme, and Charles Van
Dyne, the lower comedian, brighten the proceed-
ings a good deal by doing nothing new, but
doing it as well as anything so old could
reasonably expect to be done. None of
the other voices or personalities stand out
very strongly, and the male part of the chorus
requires strengthening; but the orchestra does its
duty manfully. The programme supplies the
cheering intelligence that “Dorcas” is in active
preparation—not that the writer ever heard of
“Dorcas” before.
* * * *
All in chain armour (or a good imitation of
it) and raised vizor, “II Trovatore” took the
stage at Her Majesty’s on Saturday night.
Verdi’s music encompasses a 15th century story by
a Spanish dramatist. None knew better than the
Spaniards of bygone centuries the arts of love and
jealousy, revenge and cruelty. A gipsy woman is
burnt alive for having bewitched the infant son of
the Count di Luna. Her daughter, Azucena,
dedicates her life to revenge, and steals tile
Count’s infant son, whom she brings up as her
own bantling under the name .of Mannco. When
grown he becomes a conqueror in war and love,
and is preferred by Leonora, a lady of quality, to
the reigning Count di Luna, who is Mannco s
elder brother. After that, jealousy and revenge
carry the day, and also the castle in
which Leonora has taken refuge rather than
marry the Count. He storms the pre-
mises, captures the lovers, and puts the darbies
on the gipsy Azucena, who evidently loves her
chain-armoured stolen property. Leonora pro-
mises to marry the Count if he will spare Mannco.
She hurries to the prison to liberate her lover, but
the Troubadour won’t have life without her love,
and, to avoid keeping her promise to the Count,
she swallows poison concealed in a ring. By now,
the Count is all gall, but regains some cheerfulness
when he beheads Manrico. Then the gipsy, for
whom the faggots are piled, tells the hidalgo whom
he has murdered, and drops dead on the straw of
her prison pallet, exclaiming that her mother is
avenged. *
Signora Benedetto’s clear, sweet soprano
galvanised the dead-and-gone Leonora (she did
live in many times and lands), and her acting had
the sinuous, snake-like charm with which this un-
emotional lady has invested her roles. She was
encored several times, but one of the encores was
almost a tumult. In the convent-scene the
Signora’s long, black velvet gown pulled a
lighted torch from its moorings as she
ran off the stage, and the blazing thing
fell right on her train. She actually drew it as
far as the exit, and only knew it was there when
turning round to bow. If the dress had been
inflammable there would have been a real fire
horror, and so the audience thought when it put
a lot of muscle into its encore. Signora Marco-
mini, as the gipsy Azucena, looked like a hand-
some Maori. The part gives her the best show
she has yet had. Her voice has the range of a
soprano, with an advantage over the latter as to
lower register, and she acted with such feeling as
to leave the impression that a big, big heart beat
under her bizarre Romany necklace and red
bodice. In the prison-scene the audience loved
her for her rendering of the ever adorable “ Home
our Mountains.” Commendatore Larizza, as the
Troubadour, looks a fine figure of a man, fit to
break a lance—the other fellow’s lance —in the
tourney, or to twang a guitar under a damsel’s
window. True, he would put a terrible robustness
into his twanging, and his entreaties that she would
ope her casement and drop him a glance might
sound like strong-lunged commands to get out of
the house before the fire reached the third storey.
In the Miserere Scena, the unseen tenor sang the
plaintive “Ah che la Morte” tenderly, as one
who puts his sorrow into his song. Signor Bozzoli,
as the Count di Luna, played with his usual
frowning earnestness, and made “a profound sen-
sation ” (as Reuter says) with his rendering of II
Balen. By now all the amateur baritones in all
the suburbs are pegging away at the “ Tempest of
the Heart.” Signorina Marchi looked nice ; and
Signor Tondini, as Ferrando, did as much as any
personable, light basso could do with his aide-de-
camp role.
* • * •
As already mentioned, Wilson Barrett
will wear the royal crown of Engle-land at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday in his capacity as Alfred
“The Christian King.” Mr. Barrett’s pedantic,
and, presumably, ancient way of spelling Eng-
land is already making this paper feel half-in-
clined to sympathise with Alfred’s Danish enemy
—not that sympathy at the distance of all those
centuries would have much effect on that hairy
intruder.
* * * *
Robert Brough and co. were still produc-
ing the “Magistrate” at Sydney Royal when
this issue went to press, but the days of that
burdened administrator of justice were numbered.
Unless something unexpected happened to pro-
long his stay, he is now only the recollection of a
dead circumstance and the memory of a departed
voice that used to fine the drunk ss. The
“ Magistrate ” was about as good a work as could
have been selected with which to close the season.
It is a happy reminiscence of the days before the
slimy and bottomless octopus of the problem play
overtook Pinero and began to swallow him up.
The problem play is a weariness because
it never solves the problem, and never even
states distinctly what the problem is. It is
a depressed thing wandering in a circle, like
a funeral that has got bushed owing to the driver
being intoxicated, or out of his mind. To close
the season with anything of that kind would have
left Sydney thinking of its vanished Brough as a
depressing circumstance that went away. Now it
remembers him as a person who made it shriek
with merriment in the last moments of its
acquaintance with him, and it will hope for his
early return to make it shriek some more.
* * * *
At Sydney Palace they have progressed
from politeness to piety. A feature of Saturday
night’s performance was sacred music by a
“Celestial Choir” of boys, with Miss Frances
Gwynne as soloist. The singing was dainty, and
the item was well received. A rumor that next
week Scripture readings, with a ballet of surpliced
clergymen, will form part of the programme is
officially denied at the offertory office. In addi-
tion to the choir, there are several changes of pro-
gramme : the Musical Burglar, for instance, has
one more rent in his coat.
* * * *
The projected hanging of a small, short-
skirted child has pleased the public taste so much
that it has been decided to keep “The London
Arab ” on at Sydney Lyceum a few nights longer.
After that the execution will be off, and this
medley, which consists of 10 melodramas crushed
together, will give place to “The 10.30 Down
Express.” Latter sounds like the pasteboard
tram once more sauntering in on a railway-line
made by black chalk-marks.
» * * *
Sydney Empire staged “The Waif of
London ” as a dramatic first part on Saturday, and
Miss Dora Mostyn proved to be a satisfactory
waif. Mr. Denis Carney contributes largely to
the fun of the second or variety part of the pro-
gramme.
* * * *
At Sydney Tivoli this week vocalist James
Bain reappears, and a boy comedian, Claud
Sullivan, supplies a ditty concerning a plumber,
which is received nightly with a fair amount of ap-
preciation. The Bulletin, however, is not greatly
struck with the child phenomenon at any time.
Dora Taylor possesses a small, light voice and a
small, light figure, which observation is meant
less as a reflection on her vocal powers than as a
compliment to her toe-and-heel work. The Craggs
(there are eight of them, though only six are de-
picted in the photo, on the programme) continue
worthy of the large letters in which they are billed.
* * * *
The temporary Circus-building erected for
Fitzgerald Bros, on the South Melbourne side of
St. Kilda Road, close to Princes’ Bridge, is the
right thing at the right place. As soon as the
octagonal “ Olympia,” or whatever they call it,
was properly fixed up, everybody remarked that a
Eermanent building of similar character ought to
ave been part of the municipal plan for im-
proving the approach to the Bridge. Under
present arrangements, Tom Fitzgerald is a public
benefactor, to a greater extent, though at less cost,
than J. G. Williamson was when he converted a
lot of shabby little shops into a handsome frontage
for his theatre in Exhibition-street. Fitz., having
hired a ragged, dirty tract of land from
Melb. City Council, proceeded to level it and
adorn it with attractive approaches to the show.
The Circusian Brethren take the financial risk and
the neighborhood gets a benefit anyhow, seeing
that the bit of country can never resume its
original hideousness. The picks and shovels of
civilisation have done valuable surface work, apart
from the removal of rubbish heaps and the decent
burial of numerous skeletons. Fitzgeralds hope
to have their tents pitched around their iron
Olympia for many weeks to come. In the new
building the performance is brighter and
altogether more attractive than it looked when
under the canvas. The roof is built to show off
gymnasts to better advantage, and the trapeze
arrangements are more slick. In the centre of
the circus a tank excavation, 30ft. x 15ft., and some
16ft. deep has been made for the accommodation
of certain diving ’osses—but the steeds that
plunge into the still waters are another story,
likewise the “Siberia” show whereof brother
Tom has been talking in a deep, prophetic tone
since he returned from Europe a year agone.
Brother Tom, by the way, has ceased to be
mistaken for Father Tom now that his moustache
is breaking out again.
• • • •
The only new matter of remark in the
Melb. show world last Saturday was Fitzgerald’s
Circus. The wisdom of dedicating a vast iron
temple to the worship of gee-gee proved itself
some time before 8 o’clock, when the interior was
packed, and the name of the people turned away
from the pay-boxes was Legion. Any pitch
for the Fitzes’ shows has always been a good pitch,
but the location of this sudden building is
evidently the best they have ever struck. More-
over, the general get-up of the establishment is
gay and festive beyond one’s wildest dreams of
hippodrome, and six flunkeys of the girl-persua-
sion are ranged in silent splendor at the entrance
to the arena. The circus is mostly the same series
of items as before, improved by long practice and
new costumes. The lady juggler, who suffered
from influenza the first time she came to Melbourne,
is a more expert vision than of yore. Also, Adelina
Antonio seems to have added plumpness to her
previous proficiency. On Saturday night a
particular success was scored by the swinging
Dunbars. Another extra-sized burst of applause
fell to six horses from Hungary, and in the whole
collection of 21 turns the weak spots were not
visible to a nak ed eye.
** * *
Three Melbourne theatres will hang out a
new programme next Saturday night. At Her
Majesty’s there was a highly pardonable decline
of rush for “The Casino Girl” when the true
character of that glittering frivol came to be
noised abroad. A “musical comedy ” which has
no narrative worth mentioning, and no attractive
melodies to speak of, is likely to catch cold in
Melbourne, more especially when the floating
population of visitors have sampled it elsewhere.
On Saturday (26th) “The Gondoliers” will be
staged with an almost quite new cast. Even an
old cast would be welcome in “ The Gondoliers.”
** # m
At the Princess’s, where “The Charity
Ball ” has been rolling heavily along like the stone
that gathers no moss, a notable change of manage-
ment will date from Saturday next (26th). The
Broughs are coming, and to say that Melbourne
will feel glad to see its Broughs is to understate
the case. A long pent-up enthusiasm awaits
them. “Lady Huntworth’s Experiment” will
introduce the latest edition of the Brough co. to
notice at the Princess’s, and the season will last
seven weeks.
** * *
Wilson Barrett’s season at Adelaide
Royal has been got through with only four plays,
except for a single night of “Hamlet”; and
Adelaide, not too well pleased at seeing no new
pieces but “Man and his Makers,” has given a
fair but not extravagant support. Besides which,
the comic opera co. had taken away most of the
small city’s money. The Tivoli, left under the
charge of Treasurer Larkin while Manager
Marcus visits the U.S., does steady business with
a good all-round but not star: y company.
Certain writs are coming out amongst
society in Brisbane; and an expectant public
holds its breath.
The latest attempt to steal Adelaide’s park-
lands comes from the City Council itself, which
wants Parliament to let it enclose 70 acres of
the people’s breathing spaces for the purpose of
establishing public abattoirs. The other day it
asked the Legislature to allow it to lease an
area of park lands to a kerosene co. for the erec-
tion of hideous stores. At present the Corpora-
tion uses much of the park area for rubbish-tips.
It isn’t only little girls who get hallucina-
tions. A Melbournite of about 30 years, who lives
with his parents, startled ma one recent morning
with a thrilling yarn about his imaginary gallant
exploits in defence of the household against
a batch of burglars during the night. Ma, much
impressed, went to the long-suffering police, and
two officers came and investigated the “ facts ”
which were soon confessed by the soi-disant hero
to be romance. Male hysteria is, however, rare,
and it hasn’t the nasty vindictiveness of the
female outrage story.
Last Tuesday the papers broke out with a
wild, weird tale of the librarian of an Adelaide
suburban institute who was seized and gagged by
two burglars, and then hung up by the hands and
fried with a door-key made red-hot in the gas-jet,
till he revealed the whereabouts of £lO5, which
he had withdrawn from the savings-bank, in view
of his impending marriage. At time of going
to press full details had not crawled over from
Adelaide, and it is impossible to say as yet what
evidences there were, in the shape of the foot-
marks of the hot door-key on the librarian’s body,
to support his statement. But it is briefly stated
that the police are investigating the case, which
possibly means that they are inquiring if-any
returned contingenters have been seen there-
abouts.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Owing to the handing over of many depart-
ments to the Federal Government, the revenue of all the
States shows a decrease as compared with last year, and
the expenditure should certainly show one also. It is
difficult to form a clear idea of the financial situation
except as regards those States which publish expendi-
ture as well as revenue accounts. They stand as follows :
New South Wales.
Quarter to 30th Quarter to 30th
September, 1900. September, 1901. Decrease.
Revenue £2,263,791 £2,183,815 £79,976
Expenditure 2,561,626 2,429,487 132,139
Deficit £297,835 £245,672 £52,163
The decrease is wholly caused by the transference of
departments to the Federal Government; but for that
there would be an increase of £221,564. And as the
Commonwealth has relieved the State of an almost equal
expenditure, it had really about £220,000 more cash to
spend. Yet it has already acquired a large deficit, though
not so large a one as at this period of last year. And it
has spent £713,529 of loan money for the quarter, which
is £154,139 more than for the corresponding quarter of
1900. In other words, revenue has diminished by £79,976
and expenditure has increased by £22,000, but the
lamentable position has been disguised by charging more
of the expenditure to loans.
Queensland.
Quarter to 30th Quarter to 30th
September, 1900. September, 1901. Decrease.
Revenue £1,210,297 £1,018,024 £192,273
Expenditure 794,272 643,179 151,093
Surplus £416,025 £374,845 £41,180
Queensland charges the interest on its public debt half-
yearly, so it accumulates one surplus which disappears on
31st December and another which vanishes on 30th June.
The situation is that last year closed with a deficit of
£528,188 ; that things are already £41,180 worse than
they were at the corresponding date of 1900, so, unless
prospects change very suddenly, this year will be even worse
than last ; and that the abolition of border duties makes
the revenue outlook for the remaining nine months of
the year even poorer than it was for the very bad
quarter just ended. And, whereas Queensland’s loan
expenditure last year was the largest since 1891, this year
promises to be worse ; the loan-money spent during the
first three months of the new financial year being
£309,135—£34,950 more than for the corresponding quar-
ter of 1900.
Westralia still lumps the State and Common-
wealth figures together, with these results:
Westralia.
Quarter to 30th Quarter to 30th
September, 1900. September, 1901.
Revenue .. ..£721,758 .. £816,813
Expenditure .. 730,934 ... 760,254
Deficit .. .. £9,176
Surplus .. .. .. £56,559
The loan expenditure is not stated, but for the financial
year just closed Westralia had the biggest loan expendi-
ture on its record, barring that of 1896 and 1897. It got
through £1,495,292 of borrowed money against £878,329
for the year ended June, 1900. This meant £8 6s. per in-
habitant of loan money, expended in one year—even the
maddest of the other States (Queensland) only spent
£2 Bs. Bd. per head while Victoria only spent 15s. Bd. per
head. Westralia’s loan expenditure alone last year was
£1 per inhabitant more than Victoria spent out of re-
venue and borrowings combined ! It is questionable if
there ever was anywhere quite such a financial madness
as that of Westraiia. It looks like a world’s record.
The Tasmanian slump continues. For Septem-
ber the State and Commonwealth revenue collected in
the island was £10,886 less than the State revenue for
September, 1900, and so far as the financial year has gone
(nine months) the revenue has dropped £62,579. The
State and Commonwealth collections in Victoria are
£186,842 more than for the corresponding period of last
year. Neither of these States supplies any expenditure
returns. As regards South Australia, the branches of
revenue which are left under State control yielded
£363,177 for the quarter just ended, as compared with
£386,881 for the corresponding quarter of 1900—a fall of
£23,704. S.A. had a deficit of £22,365 last financial year,
and it begins the new 7 .year badly enough.

The Royal Bank of Australia (Melbourne) shows
a further material improvement for the half-year to 30th
September, 1901. Reckoning from the time when it
wrote off its old reserves and cleaned up its boom losses,
results have been as follows :
Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
March, 1897 ..£3398 3 per cent. = £2250 £1,148
September, 1897 3625 3 „ „ 2250 2,523
March, 1898 .. 3865 3 „ „ 2250 4,138
September, 1898 4088 3 „ „ 2250 5,976
March, 1899 .. 4315 3 „ „ 2250 8,041
September, 1899 4624 3 „ „ 2250 10,415
March, 1900 .. 5843 4 „ „ 3000 13,258
September, 1900 5863 4 „ ~ 3000 16,121
March, 1901 .. 6488 5 „ „ 3750 18,859
September, 1901 6700 5 „ „ 3750 21,809
The increase in profits has been continuous, and they now
equal £7 18s. 9d. per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds as at the commencement of the half-year.
The Royal, though only a small concern with £150,000
paid-up capital and £171,809 of total shareholders’ funds,
is a very solid institution, with a growing business.
Deposits have increased by about £30,000 since last
March. The holding of State securities has also in-
creased by £30,000, and now stands at £60,000. The
total reserve of cash and liquid securities is £241,300-
equal to nearly 33 percent, of all direct and contingent
liabilities to the public. The Royal of Melbourne shows
the highest rate of earnings upon its funds of all the Vic-
torian banks, and the highest rate of all the banks be-
longing to the Australian mainland, the Bank of Adelaide
alone excepted. If it adheres to its present careful policy,
and keeps its dividends low until the losses of the boom
time have been fully repaired, it should have a large
future before it.
» * * *
The commercial class which desires to remove
obstructions in the way of commerce should put its
shoulder to the wheel in the matter of federating the
railways and getting a uniform guage, uniform
management, and uniform rates. The latter
are certainly wanted very badly indeed. As a
casual example, the N.S.W. Parliamentary Committee,
which recently considered the question of a State railway
from Oockhurn to Broken Hill, went into the question of
freight charges on four different railway systems—those
of Queensland, N.S. Wales, Victoria, and South Australia.
It dealt with thirty kinds of goods, covering among them,
the bulk of the railway trade, with these results :
(1) The highest rate on 18 articles was that of Queens-
land ; N.S. Wales had the highest rate on five articles;
on one Queensland and N.S. Wales were level; S.A.
had the highest rate on three articles and Victoria on
three.

* * * *
A very successful trial of the Gardner Electric
Drill took place at the works of the Austral Otis Com-
pany, South Melbourne, a few days ago. The drill,
although only temporarily Axed, bored 8J inches in three
minutes through hard basalt. A number of prominent
mining men witnessed the test, and expressed themselves
as greatly pleased with the machine. The Austral Otis
Company have been appointed Sole Agents in Australasia
for the Gardner Electric Drill.*

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
Anes and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, BeaconsAeld, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Varfners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before Anally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

(2) South Australia charged the lowest rate in 18
cases; Victoria in eight; N.S.W. in three ; Queensland
in one. , . ,
(3) The rates vary so widely that if one ton of each
variety of goods (30 tons in all) was sent 35 miles by rail
in each State the cost would be £27.25. sd. in Queens-
land ; £2l 6s. 4d. in N.S.W ; £lB 9s. Bd. in Victoria ; and
onlv £16175. 2d in South Australia.
(4) Some of the discrepancies are enormous. Jam and
preserves, rate for carriage of one ton, 35 miles : Queens-
land, £1 9s. 2d.; N.S.W., £1 Is. 9d. ; Victoria, 16s ; S.A.,
Bs. 4d, Sugar: N.S.W., 16s. 4d. ; Queensland, 15s. sd. ;
Victoria, 12s. Bd. ; S.A., ss. sd.
These discrepancies appear to go on no fixed rule.
Queensland and S.A. have the smallest population per
mile of railway, therefore the poorest chances in the way
of traffic, and they should, consequently, have to charge
the highest rates to make ends meet. Yet, while one of
these thinly-peopled States charges the highest rate, the
other charges the lowest. Also Queensland, which charges
the highest rate, has a far larger goods traffic than S.A.,
which charges the lowest. Another point is that the
latest available returns (June, 1900) show that, of the four
States, S.A., which charges the lowest rates, has the best-
paying railway system; and Queensland, which charges
the highest, has the worst-paying system. The Federal
Government might find these points worth considering.

five years. The Brisbane Permanent Building and Bank-
ing 00. does business on a large scale. The shareholders’
funds total £131,593, and it owes £148,606 to depositors.
The reserve of cash and Government securities
is £16,754, or including £10,558 of fixed deposits
the total liquid securities (if the fixed deposits are liquid
and not reconstructed for a long term in the Q.N. Bank)
amount to £27,312. The rest of the assets are property
and mortgages. An institution doing business on this
basis is always in danger in a time of panic. -The Brisbane
P. B. and B. 00. has already experienced this ; in 1891
it had £287,945 of deposits—almost all lent out on mort-
gage—and, when the panic came, its deposits steadily
dropped for seven years, till in 1898 they were down to
£137,447. As a matter of course, it had to reconstruct,
though it came through the trouble better than most in-
stitutions. The co.’s prospects appear reasonably good
on paper, but Queensland at present is in a precarious
state, with drought, tick, Governmental mismanagement,
the Q. N. Bank, and the biggest debt per head on this
earth all on its shoulders, and it is difficult to say what
may happen. ■
« • * *
A. Shadier, Limited (Sydney), the co. formed
to take over the big bakery business formerly run by
the well-known person of that name, publishes its first
annual and most lamentable balance-sheet. It took over
a going concern which was supposed to be highly profit-
able, yet its first year’s gross profit was only £3026, against
expenses £4192, leaving a loss of £1166. And this is
without paying anything off the formation expenses, or
making any allowance for bad debts, depreciation of
plant and buildings, or writing anything off goodwill, or
making any allowance for deterioration in the value of
leaseholds, except an amount off one lease, which
sum the auditor declares to be insufficient. If goodwill
and formation expenses were written off, the loss would
be £5139. Then the directors appear to admit that £lOOO
requires to be provided for bad debts (it is a truly
lamentable thing for one-sixth of the book debts to be
found bad at the end of the first year), and £1683 allowed
for depreciation. These items, added to the ones already
specified, would leave the co. with a loss of £7822 at the
end of its first year, which absorbs more than half the
original paid-up capital. That the goodwill should be
wholly written off is beyond question. To put down any-
thing for the goodwill of a business in this condition is a
mockery.
Under these circumstances, Mr. Shadier has
agreed to surrender 5000 fully-paid £1 shares received by
him as part payment for the business. Out of the £5OOO
capital thus written off the loss and depreciation are to
be provided for, £lOOO written off book debts, and some
small sums off goodwill and formation expenses. These
latter appear to have been wildly exorbitant; £lll5
expenses in the formation of a co. with a capital of
£14,894 is a ridiculous item. Then the co. is supposed to
be left with a reserve fund of £2OB. But in reality, with
the purely paper items eliminated, the accounts should
be presented thus:
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital .. .. £14,894 Leaseholds, Plant,
Less written off .. 6,000 Buildings, &c... £19,120
Book Debts .. 5,100
£9,894 Stock .. .. 2,102
Debentures .. 8,000 Cash .. .. 446
Owing to Bank .. 6,385 Deficiency.. ... 2,822
Sundry Debts .. 5,311
£29,590 £29,590
What A. Shadier, Limited, evidently requires is to write
off enough capital to clear away all the paper assets ; to
practise strict economy and good management; and to
raise enough new capital (if it can be obtained) to pay off
the debentures and bank overdraft. A concern in its
state can’t afford to pay interest on £14,385 of borrowed
money. Apparently, if depreciation and the bad debts
actually made during the year had been allowed for, the
co. would have earned £3026 and spent some £5OOO or
£6OOO ; and when a co. only earns about lls. or 12s. for
every £ it spends very strong measures are needed.

The Colonial Finance, Mortgage, Investment
and Guarantee Corporation (Sydney) continues its career
of misfortune after this fashion :

„ 1901 .. 2,573 2,665 .. 148,862
The chief asset of this company— whose balance-sheets
bear the honored name of “ Bruce Smith, chairman ”
seems to be its intense resolve to pay everybody in full,
no matter what happens. It keeps putting fresh capital
into the concern with an energy worthy of a better insti-
tution. Out of its original 100,000 shares, only 21,712
have been forfeited for non-payment of calls, and the
representatives of the other 78,288 have kept things going
after this fashion :
Accumulated Paid-up
Loss. Capital. Deficit.

„ 1899 .. 138,734 .. 137,334 .. 1400
Surplus.
„ 1900 .. 143,624 .. 146,806 .. 3182
„ 1901 .. 148,862 .. 153,676 .. 4814
If the creditors of the Colonial Finance, Mortgage,
Investment, and Guarantee Corporation don’t shout the
board at least one sixpenny drink when the final settle-
ment is made, they are a crowd to which gratitude is an
unknown quantity. The co. was founded in 1888, when
the boom was on its last legs, and it only paid three
moderate dividends before the collapse struck it. As
early as 1892 it began to make calls with a view to
covering losses, so it has no bloated past to reproach itself
with.
* * * *
Sydney Telegraph’s share-list seems to run its
leading articles on Freetrade very close in some respects.
Looking down it casually last Tuesday writer gathered
that Paddington Brewery had “ reserves and balance ”
amounting to £7776, which was certainly the figure in
1897, but it is now £21,369. It set forth that the Mercan-
tile Mutual Fire Insurance Co. £1 shares are fully paid-
up, and were consequently selling at a big discount;
whereas they are only paid up to 45., and sell
at nearly 200 per cent, premium. It described
the reserves of the Fresh Food and Ice Co. as £19,397
when they are really nearly £37,000 if depreciation fund
is included, and only about £l2OO if it isn’t. Melbourne
Tramway Co., Mort’s Dock, Balmain Ferry Co., Com-
mercial Bank of Australia, and a few other concerns that
really have reserves or undivided profits, appeared
as having none. The Melbourne Tramway Co.
has nearly £300,000 of one kind and another.
Australian Gaslight Co.’s reserves appeared as
£124,568, though the balance-sheet seems to show
them as about £114,000 odd. The reserves of
the North Coast Steamship Co. were set forth in the
Telegraph as £25,769, which was the figure at the end of
1899; it is now £28,002. The reconstructed deposits of
the Commercial Bank’s Asset Trust were given as
£3,551,000, which was quite correct at the end of 1897,
though it is now rather out of date. The A.J.S. Bank’s
reserves showed up as £69,076, though its total reserves
amount to over £220,000. These are the results of a 10
minutes’ inspection of the Telegraph’s share-list.
Probably it would repay closer investigation,

The Mutual Fire Insurance Co. of Tasmania
(paid-up capital, £37,500) is a small concern which does
business in Tasmania only, and consequently has a
smaller field of operations than any other co. in
Australasia. Nevertheless it produces some remarkably
good results, as under :
Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
September, 1898 £17,417
„ 1899 £4978 7 per cent. = £2756 19,639
„ 1900 4403 7 „ „ 2756 21,286
„ 1901 3793 7 „ „ 2756 22,323
The reserves are composed of reserve fund, £12,000 ; con-
tingency fund, £3500 ; reserve for unexpired risks, £2830 ;
and undivided profit, £3993. The company’s investments
at interest are compressed into one line: “ Mortgages,
£56,894.” They yield at the very respectable rate of
£5 os. 9d. per cent, per annum, and there is no reason at
all to doubt their solidity, but for a company to have
practically all its funds invested in mortgages looks like
doubtful policy none the less. Despite unusually heavy
fire claims this year profits amount to over 10 per cent, on
capital. The Mutual Fire of Tasmania is a small office (its
total premiums last year were £5659) doing business on a
small island, but it seems a very strong and safe institu-
tion none the less.
* * * *
At Red fern (Sydney) police-court last week a
woman was sent to gaol for a month because she could
not pay some time-payment-furniture arrears. Imprison-
ment for debt is supposed to have been abolished in
N.S.W., but time-payment merchants (in many cases
shameless sharks) are able, under the Tovvns Police Act, to
send their debtors to gaol, if a magistrate can be induced
to administer the law as it stands. Fortunately that is
not often.

owing to some improvement in the mine’s prospects, but

The Occidental, Cobar, cleaned-up last week
(battery and cyanide) for £4OOO worth of gold ; the Mt.
Drysdale clean-up was about £3BOO. The Occidental,
after squaring all accounts, will begin paying divs. in
December—and thence regularly, the management says.
The mine is now in great shape, and, with its greatly-
enlarged dam full, has ample water for many months. It
is, by the way, just about time that something in the way
of divs. was heard from the Oobar Gold Mines Ltd. (old
Fort Bourke), which has been steadily battering away at
its big lode for two years or more with 100 head of stamps,
and which, between battery, cyanide and filter-presses, is
said to be turning out between £6OOO and £BOOO worth of
gold monthly.

* * * *
Kalgoorlie mines: “The lack of official news con-
firming several recent important developments in the
leading mines has drawn attention to the present system
of publishing reports locally some time after particulars
have been cabled to London. Certain co.’s require their
managers to await the receipt of a reply to a cable, how-
ever long the delay, before giving publicity to any infor-
mation. It is contended that in the case of those mines
having Australian boards of directors great discretionary
powers should be exercised, thus putting the Australian
shareholders on the same footing as those abroad.”
Great Scott ! what is going to happen ? What has
suddenly come over the wire-hummers of Kalgoorlie
that this wail should be uttered ? There must
be something in the wind. The practice of London
keeping Australian shareholders in the dark has hitherto
gone on without a murmur. In fact, Australian
newspapers seem to like it and to regard it as the right
thing to do. But there is more than one way out of the
difficulty. The Bulletin’s is to force the situation.
Instead of painting in exaggerated colors every good
development, why not tell the simple truth about a few
bad ones ? The custom is to conceal everything bad and
to exaggerate everything good. For instance, it is well
known out there, though not a newspaper word has been
said about it, that the lode from which the Golden
Horseshoe gets the great bulk of its gold, and which came
from the Great Boulder mine, looks as if it were going
to swing back into that mine again. A good thing for
the Boulder, but a very bad one for the Horseshoe.
Besides, at about 1000 ft. in the Great Boulder there is
already a lode which is hard to identify, and which may
have come in from the Horseshoe where its existence has
not been established at that depth. Why not, too, give
up preaching that all the wonderful consignments of rich
ore from the Associated Northern come from develop-
ments, and that not a foot of stoping has been done—
when it is well known that the gold chutes have such a
course, and are so worked, that more ore is likely to be
got from this so-called development work than from all
the stoping put together ? Hey 1
* * * *
Associated Northern : Several London news-
papers have started in to boom this mine beyond its worth,
good as it is. Among them is the Statist, which ought
to know better than to smirch its good reputation by talk
so tall as to have a fishy flavor about it. It gives the
co.’s record up to April last for ore sent away as 3925
tons, containing approximately 31,0000 z. gold, and it all
came from development work only. A table is given
showing the weight and gold production from each
parcel, the total being 4552 tons for 36,9090 z. ; aver-
age per ton, Boz. 2dwt., and total value, £128,212.
“Only the Iron Duke area has, so far, been at all
developed upon, and it is noteworthy that, with no
reduction plant whatever, the co. should have se-
cured about £130,000 from ore, nearly all derived
from development work.” True, this co. has several
other leases, including the Iron Monarch and the
Menindie Hill, but these are not worked, for the very best
of reasons—they are not considered worth it. They are
duffers surrounded by duffers—altogether outside the
gold-bearing belt, and quite valueless. Developments in
the Iron Duke have disclosed two rich chutes of ore, one
close to the Hannan’s Brown Hill boundary, and the other
near the main shaft. The Statist thinks that were
it not for the unfortunate state of mistrust that
exists in the W.A. market, more attention would
be given by speculative investors to these shares,
and they would have reached the market appreciation of
the Golden Horseshoe. The directors are recommended
to pursue the policy of increasing shipments, and to do
some stoping as well as shipping ore only from drives and
winzes. “There appear to be large quantities of ore
available, and, by marketing it to smelters, large profits
can be secured without interfering with the progress of
development in depth.” This would sound remarkably
well if development work did not take up the best part of
the chutes, and if the mine had not been so worked that
its value and ore tonnage can be measured to the nicest
accuracy. If the Statist bought Northern Blocks at £4 a
share and waited, it would then forcibly and feelingly
understand that it is possible to carry drives and winzes
on ore chutes and leave little behind for stoping.
* * * *
Peak Hill (W.A.) : These shares have fallen in
London to a little over £2, and for every drop that un-
mitigated boomster, “Moses Moss,” of London Truth,
has some excuse, and predicts a “ rapid recovery.” The
last excuse was on account of the shortage of water and
a strike amongst the miners, and he gave the returns for
the two previous months —July and August respectively
—2391 tons for 23860 z., 2172 tons for 16360 z. It will be
seen that, although for the last month there was a big
falling-off in yield, there was but a slight reduction in
tonnage ; so how could the shortage of water or the
strike of the miners have anything to do with-that? The
persistent, and yet inconsistent, way in which “ Moses
Moss” has boomed this mine for three years
has always been a mystery to the writer ;
no doubt to some people the reason is as clear
as noonday. Writer has just received from the mine a
sketch of it, which clearly shows that it is a basin of
kaolin, which, in any other country, would be regarded
as an alluvial deposit. There are quartz reefs below it,
but, wherever these have been opened, they have proved
barren, and none of them are being worked. What is left
of the basin is being mined by open cut, and probably
another 30,000 tons, for a much less number of ozs., will
exhaust the basin and end the life of the mine. Despite
this fact, it has a valuation of over £600,000 —at one time
it was worth on the market over two millions ; and it has
only given, in four years, 10s. Bd. in divs., the last of
which was paid over a year ago. At less than 2000 oz. a
month the mine must be working at a loss.
Oroya, W.A. : These shares have doubled in
value within the last two months. The first spurt in
them was due to the striking in a bore-hole what is
believed to be a continuation of the Brown Hill and
Northern Associated run of gold. The second and bigger
spurt was on account of finding a rich continuation of the
old lode worked in the mine. Statements vary as to its
value, but the market talks in the right direction, and it
looks as if the Oroya will yet become a gold-producer, if
not a div.-payer.
* * * *
Cosmopolitan, W.A. : A few weeks back writer
remarked how steadily these shares had been rising from
£1 till they reached 355. A set-back started immediately
afterwards in face of a spurt in most other Westralians.
No newspaper reason appeared. In fact, by several it
was declared that the mine was looking better than ever ;
shares fell from 355. to 28s. in a fortnight. The cat has
now got out of the bag—the reef has pinched from
several feet to a few inches. Months ago writer was told
of a big break in the country which would likely be met
with in main shaft, and it looks as if that has come about.
No statement has appeared in the press about the
pinching of the reef —that is only in accordance with the
usual custom. The truth is generally revealed after the
shareholders have been nipped, and we may expect to
hear all about it next month. Had the reef increased in
size and richness the fact would have been pictured in
variegated colors. The Bulletin has also been furnished
with some details of Bewick, Moreing and Co.’s connection
with this mine. They received 100,000 shares for which
they agreed to erect a modern 50-head stamp mill on
the Englishman’s lease, and they also “ chucked” in the
Diamantina, a lease on the south end of the Englishman.
All chutes of gold strike north towards the Cumberland
lease, which, ’tis said, will be the mine, if the reef lives to
(say) 700 ft. The mill should not have cost more than
£30,000 to £40,000, so even at present prices of shares
B.M. and Co. have a long way the best of the bargain.
They usually do, until it is often asked how much longer
this cute and enterprising firm is to be allowed to play
the game of erecting batteries on proven good mines for
shares which generally represent several times the value
of the plant erected. The manager of the Cosmopolitan is
W. Hayes, formerly of Parkes and Grenfell, N.S.W.
» * * *
Mt. Garnet Freehold : Half-yearly meeting last
week in Melbourne. Cost per ton of copper for period in
question is given as £37 10s, thus made up : Smelting
per ton of ore, 31s 9d : matte bagging and bags, 2s 6d ;
mining, 7s 5d ; development, 2s sd; transport charges,
6d ; water supply, 7d ; assay-office, 6d ; electric, 2d ;
general works, sd; management and offices, Is 7d. No
doubt the meeting dispersed under the impression that
£37 10s represented the total cost of the production of a
ton of copper, whereas it does nothing of the kind. It
means the total cost of a ton of copper in the form of
matte, which will need something to turn it into copper.
That something will depend on its grade. It could
not be worked-up on the mine without adding much to
the cost of a ton of copper. At present it is being sent
away on camel-back, by no means a cheap form of transit.
It is then sold to other smelters, and there are none run
on philanthropic principles. Then there are realisation
and other charges, of which not a word seems to have been
said at this meeting, and no allusion to them has been
made in the papers. Early next year this co. expects to
be able to make copper on the mine, and is building
a number of reverberatories for that purpose. Much
later than that they expect to have the railway
completed. As the ore is running about 6 per
cent, copper and lOoz. silver, the two furnaces may be
run without loss, but there can’t be much profit while the
matte has to be sent away, and coke and other supplies
brought in, by camel; if a profit was being made we
should have had it more clearly set out in the report or
by the chairman. A shareholder desired to know whether
the overdraft of £20,000 had been reduced, and, as the
question is skilfully evaded, it may be taken for granted
that the overdraft is still there. Better to go slow till
the railway is finished. The Garnet’s object-lesson
should be the N. Lyell, which, after sending many thou-
sands of tons of rich ore to the Mt. Lyell smelters, giving
them a fat flux, swelling that co.’s divs., and paying none
of its own, is about to start its own smelters.

Mount Flora copper-mine, 60 miles E. of
Mackay, Q., after lying idle 35 years, has been re-opened.
Ore from the surface and to a depth of 30ft. is said to
have yielded from 18£ to 35J per cent, copper.

“The Australian mining goose has been cooked
in London market certainly for another four years,” writes
a Bulletin correspondent. “ There may be a gosling or
two tastily served up during that period, but it won’t be
worth talking about. The English public that made the
boom in Westralia is now getting lost in the thickets that
abound in the West African jungle, and will only emerge
to dance on the Rand to the tune piped by Werner, Beit,
J. B. Robinson, Edmund Davis, and the Consolidated
Goldfields of S’Africa ‘ school.’ All of these, particularly
Beit, have ground staked out, both by English and Dutch,
in the country which was kept a close reserve by Kruger,
because he didn’t want any additional wrangling and
stock and share juggling to that already going on in
Johannesburg. This country, the writer was informed
by no less a person than Dr. Hatch, who wrote the
standard work on the Rand, will prove even richer and of
greater extent than anything yet opened up.” (And that,
of course, is why the benevolent S’African and London
Stock Exchange gang was in such a desperate hurry to
confer the blessings of Liberty on the poor Uitlanders,
downtrodden by the dirty, brutal, nigger-flogging Boers.)
“ Certainly there is no one in London at present capable
of running the Australian markets like the Whittaker-
Wright crowd, and it is equally certain that the English
speculator will never put up money to any extent unless
the market is being run. The English speculator remains
stolidly unmoved at the output of a Mt. Morgan or
anything ten times richer than Mt. Morgan unless he
knows, or thinks he knows, that a Whittaker-Wright or a
Percy Tarbutt, or an Edmund Davis, or even a Frank
Gardiner is going to put prices up. There is too much to
be made for years to come by such operations in the
‘ jungle ’ and in Kaffirs for London to care about Aus-
tralia to any great extent, meanwhile.”
» * * •
“The financial world was completely staggered,”
writes a Bulletin correspondent from London, “at the
‘ bust ’ which, by some apparently superhuman interven-
tion, was averted when Yankee rails crashed last mid-
summer. The secret history, not hitherto written, is
very simple. Practically the whole London Stock Ex-
change had been caught, either directly or indirectly, in
the shortage of Northern Pacifies by the combination
engineered by Pierpont Morgan. Morgan was chuckling
on the Saturday morning, on the Continent, when he
received an intimation that it would be as well for him to
call at Lord Rothschild’s private house on the Sunday
evening. He did so, meeting a pleasing little coterie, and
was told by Rothschild that he and his friends would
refuse in future to handle any paper issued by the Pier-
pont-Morgan house unless the ‘ shorts ’ were released at a
figure which, although much above the intrinsic value of
the stock, certainly did not resemble those that Morgan
had in his mind’s eye. Strange to say, not one of the
great financial houses represented at Rothschild’s resi-
dence that quiet Sunday evening were directly interested,
but Rothschilds were not going to allow a crisis to run
riot unless it had been brought about at their direction.
It was the first time in his life, perhaps, that Morgan
ever learned that there was another power in the universe
besides himself and the Almighty. He is reported not to
have liked England so much afterwards.”

Dear Bulletin, — Your “Wild Cat ” contributor
of about four months ago sums up Rhodesia pretty
fairly, except that he errs about the gold returns. In
the Globe and Phoenix, Matabeleland, we have a mine
that ranks among the world’s best, having yielded an
average of over 5000 oz. monthly for the past six months,
50770 z. July, and 57020 z. June, being the returns for the
past two months. Matabeleland returns for June give
a total of 12,5920 z. from six mines, and the July returns
totalled 13,3840 z. Mashonaland has three producers—
Alice, Ayrshire, and Reyende— and these for June and
July totalled 45320 z. ; which, with the Matabeleland
output of 25,9830 z., gives for these two months an output
of 30,5150 z. These returns—with white labor, paid 255.
for eight hours, black labor scarce, and explosives and
material at prohibitive prices—speak for themselves ;
and, in days to come, Rhodesia, with a beautiful climate,
plentiful water, and a magnificent soil, will surely
prosper.—Ex-Aus, Umtali, Rhodesia, 24/8/’Ol. [But will
our contributor, when he next writes, tell about Rho-
desian mining conditions—especially rates of royalty, and
so on. That, no doubt, is the fly in the ointment.— Ed. B.]

The Kanaka cause must be at its last gasp.
Cant has come to its aid. Says a correspondent
of Sydney D.T. :
In North Australia the climate is, to a large extent,
more or less unsuited to white labor, and are we not all,
whether white or black, children of our Father which is
in Heaven ; for lie maketh His sun to rise on the evil and
on the good, and sendeth rain on the just and on the
unjust.
Quite correct. And that is the reason why the
Unjust invented umbrellas. And—the moral is
obvious.

• * * *
Bishop Goe’s episcopate has been a dismal
failure, and no sooner is the burden of the Old
Man of the See lightened than up spring three
new dioceses—Bendigo, Wangaratta and Gipps-
land—like Minerva, ready-armed, from the brain
of Jupiter. Jupiter happens in this case to be a
certain Canon Hindley, a square, rasping-voiced
man, of high ability. Meanwhile, England is to
be ransacked for a nice, fat gentleman to drop into
the Melbourne hole, while the poor shows of
Bendigo, Wangaratta, and Gippsland are to be
distributed locally. Victoria can create a Madden,
but not a Goe.

The John See Ministry, looking at it in
the collective jaw, does not seem a likely body to
pause from faked-figure discussions to put a sudden
stop by Act of Parliament to the hideous
defilement of Sydney Harbor and other beauty-
spots with advertisement-hoardings. But if
legislation does not protect the people from the
outrage of having scenery which is a national
possession ruined by glaring puffs of tea and
obscene-disease medicines, there ought to be
enough public spirit in the community to ensure
the general destruction of these disfigurements
some dark night, and a general agreement to pass
by the shop of the man who resorts to this
vandalistic form of advertisement.

Apropos the harbor-advertisement leprosy.
The recent yachtsman’s aquatic funeral in Port
Jackson was a charming spectacle, until there
intruded among the white-winged angels of the
sapphire waters a boat whose sails carried the
device, “ Bile Beans for Biliousness.”
* * * *
Fred. Dickson, the erstwhile genial
accountant of the Melbourne University, was
always a favorite with the students. They pre-
ferred to deal with him rather than with Registrar
A’Beckett, who had the reputation, rightly or
wrongly, of being crabbed and difficile. It will be
remembered that when A’Beckett’ obtained the
appointment John Madden, Preacher, was also a
postulant for the billet. A’Beckett is a brother of
the Judge of the same name. If his Wax. had got
the job it is not at all likely that things would
have been allowed to drift as they have done.
But, on the other hand, Victoria would never
have known the wondrous glory of Madden.

* * * *
Magnificent war ! Private A. Lancaster,
of Ballarat (Vic.) City Engineer’s office, who
lately returned from active service in S’Africa,
brought with him a trophy in the shape of a Boer
baby’s “ bootee ” ; history as follows
On 17th Feb., 1900, at Paardeplats, the 4th Vic. Con-
tingent were chasing a Boer convoy, and a Cape cart,
covered, was seen going at a gallop. Some of the men
shot at the horses in the cart, with a view to stopping it,
and it appeared, when they examined the cart, that they
had shot a Boer women through both knees. She was
sitting in the cart sheltering a baby. The bullet passed
through both her knees and the lower part of the baby’s
body, and the child was killed. Mr. Lancaster brought
away one of the baby’s “ bootees ”as a relic. The next
day he was shot, and, when he left the scene of battle,
the baby’s “ bootee ” was the only souvenir of the war
which he carried away.

Furious call for Ber-lud by a “ right-
thinking person ” in Adelaide Advertiser :~
To the Editor. —Sir, —Leon Czolgosz, the anarchist, has
been sentenced to death, and up to the present time has
escaped lynching. There is no warrant in Scripture for
the electrocution of the ruffian. On the contrary, *
“ Whosoever sheddeth man’s blood, by man shall his
blood be shed.” No definite time, and no special mode
are named. Exemplary punishment (not necessarily ven-
geance) is required as a warning to evildoers. The law
relating to capital punishment should be amended to
meet special and exceptional cases of brutality. Leon
Czolgosz should have been sentenced to death by guillo-
tine or axe, after the space of 21 years. Immediately
after the death sentence had been passed, he should have
been taken to a public pillory, and in the presence of
spectators flogged with “forty stripes, save one.” On
every anniversary of his dastardly act the same punish-
ment should be openly and publicly inflicted. Such a
course would stop the future commission of such offences.
—I am, &c., Zacharias J. C. Barker.
It takes your true religionist, with a Biblical
name, to hoard up vengeance and deal out savage
torture to a lunatic for one-and-twenty years.

Old age pensions in N.S.W. have created
a new calling. Last week a “professional
corroborator ” was sent to gaol. His business was
to furnish pension applicants, for a fee, with
declarations corroborating their grounds of claim.
♦ * * *
General Hector Macdonald “knocked”
Melbourne’s Naicest endways by turning up to
all functions in a brown slouch hat. The bell-
toppered crowd who were waiting on the plat-
form when he arrived didn’t know him as he
stepped out of the carriage, and were standing
aside to let him by. The General was utterly un-
military in his acknowledgments, and his free-
and-easy style nearly made several Leg. Council-
lors, who have spent a life-time in the effort to
acquire Dignity, faint on the spot. He collared a
big bandsman by the shoulder in a rough,
brotherly way, shook hands with anybody who
chose to coine along, and was no more impressed
by the gilded persons who saw an opportunity for
self-advt. in his visit than he was by casual Aus-
tralians who simply felt interested in a plucky
fighter. The General is said to be here on a
pleasure-trip, but it is a peculiar coincidence that,
after five months’ delay (which the Govt, is unable
to explain satisfactorily) in the appointment of a
Federal Commandant, the screw should be re-
raised to surpass that which Macdonald is now
getting in India ; and, further, that he should
suddenly come to a quarter of the globe in which
he appears to have no particular personal interest.
* * * •
The Mayor of Ballarat (Vic.), is a strict
teetotaller and a right-thinking man, and is
striving to throw an interesting gloom over that
place. One of his ideas is a bye-law to ring a
curfew bell at 9 o’clock, when children under the
age of 18 must go home. He will not have any grog
kept in the Town Hall, and visitors are offered tea
or coffee. At a council meeting, t’other day, the
King was toasted in coffee by all except one
councillor, who, furious at such chilling blasphemy,
produced a whisky-flask from his pocket, much to
the disgust of the Mayor.

tralasian Govt, not to care about books —except
the faked national account-books. Hori Grey’s
valuable collection, bequeathed to Auckland
library, has not yet been unpacked, but lies
higgledy-piggledy in odd corners with the cob-
webs and dust of years for a covering.

public has, pro-

that public

* * * *
One of those disastrous persons who
would rather kill a man than not do him a good
turn lately transpired in the Westralian Assembly.
George, M.L.A., rose to ask the Attorney-General
if he would do something, as a man and a brother
should, to protect Mr. Justice Parker from un-
merited outrage. He had heard that a certain
paper had prepared an article grossly attacking Mr.
Parker (a newly-appointed Judge, who isn’t what
might be called wildly popular); and that Parker,
hearing of it, and probably feeling that the law
gave him no adequate protection against such
things, had paid a large sum to have it suppressed.
George was so broken up with grief that he read
the article to the House, so that it might
see the full dimensions of the outrage com-
mitted upon Parker. Now, Judge Parker says
indignantly that he never paid anything to have
any article suppressed, and that he never
even heard of the thing before. So the
result seems to be that George, in his wild
yearning to do the Judge a good turn, has held
him up as a weak man who submitted to black-
mail, and has also published a large mass of
violent abuse against Parker which probably no
one would otherwise have heard of ! And the
abuse being read in Parliament, where such things
are privileged, Judge Parker has no redress. The
world is full of friends like that.

“ Parramatta ” writes :
Among the items passed by N.S.W. Assembly among
“charitable allowances” were—Salvation Army, £500;
Westmead Home, £750 ; and Sydney Rescue Work
Society, £5OO. Westmead Home is a purely R.O. institu-
tion ; the L. F. Heydon crowd run it. The Rescue Work
Society is an institution run by G. E. Ardill on out-and-
out anti-Rome principles. On these votes Haynes,
M.L.A., challenged the Government with aiding
denominational institutions. See explained that all
were subject to Govt, inspection, and their accounts sub-
mitted to the “ Inspector-General of Charities.” Let
Haynes ask Mr. See the name of this Inspector-General of
Charities. He will find that there is no such person.
Also, when did a Govt, officer last visit Salvarmy sweat-
ing shops or Heydon’s fledgeling, or Ardill’s Rescue
Home ? The last time Ardill’s places were “ inspected ”
was in 1897, by the Public Service Board, sitting as a
Royal Commission, and the report—well, j'ust look it up
for yourself.
* * * *
Shocks and distractions have arisen in
Melbourne through the conduct of a lady, who
seized upon the son of a N.S. Wales M.P. in the
street, and demanded that he should be handed
over to her as her long-lost husband. At least,
defendant claims to be the son of a M.L.A. —and
no evidence to the contrary is just now available.
He is at present out on bail upon a charge of
wife-desertion, and denies being anybody’s
long-lost husband, and declares his intention
of pursuing the lady—described as a nice-looking
person of 32 —for perjury with the utmost horrors
of the law. Her story is that he married her in
Sydney under the name of John Reade, and when
his writing was compared with that of John
Reade, whoever the latter might be, on the
marriage-certificate, the experienced Mr. Pan-
ton, P.M., intimated that certain facts ap-
peared to stick out conspicuously, and that a
very strong impression of some kind or other had
arisen in his mind. He sternly impounded the
documents, and seemed to imply that something
large and remarkable was liable to happen to
somebody. The lady says, by the way, that she
doesn’t want to keep the man she has found ; she
only desires to prove his identity so as to facilitate
divorce proceedings. Under the circumstances,
it seems that the court might just as well grant
her a divorce from John Reade on the ground of
desertion, and attend to the other questions
afterwards.

The nerve of some people ! A burglar
who had his leg broken by a policeman’s bullet at
Waverley (Sydney) last week tried to escape by
hopping off on one leg, the broken limb dangling
as he ran. After capture he was put into a
delivery-van, but again tried to get clear by
jumping from the fast-moving vehicle, and
hopping away till he fell exhausted.

Dear Bulletin. —lie this par., published
in Bulletin 19/10/’01:
R. D. Meagher, M.L.A., has been elected to Sydney
City Council, polling 316 votes out of 2284 on the roll.
The choice, and the sma’lness of the vote, indicate pretty
clearly what Sydney thinks of its “ reformed council.”
Surely that is not the only deduction. May not
that curious result indicate (1) that the citizens
don’t think at all on municipal subjects ; (2) what
Sydney thinks of Mr. R. D. Meagher and Mr.
Blake; (3) that Sydney (that is, the half-million
of the real Sydney) has no chance of expressing
its thoughts in one small ward of the little
Sydney; (4) that the vote of “316 votes
out of the 2284 on the roll ” only indicates the
apathy of that particidar ward of Sydney.
The “reformed council,” no doubt, does as well as
it can for a body with wretchedly inadequate
powers, ludicrously insufficient funds, a legacy of
debt, bad system, mismanagement, and incom-
petent servants from its predecessors, and only
nine months of municipal existence. Anyhow, at
the general election, when all the city was bursting
for reform, the percentage of votes polled in
Sydney was but 50 per cent, of the actual number
on the rolls. A Combined Sydney may arouse
more interest, but the little spot called the City
of Sydney for municipal purposes, and of which
many of the ratepayers are non-residents, can’t
do enough to keep itself warm. —Yours, G-eebung.

* * * *
A civilised piece of legislation, introduced
in Vie. Leg. Assembly by little Dr. Maloney, was
badly mangled by Attorney-General Gillott ere it
got through. Maloney brought down a Bill to
amend the law so as to enable the father of an
illegitimate to register it in his own name, and to
confer upon it the same privileges as if it had
been born in wedlock ; whereupon barrister
MacKinnon and onion-grower Levien objected
that the Bill proposed to damage the marriage tie.
Gillott, in his smug police-court style, moved that
a father should only be allowed to register his
illegitimate child in his own name if the parents
afterwards married. Maloney opposed the amend-
ment, but the instincts of the House was against
him, and he was forced to accept Gillott’s clause.
Dear Bulletin.— The “ cost of
babies,” apparently, doesn’t trouble
us much in the bush. Within a score
of miles of me just now are four
families with, respectively, 13, 14, 16,
and 21 children —all living, and all
“introduced ” without other aid than
that of the “handy woman,” who has
never even heard of “obstetrics” or
“aseptics.” Women reared in the
bush, and living an active life, get
over their troubles with far greater
ease, and with less ill consequences,
than their city sisters. —B.R.

“Vulcan”: Re Vic. Customs book-sup-
pressing farce. Seems to me that the Minister,
though well-meaning, blundered badly. If he is
consistent in a determination to prevent the circu-
lation of “improper” works, he cannot stop at
Balzac, and the following will have to be sup-
pressed : The Bible, Shakespeare, Dryden, Defoe,
Swift, Butler, Fielding, Prior, Beaumont and
Fletcher, Sterne, Massinger, Jonson, Johnson,
Smollett, Burns, Byron, Wycherley, Rossetti,
Swinburne, the majority of the “classics,” and no
small proportion of the “moderns.” What is
left ? And, once begun, where is the crusade to
be stopped ?

The native flora of the N.S.W. public
parks is being utterly destroyed by flower-sellers.
At Kuringai Chase, recently, a plantation of
waratahs was completely denuded in one night, a
cart being used to remove the spoil. This sort of
thing should mean gaol every time.

w • * *
A visit to Rookwood (Sydney) Asylum for
the poor, by a Bulletin man, did not disclose
anything to justify the complaint from “ Carolus,”
recently published. The asylum-building was
once intended to be a reformatory for bad hoys,
which was, perhaps, one of the reasons why it was
located within sight of the big metropolitan ceme-
tery, so that the fear of death {which is the begin-
ning of wisdom) should be instilled into their hearts.
Excepting this proximity to the graveyard, there is
nothing to find fault with at Rookwood. The
asylum has spacious grounds, prettily planted
with flowers and vegetables ; its situation is airy
and wholesome. The buildings, practically all of
one storey, arranged on the pavilion principle,
are admirably ventilated, and kept thoroughly
clean. No fault could be found with the bedding
or with the laundry arrangements, though the
need of a steam-laundry is severely felt in damp
weather. The food seemed clean and wholesome.
It only costs 5s. a week to maintain an inmate at
Rookwood, though many of the wards are hos-
pitals for various types of chronic and incurable
diseases. But the institution grows its own
vegetables on a small irrigation farm, and also
keeps pigs, poultry and cows. Economy is thus
secured without stinting the sick, who are allowed
eggs, milk, brandy, chicken-broth, and other
delicacies as ordered by the doctor. Mrs. McDer-
mott is the matron superintendent, and the in-
mates seem to like her rule. If the writer ever
comes to a penniless old age he would certainly
prefer Rookwood, despite its graveyard view, to
any other of the State asylums for the poor.
* « • *
“Ex-R.E.” writes concerning the recent
Newcastle (N.S.W.) accident, involving the death
of Mr. Allsopp, engineer, by the sudden break-up
of the new reversible turbine engine from which
he hoped to make fame and fortune: —
The machine was running at the speed of 6000 revolu-
tions a minute when the revolving-disc (made of cast-iron)
flew to pieces, one of which hit Allsopp on the head and
killed him. But it is astounding that anybody calling
himself an engineer should have subjected a substance so
brittle as cast-iron to such a strain. If that disc, flying
round 100 times in a second, was but a foot in dia., every
point Of its circumference would move 314ft. per second ;
but if, as is more likely, it was 3ft. or so in dia., its cir-
cumference would have been moving 942 ft. per sec.—a
pace that would cause even flawless cast-iron to fly ; and
the trouble is that you can’t get an iron-casting without
flaws. Poor Allsopp seems to have forgotten that there
is such a thing as centrifugal force.
* * * *
Invermay (Vic.) tragedy developments had
some curious features. James O’Reilly (75) and
his wife Bessie (72) were charged with killing
their daughter. The police exhumed the girl’s
body and had it examined without any representa-
tive of accused being present, and one witness was
pumped whilst too drunk to sign his name. The
Crown had sense enough to suppress this witness.
In the end the police proved nothing. They
seem to have started with the idea that it was
necessary to hang someone, and to have fixed on
the handiest person—the old man. As he failed
to fill the bill the old woman was roped in too, in
the hope of getting at least one neck. But the
evidence wasn’t strong enough to hang a cat, and
there is a pretty strong impression that the young
woman, after all, died a natural but unexplainable
death.
DIDN’T KNOW WHAT
TO DO.
MR. UPTOSNUFF : “ There go
the Oldings. Their family seems ever on
the increase.”
MRS. U. : “ Yes, she’s like the old woman who

MRS. U. : “ Well, she didn’t know what to do,
of course.”
The Australian Churchman is convinced
that outrages upon missionaries in China are still
to be expected, and considers this
is a fact, which our readers will consider a call for more
urgent prayers. It should give increased interest to the
prayer-meeting in the Chapter House and the valedictory
meeting to Miss Oxley, who is returning very shortly to
her station in China.
There is in this missionary business a good deal of
the spirit which prompts audiences to go tp a
circus to see men swallowing swords, or somer-
saulting over bayonets—a spirit which is an
atavistic survival of the bloodthirstiness of pri-
meval man. A missionary can’t rouse any en-
thusiasm unless he or she is setting off for a
dangerous place, but then a big crowd rolls up,
fascinated by the prospect of a report coming out
in a year or two of some revolting outrage on
the departing one. d
* * * *
The Marrickville tragedy .’’tvas a great
windfall for the picturesque reporter. Sydney
D.T. man thus got grimly to grip with his sub-
ject :
With a terrified scream she closed with her maddened
husband, and they struggled desperately about the room,
the woman meantime screaming- for help. The man con-
tinued to shower blows upon her, but their force was
broken owing to the fierce nature of the struggle. They
grappled together for several minutes, and the man
stumbling near the doorway gave the woman an oppor-
tunity to break away, and she rushed shrieking out of
the house.
It is almost a premium upon murder to have the
gory deed written up in this earnest and adequate
style. One can picture the reporter sitting in the
D.T. back-room stabbing his pen viciously in the
ink-pot and wishing that it contained blood—or
Beer. There is, however, a drawback to this
vivid, lurid style of narrating simple incidents.
There is a gruesome story of a conscientious
French reporter who tried to cut his editor’s
throat in order to be able to describe realistically
the feelings of a homicide.

“ The ‘ light-cure ’ for lupus,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ has just
received a tremendous ‘boost,’ thanks to the
engineer of London Hospital where the Finsen
system is installed. Up to now the Finsen
30,000 candle-power lamps (electric arc) costing
£lOO each have treated 214 cases. Of these, 40
have made complete cures, the remainder are
nearly all being successfully treated, and there is
not one case which has not yielded in some
degree. The engineer aforesaid has invented a
new ‘ curing light ’ which costs but £2O, and
affects three times the area treated by the Finsen.
The latter costs two shillings per hour per
patient; the former twopence. Again, the new
lamp takes but 10 or 12 minutes to do what is an
hour’s work for the Finsen. Further, this wonder-
ful Aladdin’s lamp is handy, allows the patient
to ‘lie comfortable,’ and it can be brought with
ease and celerity into any angle. It would appear
to be as great an improvement upon the Finsen
as the latter was upon our former medical im-
potence in curing the ‘ disease of the wolf.’ ”
* * * *
A supposed burglar broke into a house in
a N.S.W. country town, t’other night. The
house-mother awakened her husband, who grappled
desperately with the intruder; then she loaded
a gun, and was about to shoot when the burglar
surrendered. There is talk of presenting him
with the Royal Humane Society’s medal for
saving the husband’s life at the expense of his own
liberty.
