All Drapers Everywhere.
A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, October 22, 1901.
My Dkae Abini, —
The fashionable dressmaker is the most ex-
hausted woman at present in Australia. She
wishes there was no Melbourne Cup, or that the
time between now and then was elastic, or that
she was not called upon to help decorate the Cup
at such short notice. To the woman whose Cup
dress “has come home” other women are pil-
griming—to see it spread out on the bed. “ And
here’s the hat I’ll wear with it. Wasn’t I deter-
mined to have something different from anyone
else ? My Derby hat isn’t home yet, but it’s a
dream trimmed with—what do you think—trails
of hops in three shades of green. (A dream
trimmed with hops!) And here’s my shoes
(bother the grammar.) Just look at the laces, a
finger wide and stitched with silk. Sally sent
them to me from Paris. I don’t think anyone else
has got them. And here’s the newest thing in
cloaks—a dust-cloak by day and a theatre-wrap
by night. Of course, I’ve got a chiffon boa and a
change-parasol cover, and ”
Then the pilgrims file out of the shrine and
drink tea, and talk gowns and horses. A local
young man went round last week amongst his
friends, exclaiming joyfully, “I’ve drawn a
horse 1 ” He had 20 invitations to drink within
an hour, and his hand was swollen with
shaking before the inviters and shakers learnt
that the horse was drawn in pencil on a sheet of
notepaper.
To return to the dressmaker. If she had the
trick of using (or misusing) words, and had time,
how she could write ! Because she is the con-
fessoress of such a lot of women. It seems to be
an overwhelming necessity to some women to tell
their most private (and what ought to be sacred)
domestic matters to some other woman—the
costumibre preferred. Perhaps the fact that the
other woman is making a nice frock for you brings
to the surface all that is grateful, confiding, and
idiotic. Anyhow, whether it be sheer love of talk
(to a good listener) or a desire for sympathy, or
the dramatic instinct to excite wonder and pity,
the fashionable dressmaker is made a magazine of
dangerous explosives. ‘ ‘ And Saturn said nothing,
but went on making oysters a thousand years.”
That has always seemed to me a specimen
of splendid silence. “Oysters and a thousand
years! ”
The tariff duties may keep much gold lace and
swagger out of Sydney Harbor. Men-of-war
entertain largely, and the new duties will be like
Ossa on their wine, whisky, and tobacco. But
the revenue—think of what it means to the re-
venue ! The intrinsic value of good whisky is, I’m
told, seven or eight shillings a gallon, and
at that figure Jack, the glorified and the plain
Jack, has been used to getting it, and now his
contribution to the revenue on this item will
be about 200 per cent. A Freetrade philan-
thropist tells me that the raisins for poor Jack’s
plum-duff will carry a duty of 3d. per lb., which
means nearly 100 per cent, as the average price of
raisins go. But it seems to me if Jack prefers to
use the produce of Spain and Portugal, while he
can get fine, rich, clean Australian raisins, made
by his own countrymen at Mildura and other
places, it isn’t worth while weeping over his
troubles.
Mrs. J. C. Williamson entertained the lady
principals of the Italian Co. at luneh last week.
Also Signora Dani, a plump, grey-eyed, pretty
woman, was present. Likewise Signora Hazon
and Mrs. George Henderson. It was a crowded
table and an interesting. The Italians, with the
exception of Signorina Vaccari, have great masses
of black hair. Signora Benedetto’s is of the
blackest possible shade, and their complexions are
of the clearest olive or dead white. They have
picked up very little English during their three or
four months out of Italy, although some of them
speak French and German. Someone was trying,
in French, to make the Marcomini understand
that Sydney climate was injurious for the com-
plexion, and that lady, striking her cheek, replied,
in English, “Yais, yais, ver bad for de meat.”
Through Madame Sapio, who interpreted. Signora
Dani wished to know why some Australians said
“no ” and others “neow.” Isn’t that up against
us ! Signorina Marchi, wearing red and black
and a pair of big hoop earrings, looked like
Rebecca that someone in Scripture mashed at the
well. After lunch Madame Sapio read a letter
from Mr. Williamson, thanking the soprani and
contralti for their work, and for the generosity
with which his wishes had been carried out.
We are all glad to hear that London is “ re-
discovering” Mr. Titheradge. London is wont
to “snow-under” the actor who returns to it
again after 20 years’ absence. Pinero, at a
rehearsal of “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” in
which Titheradge played Aubrey to MTrs, Patrick
Campbell’s Paula, did, for him, a most unusual
thing, when he applauded the daffodil man for his
fine, original Tanquerayness. The Times also
compares Titheradge and George Alexander
(the original Aubrey) very much to the former’s
advantage.
How refreshing to'hear of probate being sought
on the will of an artiste who has been years off the
stage ! Mrs. Georgina Lyster, the widow of the
impresario, who died in Melbourne, recently, left
real estate valued at £1525, and personal at £6075,
in trust for the benefit of her daughter, Mrs.
George Dickson, of Sydney. Mr. Armes Beau-
mont, the tenor, gets a bequest of £l5O.
“Said Pasha,” “the operatic conceit,” shows
some fine color-blendings in the harem scenes.
'The dresses are all brand-new —made in Sydney—
the long Maoriland tour made the renewal neces-
sary, and the new robes do our costumibres credit, :
especially as they don’t make regularly for
•Sultanas and odalisques. Miss Stanton was
warmly received. Her voice is a pretty, light
soprano, and there is much of the Vital Spark in
her acting. For the song “ Kentucky Babe ” she
is encored over and over again. Miss Bertha
Nilsson (the Ideal for whom the hero has been
seeking, as of old they sought for the Holy Grail),
looks queenly enough, but her speaking voice
would be better if lower and slower.
Mr. G. Carlisle Taylor, who marries Miss Jean
Sawers, daughter of the general manager of the
Bank of Australasia, in Melbourne this week, gave
a farewell “ At Home ” to friends in this State at
the Hotel Australia on Monday afternoon. It
was held in the winter garden, and was extremely
Somebodyish. De Groen’s band played in one
corner, and a fortune-teller was behind a screen
in another.
Miss Daisy Chamier, who used to be a Sydney
infant prodigy on the piano, has returned from
years of foreign study, and gave her first recital
last week. Her infancy has matured to nice,
plump young womanhood, and her prodigious-
ness, musical of course, has developed along
chastened and technical lines. Her favorite com-
Eoser is evidently Schumann, and she interprets
im with insight and delicacy. She is not a florid
performer, and what little mannerisms she has
are daintily artistic. “Come hither, my dainty
Ariel,” she seems to be saying as she droops her
face very low over the keyboard. Mr. Edward
Sykes, Miss Katherine Pell, and Mr. Henry Weir
assisted at the recital. If Miss Chamier will
eliminate the Greek-cum-Madonna style from her
hair-dressing she will do herself a good turn.
Eyes like hers want rings of hair above them, and
a head like hers isn’t improved by a close-fitting
suit of hair ironed down and knotted on the top
of the spine.
Miss Flo. Greaves and Miss Daly held an exhi-
bition of their pictures before sending them to
N.S.W. Society of Artists’ Exhibition, which
opens next month. Like most young artists, the
two girls paint at the top of a high building, so
close under Heaven that they must often hear the
angels padding about aloft. However, a crowd
of well-known, well-dressed persons climbed the
stairs, and looked knowledgeably at the oils,
pastels, and water-colors.
Toxteth House, Glebe Point, formerly the resi-
dence of the late Wigram Allen, is now a convent.
The Sisters of the Good Samaritan have just
secured it. Their former quarters were in the
way of the new remote central railway station
and had to go. Toxteth House has over 70 large
rooms, and stands in beautiful gardens. Curiously
enough, there was already a chapel in the grounds.
Governor-General Hopetoun is said to spend
£12,000 a year in excess of his official salary.
Now that is real altruism, for the G.-G. doesn’t
get good fun or good health out of his billet, and
his wife doesn’t seem to like us overmuch. It is
probable that the State Governor’s salary will be
fixed at £5OOO, with salaries for private sec. and
A.D.C. at £350 each. The understudy Gov.
won’t be able to give many balls with boar’s head
and champagne on the supper-tables if he doesn’t
get at least that wage. By the way, Lord Lam-
mgton has had a little fortune left him by his
grandfather’s widow, and “the ladies of Queens-
land ” have sent to Grizel, the little daughter of
Lord Carrington, a present of a necklet in gold
fetter-links. Now, why this dwelling on the birth-
stain ! And what has Miss Grizel to do with
“ darbies” or the memories thereof ?
Lady Francis Hope (May Y ohe) about whom there
has been so much talk and print in San Francisco,
was here a year or so ago (she was staying at the
Australia Hotel) with her nice-looking, fresh-faced
husband. She looks as if she has a fifth or so of
negro blood in her, but she’s handsome, with a
good figure, a white face, and black hair and eyes.
It should interest the crowds of Australians who
continue to go to England for musical study, that
Mrs. Lewis, widow of the late Sam. Lewis, the
tri-millionaire money-lender, has founded 15
musical scholarships at the Royal Academy,
tenable for three years, and open to all British-
born subjects.
A Sydney bank-clerk was recently told to send
in his resignation because an infuriated husband
had been complaining to the manager of his (the
clerk’s) alleged indiscretion with complainant’s
wife. The manager said it wasn’t the giddiness
that he took exception to, but the possibility of
bloodshed on the bank premises disturbed him.
His nerves would not bear it. Few bank-managers
have such a fine capacity for getting upset by
other people’s troubles.
Oh, that the overworked, underfed bus-horse
could find a tongue like the Biblical ass ! Or that
he could yelp like a dog with a trod-upon paw.
Then would our streets be made hideous with
horse-talk or horse-screams, and the Common-
wealth would arise and insist upon eight hours
a day and three square meals for the laboring
horse. For the sake of the ’bus-culler I shall be
glad to see the motor-car come in for passenger-
carrying. It might do some good if State-school
and Sunday-school teachers would say a
daily word to their pupils on the blessed-
ness of pity for dumb beasties. There’s a terrible
lot of unnecessary cruelty. A youth whom the
police lately tracked for some offence to his hut
in the bush had three or four live rabbits nailed by
their feet to the table “for amusement.” Lately
as I was looking at a big live turtle in a Castle-
reagh-street doorway, a young man, very well
dressed, came up and exclaimed, “Good for
soup ! ” He then jumped on the creature’s back,
and stamped as if on a pavement. Even sup-
posing that it didn’t hurt, the instinctive cruelty
jnade him seem more of a reptile than the turtle.
All true patriots will rejoice to hear that,
according to the D.T., the hen-laying competition
inaugurated in Australia is receiving “world-
wide attention.” The eyes of four continents are
on our hens. The noise of cackling here is heard
above the roar of cannon in S’Africa. Competition
will extend over six months, and while the hens
will not be bustled or hurried up in any way, the
duration of the trial will be kepc from them in
case they might loaf for the first five months, and
rush matters at the last. How the desire for
higher, newer things affects even the hen ! An
advanced hen, somewhere in Sydney suburbs, has
laid her proud owner two heliotrope eggs. Now,
as soon as that hen lays an egg with an etching of
Sydney Harbour on it, or a tracing of the Federal
Flag ,
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Wonderful and woeful were some of the sights
making memorable Caulfield Cup. Some hats sat
like inverted soup-plates on the head. Some
over-frilled frocks stood out from the wearer’s
form divine, like much decorated shades on a
standard lamp. Chiffon ends did side-strokes
like an expert swimmer. Net, tulle, and lace
ruffs stood out around certain moist necks like the
fur of a startled cat. Trains of diaphanous stuff
trailed along the ground like sea-surf over the
sands. Hand-painted aprons in more than one
instance stretched ostentatiously over dainty
white silks. Yellow silk petals covered one frock
so that the wearer emulated the white and gold
roses climbing the lawn trellis.
“S’Rupert, looking, after his globe-trotting, of
rather a Nile-green complexion, introduced a
fashion in pale yellow kid gloves—the latest from
London. His dame starred it in strawberry-pink
voile, with a pantomimic ruff of white chiffon fill-
ing in her slender neck. Innumerable jewels,
chiefly emeralds and pearls, hung in a variety of
charms over her laeey vest. Strawberry tints pre-
vailed above all else. Mrs. Andrew Chirnside, a
rare visitor to town, wore the most exquisite turn-
out in this shade. Sassiety by one accord voted
for voiles, dull grounds, sheenless fabrics. The
booky and theatrical world went for silks of every
description and tremendous reefs of coral.
“The Countess Hopetoun and Carrie Moore
starred it on the lawn—Her Ex., tall, cool, ele-
gant, in the eternal grey, which had little har-
mony with its blue-chiffon octaves ; Carrie, on
the other hand, small, soft, fluffy, winsome,
in an embroidered white silk and chiffon outfit,
with a blue toque and gamp.
“ The Y.R. party was a big one—actually three
women in it with the Countess. Two were the
daughters of Gen. Sir Robert Low, mild-looking
girls, dressed in two shades of blue muslin. The
third was Mrs. Philson (Lady Lygon’s double), in
ficelle voile. A lesson in the cut direct was fur-
nished on the Terrace, when an exalted personage
refused the friendly hand of a squatteress. Hun-
dreds saw it.
“Among the gay birds of Sassiety’s most gilded
circle, none sported more showy plumage
than Judge Williams’ daughter, Muriel. ‘This
beauty was the most conspicuous person
in the assemblage. Like a good shopman,
she knows how to set her wares off ; and
her remarkable black and white striped silk
frock was a bull’s-eye affair in more senses
than one. Topping it was a theatrical —a regular
Floradora —hat in the same bull’s-eye tone, a big,
bold George Reid style of bow occupying the back
area. There wasn’t another dazzler as gorgeous or
as lovely in the whole throng of pretties. A
glorious confection in strawberry and cream tones
of Melbourne’s leading booky’s dame was the talk
of the day. It was fresh from Paris.
“Once more the Sydney Johnnie with the drain-
pipe neck annexes our youth, wealth, and beauty.
He smokes elephantine cigars, and sends up his
rakish wreath, as he gives an ear to beauteous
matron or maid on the terrace. Much of Saturday
he spent under the pinkish glow of a strawberry
dame’s gamp.
“ The crowd on the Terrace, and the length of
the ‘sweep promoters,’ led to much sartorial
sorrow, for wanton trains, flowing out willy-nilly
ways, were torn to shreds, and their tattered
remains lay, in sad reproof of the fashion, on the
tiles. However, one became resigned to have
one’s tail cut off, when the Countess stepped into
sight with a yard or two of salvage showing at her
skirt’s beam-end.
“Of the inter-State visitors at Caulfield Cup,
President Baker’s daughter was among the most
noteworthy. This Adelaide girl is inclined to be
buxoui, pretty, and lively. She wore a grey frock
and a heterogeneous hat, with blue and violet
mainsails.
“Alice Hollander, the Maoriland contralto,
seen at her first Melbourne concert (Oct. 17tli),
looked a comfortable, comely, white - frocked
young woman with dark hair, serious brown eyes,
'and solid, not-too-winsome platform manner. Her
voice is mellow and rich, especially in the middle
register, but the singer seems to suffer from a
shortage of sympathy, or else lacks the faculty of
expressing it. In ‘O Divine Redeemer,’ the
vocalist’s thoughts were set on the favorable pro-
duction of her pet low notes, so that when one of
those deep-downers had to be unearthed, the sen-
timent, written by Gounod into his song-prayer,
failed to get expression.
“Pale, slight pianiste Una Bourne, in sparse
white frock, was brilliant in a Kullak octave
study, and brainy in a Chopin group, but, to my
mind, she needs to be more hearty and less heady.
Reginald Gooud tossed off his songs in a truculent
spirit, not becoming to one of his slim build;
while short, squat, violinist Kahn, his head ‘ brim-
ming over with curls,’ looked about as artistic as
a Dutch cheese. The mention of truculence re-
minds me that Surgeon O’Hara heard the Hol-
lander concert from the balcony. What madness
impels a man to part with his mo. ? Male owners
of decorative mouths are not too common, and the
well-featured few are generally inspired by their
women-kind to clean-shave from the start. Thin
men who mow their countenances late in life are
prone to resemble seedy actors, while flesh-potters
are mistaken for bruisers. Mrs. O’Hara also
sampled the Hollander, as did warbler Olive
Kingston and many other songsters.
“ T’other night, when the Hopetouns saw
‘Tom, Dick, and Harry,’ they seemed to enjoy
themselves all the better for having put some of
the suite into the opposite box, to divert part of
the public glare from themselves. Hopey’s pet
farce-plum seemed to be Ruby Ray’s advice to her
supposed lover re his fancied disguise. She says,
tragically, with a descriptive hand-sweep : # * H>
won’t work; take it all off! ’ Her horrified
hearer (a perfect stranger) runs his eye over his
red beard, his blue serge, and his barred socks,
and moans, blushfully; ‘ Take it off ! Take it off !
She must be mad ! ’
“Lady Hopetoun has tried hard to gush and
frisk ever since Himself fell into poor health. It’s
pathetic to watch her struggles to thaw socially
and keep the big water-wheel of social duty going.
She gets on better when it’s only a matter of
humoring her convalescent and keeping him
cheerful. The naturally-sedate Countess was the
smiling, hopeful one of the Hopetoun pair when
Royal-visit worries were palpably setting hubby’s
teeth on edge, and even now, whenever they’re
together in public, she’s a model of wifely duty, so
heartily does she laugh at the good man’s jokes.
“Victorian professional musicians are out after
Ormond Prof. Peterson’s scalp. Marshall-Hall’s
successor is very canny and very Scotch, and
assiduously rakes in the bawbees outside his pro-
fessional radius. The prof, has sat in judgment
at Ballarat and Bendigo musical tugs-of-war for a
•consideration, and competed with the ruck in
other ways, and the un-official experts tear their
hair, and accuse him of poaching outside his pre-
serves, and making much more tough the lot
austere that waits on men of crotchets here. The
case against the Ormond monopolist seems fairly
convincing. Mr. Petersen receives close up to £BOO
a year as a hired man at a public institution, and
in doing musical odd jobs outside he appears to
be taking unfair advantage of the land. Where,
by-the-way, is the Melbourne Musicians’Union
that was established with such beating of trum-
pets and blare of drums a year or so ago ?
“Arthur Snowden has publicly contradicted
the report of his approaching marriage to the
widow of baritone Barker—the most popular
singer of his day. There is a bond of sympathy
between widow and widower—nothing more.
“ The day General Macdonald left Melbourne,
he was entertained at Parliament House. An old
lady was presented to him as a friend of
his infancy. At first, the Highlander was
incredulous, but when he heard the lady’s maiden
name, See., and found she had nursed him as a
baby, eye-witnesses testify that he literally cried.
Then, instead of going back to Government
House for his luggage, he asked Premier Peacock
to send for it, as he meant to spend every minute
left in the company of his dear old friend.
“When Mrs. Cornwall and York was here-
abouts she sent for an old Scotch body (a former
servant) from an inland town and yarned by
the hour. Then she summoned the meenister’s
wife from the same hamlet, and, in the course of a
long interview, made things pleasant for the old
darling.
“ Local Baptists mourn the departure of their
most popular preacher, a histrionic bachelor, who
habitually drew big Sunday-night crowds, thanks
to artistic ‘immersions,’ conducted in a ferny
fountain, ’mid rippling sweet musio and the flap-
ping of damp, white gowns. Lately his reverence
engaged himself to a young thing who (so much
the worse for herself and wooer), was not related
to any of the deacons. To them her suitor
officially confided his love-story.
“The elders, having other views for their
bright gospeller, refused their blessing, and
when the headstrong lover sent in his resignation,
accepted it with a snap. Then the congregation
took a hand, and flouted the tyrant deacons ; but
the parson—wise man !—means to ‘ take to the
wallaby-track,’ all the same. It’s always a dark
day in ‘ Amen Corner ’ when a bachelor shepherd
‘throws the handkerchief.’ Last time it hap-
pened hereabouts, patriarchs prayerfully ‘disci-
plined ’ the meenister, while ewe-lambs of the
flock flooded him with tear-blotted anonymous
letters. Poor Mr. What’s-his-name ganged his
ain gait, and, after a worried year or two, took
ill and ‘fled indignant to the Shades,’ leaving a
plucky girl-widow, who openly rejected the con-
dolences of her some-time opponents.
“A certain personage, who is just now under a
severe cloud, always seemed to be so brutally
frank that even a whisper against him comes as a
surprise. He was a church-reader. First time he
arrived to ‘ take the lessons ’ at a church his head
would be well thatched. Next Sunday he would
be bald, and after service he made a point of con-
sulting wardens as to whether they preferred him
with or without his wig. This child-like and
bland gospeller married a rich wife, and in
recognition of services rendered to the church by
the zealous bridegroom and by the bride’s moneyed
clan, a choral marriage service in the cathedral
was tendered to the happy pair.
“To a persevering penman on the hunt for
gossip, who worked his way through more
than one Cerberus-guarded door, till he arrived at
Minister Kingston’s, the cheery greeting came in
the well-known tones, ‘ Not to-day, Baker ! ’
“ The fact that Bendigo and Ballarat have both
again elected teetotal Mayors has highly pleased
the hotelkeepers whose houses are nearest to the
respective town-halls, because no whisky in the
mayoral cupboard means a continual stream of
thirsty souls to the liquor-shop. One Yic. cold-
water Mayor, who had the Gov.-General at his
‘ ball ’ not long ago, couldn’t make out how the
male guests became at times so scarce that the
ladies languished along the walls in droopful
rows. At last, however, somebody led his Wor-
ship to a window, and showed him the missing
ones popping in and out of the hotel across the
way. Said that Hopetoun enjoyed the joke im-
mensely. Yours affectionately, Akenehi.

Warner’s Safe Cure.
FEDERAL TARIFF PRICES.
The proprietors of Warner’s Safe Cure beg to
announce that, as they have a branch laboratory
in Australia, Warner’s Safe Remedies will not be
subject to the duty of 25 per cent,, which will
have to be paid on all proprietary medicines
imported into Australia.
The wholesale prices of Warner’s Safe Remedies
have been slightly advanced in New South Wales
so as to make prices uniform throughout Australia,
but this advance is so small that it will not be
necessary for the public to pay more than two
pence per bottle over and above the prices paid
before the Federal tariff came into force
Warner’s Safe Cure is the only emely known
to science which will unfailingly cine ail diseases
of the kidneys and liver. The following diseases
are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure, because they all
all arise from the retention in the system of
urinary and biliary poisons which it is the province
of the kidneys and liver to expel: Rheumatism,
indigestion, gout, neuralgia, biliousness, lumbago,
sick-headache, backache, sciatica, gravel, jaundice,
bladder troubles, blood disorders, anaemia, Bright’s
disease.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Tasmania has alive and working two ex-
Premiers over 80 years of age—Adye Douglas and
Reibey.
Premier Lewis (Tas.), will be 43 on
Monday next, and Judge Boucaut (S.A.), will be
60 on Tuesday.
Mr. Want, K.C., appears for Carrie Moore
in her breach-of-promise case, with Mr. Wise,
K.C. on the other side.
The death of Dick Speight, in Westralia,
recalls that in his halcyon Melbourne days he had
a pair of brisk bull-terriers named Brandy and
Soda. Do they still live ?
Tomlinson, the convicted White Cliffs
coach-robber, was a devoted reader of Deadwood
Dick literature. “ And the god that ye took from
a printed book be with ye, Tomlinson ! ”
Mr. Justice Cohen was “ noticed” by the
newspaper reporters at the Sydney amateur pugs’
set-to last week? Does His Honor hope to tread
the Madden way to the Chief Justiceship ?
S.A. has eight King’s counsel, and four of
them are immersed in Federal politics—Downer,
Symon, Kingston, and Baker. Gordon now has
practically a monopoly of the pick of the civil
cases, despite his heavy work as Attorney -General.
Ex-Mayor Tucker desires to run for
Adelaide mayoralty again in the Snow tramway-
scheme interest, if Aid. Wells (who favors muni-
cipalisation) will guarantee to keep the contest to
a duel between the pair of them. Election takes
place December 1.
Quite a number of new Federal civil
servants are arranging to marry while the glow of
their incomes still lasts. The salaries are not, as
a matter of fact, gorgeous, but to the man who
has been a junior at the N.S.W. Bar any settled
screw justifies wedded bliss.
Someone riled Premier See in N.S.W.
Assembly, t’other evening, and then a voice was
heard: “Ha! he’s bellicose to-night.” Which
remark was resented by the Premier (who is not a
very distinguished philologist) as an affront. The
point is a tender one with him. He takes a
Turkish-bath daily to keep it down.
BREECHES.
“ Good-bye, Lizzie! Take back these braces you
worked for me. I’m going to enlist in the
Melbourne Highlemders."
The funniest thing about General Hector
Macdonald’s Caledonian smoke-night in Mel-
bourne was the face of teetotal Jimmy Munro (of
Federal Bank fame), while someone was reciting
verses about a Scotch parson, who tried to make
his township sober by substituting dissolving-
views and lecturettes on the Holy Land for the
usual Bacchanalian New Year’s Eve. The Scotch-
men turned up, and when coffee was served, each
Scot calmly produced a flask and filled his cup
wi’ whusky. Old Jimmy’s countenance was a
study during the recitation.
G. H. Cox, M.L.C., was 78 on Sunday-
last He is one of seven sons of the late George
Cox, of Mulgoa, and was a member of the first
N.S.W. Parliament under Responsible Govern-
ment, in 1856 —Daniel Cooper being the only
other’member now living. Mr. Cox is the ‘ ‘Father ”
of N.S.W. Leg. Qouncil, having been appointed
by Charles Cowper in 1863.
One of the comedy treats of the first
Federal Session was G. H. Reid’s masterly acting
of a supposititious scene between Barton and
Chamberlain. The “No” Reid took the part of
Chamberlain, the “Yes” Reid that of Barton.
“No ” Reid sternly set forth what must be done.
“Yes” Reid gently murmured, “Yes, Mr.
Chamberlain.” If G. H. Reid had only less bulk,
what a delightful accession he would be to
Brough’s company !
“ X. 0.” writes :
That “ leading R.C. politician ” who denounces Cardinal
Moran for mischief-making is a bad judge. Every little
fight serves to rally the faithful, and makes them cling to
the building, or school or church, or other fund. Writer
thinks the Cardinal an able man, and admires the way he
keeps his flock together by giving them something to
fight the other side over. Let alone, they would pro-
bably grow luke-warm, or fight among themselves.
Foreign war, but peace at home, appears to be the
Moran motto.
The Bulletins asked to print an assurance
by Miss Annie Golding that the conversion of Long
Bill Lyne, John See, and the N.S.W. Protectionist
party generally, to womanhood suffrage, was
mainly due, not to Miss Rose Scott, but to Miss
Bella Golding, Miss Annie Golding, “and their
followers of the Newtown branch.” An assurance
like this gives gratifying proof of woman’s fitness
for the game of politics, the main rule of which is
to take care that no one else gets credit for any-
thing—from the Creation downwards.
Whilst old hands agree that the present
S.A. Assembly is the most verbose collection of
second-raters ever known in S.A. history, Wood,
M.P., himself a drivelsome local preacher, wants
Parliament to pass an Act enabling parsons to
occupy seats in the Legislature. Holder was a
local preacher, and could talk by the yard;
Foster, Works Commissioner, is an ex-parson
with a long tongue; and Caldwell, who chatters
until his fellow-members fall asleep, occasionally
thumps a pulpit.
“Pukaki” : Seddon seems to be losing his
head faster and faster. His last exploit is to pub-
lish a book, at public expense, re his tour in the
Islands. The book, it is understood, was first
headed “A Premier in search of Health, ” but that
title was dropped, at the cost, it is said, of sup-
pressing a first edition that was ready for issue.
The work consists largely of pictures of Dick in
sundry positions and sundry company. The rest
is chiefly extracts from goody-goody speeches made
by Seddon to various islanders.

ward which
makes him carry
such a load.
The Bulle-
tin begins to
understand the
mysteries of Eng-
lish Bits litera-
ture. A sausage
man (“ sausage and
mashed potato for two-
pence half-penny”) of the
Strand was claiming com-
pensation for the resump-
tion of his premises. He ex-
plained the magnitude of his
business, and went on to men-
tion that the rooms over his
shop were let at a considerable
profit. “Would he not be a
strange man who would live over
a sausage shop ?” Mr. E. Boyle,
K.C., wanted to know. “He
would have to have a very strong
stomach.” The sausage man
(“sausage and mashed, 2d.”)
demurred. “ Why,” he de-
clared, “Mr. Roberts, of Illus-
trated Bits, whose offices were
over my shop in Fleet-street,
used to ask me to leave the
door open to let the smell come
upstairs.” Picture this Temple of
Letters, and the savory fumes en-
circling the presiding deity, and the
Bits publications become more comprehen-
sible.
Among the “ distinguished ” visitors at
the tariff-debate was a Mr. W. A. M‘Arthur,
member for Mid-Cornwall in the Imperial Parlia-
ment, and one of the Liberal whips. Many
thought it was the M‘Million’s principal, come
out specially *to watch the wreck of his importing
business and deal with the demand of the firm’s
Sydney tailoressesfor the Victorian standard wage.
The spectacle of a thousand weary employees of the
late “ natural industries growing robustly in the
fresh air of Freetrade ” on strike to have their
wages levelled-up to those of the ricketty pro-
tected Victorian ditto would be a lovely com-
mentary on Dry-dog blatherskite.
The Montpelier Estate, Neutral Bay, Syd-
ney, now being subdivided, is historic through its
association with Henry Parkes. The old man
leased it as a large block from the Cooper family
for 99 years. He then sub-let to various people,
who built houses. Parkes had mortgaged his
lease to an insurance co., and, having failed to
pay his interest, the co. shut down on leaseholder
and sub-lessees. One of the latter, having lost
his house, used to follow Parkes to all his meet-
ings and make things very warm, “swindler”
being about the mildest term used.

The rage for self-advertisement afflicts
municipal bodies as much as individuals. When
General Hector Macdonald struck this effusive
continent the first telegram he received read :
Stawell Borough Council welcomes you to Australia.
The mighty Hector was gratified, but it took him
half-an-hour to find the place on the map after he
had exhausted the W.A. gazetteer.
Rev. Dr. White, who has been Presbyterian
minister at Singleton (N.S.W.), for 54 years, is
still mentally and physically a marvel. During
his long incumbency he has never missed a church,
funeral, or christening engagement, though in the
early days he had to travel scores of miles on
horseback or on foot, and at the time, too, when
bushrangers held the Upper Hunter district in
terror. When the old gentleman’s jubilee was
celebrated people smiled at his assurance that he
was looking forward to his next jubilee, but now
they’re beginning to think he’ll make his words
good.
“S.S.” : The Smiths are no longer in it in
Melbourne—the Macdonalds have swarmed all
over them. In a recent issue of the Age the name
Macdonald cropped up Id times, quite apart from
two cols, of oratorical gush about one Hector
Macdonald, a professional slayer from abroad.
Same day I was introduced to three Macdonalds,
listened to a speech by a Federal member, one-half
of whose name is Macdonald, and read portion of
an Australian book by Edgar Macdonald ; in the
evening I went to see “Tom, Dick and Harry,”
and found two Macdonalds figuring in the curtain-
raiser.
At present, an S.A. Act provides that a
Supreme Court Judge may retire on a pension of
£l3OO a year, if he has served 20 years and is
physically incapable of performing his duties.
Lawyer von Doussa has introduced a Bill to allow
any of the present judges to claim the pension on
either ground. Way and Boucaut have each
served 20 years, but are physically fit, while
Bundey, who is a confirmed invalid, has three
years to go ere he completes the requisite term of
service. No one seems quite to know whether
the Bill is meant to help Boucaut or Bundey to
pensioned ease.

Cabled that Boer Commandant Sclieepers
(who for some time has been said to be suffering
from that cruel trouble, appendicitis) has been
captured by one Vogan, of these parts. This will,
no doubt, be A. J. Yogan, a restless mortal better
known in the four corners of Australia than in
Melbourne or Sydney. He toured Papua, where
the natives nearly killed him. He lived
in N.Q., and wrote the “ Black Police,”
a novel, to show up the horrors of
the treatment of the Queensland aborigines.
He spent some time on W.A. gold-fields, where
he acquired much fever and but little gold. He
was one of the first to volunteer for service in the
Transvaal, and once got so far as being made a
sergeant in the Imperial Bushmen’s Contingent,
but finally departed as a free-lance.
Kingston is nothing if not mysterious.
From the inception of the Commonwealth until
the week before last he had not visited Adelaide,
and then he disembarked at a Hills station, dis-
appeared for two days, and was only seen again
when the time came to return. As the tariff dis-
cussion was then in full blast, the incident pro-
vided a fine opportunity for political gossips.
Maoriland Radical, George Fisher,
is aggressive, physically as well as oratori-
eally. He has a family of gigantic sons,
and in the old days, after a tirade against
the squatter Tories, would get into the
backyard and thump at his eldest son, Bill,
with the gloves. The thud of Fisher’s right-
handers could be heard 100yds. off. Occa-
sionally young Bill would get one home on
the old man, and the rally that followed
gave sounds worthy of the Golden Gate.
In those days, “Digger Dick” and “Our
George ” were united by common sympathy
—squatter contempt. Now they have drifted
apart. Fisher is still the friend of the wharf-
laborer, but Seddon is now the churn of
Chamberlain.
The most irascible member of N.S.W.
Leg. Council is non-practising barrister H. C.
Dangar, who shears many sheep and no clients.
Dangar, short, broad-shouldered, stiff-necked,
firm-lipped, keen-eyed, florid, strong-jawed, grey-
haired, sits back on the Opposition bench and
scowls at the Labor nominees as though they were
so many rascally selectors on the Dangar runs.
Toryism is with him a superstition. In private
and commercial life, squatter Dangar is most
genial and generous, arid rare good company
Temperance-Lecturer Woolley (who .*-as
once State Attorney at Minneapolis) told a gather-
ing of Christian Endeavorers at Adelaide Town
Hall, this month, that he hoped “the hand that
was held up at a Christian Endeavor meeting
would never be stretched out to shake that of a
publican.” Fortunately, Mayor Ware was not in
the chair, for he both brews and sells beer.
Marshall-Hall, when at Melb. University,
used to give much of his spare time to answering
communications from budding composers, asking
for musical advice. On one occasion, he received
a question, by letter, from a country church
organist, inquiring in which of two ways it would
be better to place a certain musical passage. His
answer was on the back of a post-card, terse and
to the point. It simply stated —
It does not matter a damn. —Marshall-Ham.
This so shocked the coming Mozart that lie for-
warded the post-card to the University Senate,
with a letter of complaint. That august body sat
in solemn conclave on the offending document,
and decided to write to the Prof, asking for a
report on the matter, which was accordingly done.
The answer was prompt and as follows :
The Chancellor and Senate of the University of Melbourne.
Sirs —I have the honor to acknowledge your communi-
cation of , asking for report on post-card, forwarded
to you, and. in reply, beg to state that it really didn’t
matter a damn. —I have the honor to be, sirs, your
obedient servant, G. W. Marshall-Hall.
From an English paper’s account of the
obsequies of the Empress Frederick : —
After the recitation of the “ De Profundis ” in German,
there was a pause. “The Princes all stood silent, tli3
veterans stood breathless in their sombre rows. At ti.e
coffin’s foot the Kaiser stood silent too. Among the many
lights and shadows, his rigid body loomed greater than
it had ever seemed before. He had lowered his swoid
across his breast till the point rested in his left hand.
His chin was lifted up and his eyes were closed. A light
fell upon a young but most proud and royal face. He
might have been some knight of old taking his oath of
chivalry. Every eye in the little church was fixed upon
his face. All was so still ; the living were as silent as the
dead. Thus he stood for some minutes. Then his head
bent slowly forward till his chin fell upon his breast, and
his shoulders hunched as though from a heavy blow. He
forgot his kingdom in his grief. Down upon one knee he
dropped, without any noise, and, seeming to kiss the shell
that hid his dead mother, buried his face among the
silken draperies, resting his head upon his hand. There
was a little rustle among the Princes, and each man
looked at the next. Then the Crown Prince, who stood a
little apart from the rest, seeing how his father knelt,
kneeled down too, and all the Princes behind him like-
wise fell upon their knees. For full three minutes, that
seemed like three full years, the Kaiser knelt, pressing
his face closer and yet more close into the coffin’s side.
He rose up, no longer a son heavy with grief, but an
Emperor, and without a single word, and without a back-
ward glance, walked proudly down the nave and out of
the church.”
But, for all that, it is notorious that the Emperor
was not over-fond of his mother, and he abhorred
the morganatic marriage which she made after the
death of his gallant and hand’some father, who
personally had the goodwill of all the world—eve>»
of the French nation.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —In the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
AN AEOLIAN HARP.
CHAP WITH LONG HAIR : “ Good day, sir.”
COOKY : “ O’day.”
C.W.L.H.: “ I’m a piano-tuner, and would like
to know if you have a piano that needs

“Trotter” : Recently, in Mackay (Q.)
district, I was at a farm worked by the father,
three daughters (18, 10, and 14), and a son (12).
The girls (barefooted) cut, trashed, and loaded
200 tons of cane. The same trio have cut,
trashed, and loaded 21 tons of cane in six days.
On another farm, I saw a boy, a girl, and a
Kanaka cutting, trashing and loading while the
old man drove the dray and stacked the cane.
These three loaded a dray in 45mins. Quite
common in the district to see women and girls and
children working in the cane-field.
“Alpha”: Near Pambula (N.S.W.), re
cently, a cow dropped a calf, but no milk was
forthcoming until six weeks la ter, when calf No.
2 appeared on the scene. Can be vouched for by
local residents.

Cootamundra (N.S.W.) has pro-
duced a pony which, at 12 months old,
stands only 2tin. high, and weighs 1071 b.
The Kanaka has, at least, one weakness
in common with his white brother—he loves a
widow. A week after the death of “Johnny,”
at North Rockhampton, there were nine candi-
dates for the hand of the much-married and
often-widowed Mrs. Johnny, with her 10 “ pledges
of affection.” The suitors ranged in age from 19
to 40—the lady herself being a grandmother of six
months’ standing. The “boy” who got her is
about 25, and a sailor, and the couple were married
by a C. of 15. clergyman, the eight rejected
swains providing the marriage-feast.
* * * *
Remarked with pride by prominent Q.
alien-coddlers that the physique of the S.S.
Island “boys” improves greatly after a few
years’ residence in Q. In proof of this they com-
pare the build of the older and more experienced
Q. boys with that of the new arrivals. There is
an undeniable superiority on the part of the
former, but the reason is plain to anyone who
understands the conditions of the traffic. Half of
the new arrivals are ill-grown, weedy youths of
whom 30 to 40 per cent, die off during the first
few years of plantation toil. The survivors are
necessarily men of superior stamina, but there is
no denying the fact that the average apparent
gain in physique is largely accounted for by the
planting of most of the Polynesian “weeds” in
the quiet plantation cemetery.
♦ • • *
The report of N.S. W. Western Lands
Commission states that in the last ten years the
number of sheep in the districts inspected has
fallen from 15,400,000 to 5,704,000—a decrease of
nearly two-thirds.
* * * *
“ Shaker ” : Despite the enormous loss of
stock, the late drought in Queensland has not
proved scch an overwhelming disaster to the
pastoral industry as has been represented—chiefly
for political purposes. Prices have so risen that
the total capital value of the stock now depastured
in the State is probably greater than it was before
the drought set in, and the big cattle-properties
around the Gulf, which were not seriously affected,
are simply coining money. Also, there is every
prospect of the pastoralists now obtaining that ex-
tension of their leases, which they have been
demanding, on various pretexts, for years.
• * * »
“ Bilby ” : A farmer in Avoca district
(Vic.), was carting a load of wheat when a bag
from the top fell off. He was in a place where no
assistance could be got, so, putting all his strength
into the job, he got the bag back into position,
though it weighed 2401bs. Would like to know if
this feat has ever been equalled—barring by pro-
fessional “strong men.” The same man, during
harvest time, works from 4 a.m. to 8.30 p.m., and,
when Saturday night arrives, goes to bed and
sleeps right on through till Monday, only waking
up to eat. And this in the hottest season of the
year, in a one-storey house with an iron roof !
Here’s a solid fact: A white
fantail of mine, having got some distance
from the house one day, was attacked by a
hawk, and, just managing to escape the
first swoop, started for home at the
“double.” There was a-strong,jvvind blow-
ing towards them from the house, and the
hawk was faster than the pigeon while
travelling against it, but going with it
latter’s greater spread of wing helped him
wonderfully. The pigeon evidently per-
ceived his own advantage, for, when over-
hauled on the home run, he would turn
sharply and make a short run with the wind,
then double again towards home. By
repeating this dodge, the quarry gained so
much that I believe it would have escaped
—even if I hadn’t chipped in with a gun.

“Billycan” : To Yril(B. 8/6/’01), re
marching power of men. My mate and I
“humped the drum ” from Esperance Bay
to Kalgoorlie—over 250 miles in 10 days.
In January, too (’95), and over the most
God-forsaken track in W.A. We had no
waggon, but had to hump our tucker and
water ourselves —when we were lucky enough
to have any.
Dear Bulletin. —Let me in turn contra-
dict “Dan Creed’s” contradiction of “Gnome’s”
statement re periodical “explosion” in White
Rock mine. The “ boom ” actually occurs as
described every day when the sun is shining; and
here is the explanation: A square galvanized
(plain, not corrugated) iron-tank, used when the
mine was working for baling purposes, stands on
the top brace, and the sun playing on it about 1
o’clock p.m., causes an expansion, which results
in the mysterious “ boom.” Probably the sun is
also responsible for the expansion of the “lode,”
since the mine shut down in Sept., ’9B. —
Exponent.
* * * *
At Morchard, near Orroroo (S.A.), half-a-
dozen men were in a little parlor playing euchre,
when a shot smashed the window and caused a
stampede. Investigations next day showed that
this was the work of a somnambulist who after-
wards woke up on top of Coomooroo Hill six miles
distant. He had previously walked a couple of
miles into Morchard, and had in his sleep negoti-
ated all kinds of fences.

In a recent horse-stealing case at Lawler’s
(W.A.) the prosecutor swore to a bell-strap found
amongst accused’s belongings as being one which
he had placed round the horse’s neck the previous
day. Questioned by the Bench, he said that he
had taken it from olf the neck of a dead camel a
few days previously, and that he could identify it
by the smell. The Bench took a sniff, and testified,
by the twist of its features, that the evidence was
strong.

A caution to squatters who lay phosphor-
ised pollard on travelling-stock-routes and public

“Louis M.” : Re immersion of dog in
water as cure for snake-bite (“W.J.8.,” 8.,
6/15/’01). Don’t know about snake-bite, but have
seen a strychnine-poisoned dog thus saved : —■
Secure the animal’s muzzle with strap and hand,
to prevent swallowing of water, and keep him
under till he ceases to struggle. Then promptly
lay him out, with head well down-lull, and
“pump.” I should prefer to use an emetic
(tobacco or salt), but I regard the “immersion
cure” as a reasonable one if adopted in time.
The lockup-keeper of
Fitzroy (Melb.) watch-house
was absent on leave when a
drunk was run-in one night,
accompanied by a dog. There
was trouble in separating the
pair, so they were both shoved
into the cell. The arresting
constable then, according to
rule, went for a superior
officer to inspect his charge.
He returned with a senior-
constable, and they entered
the cell in the dark. Where-
upon the dog attempted to
sneak out, and brushed against
the leg of the senior, who,
thinking it was the prisoner
trying to escape, jammed the
door shut, forgetting that the
key was on the outside. The
traps struck matches and
yelled for help, while the dog
yapped, and a female derelict
in the next cell warbled “Wait
till the clouds roll by.” Then they
tried to lift the comatose drunk from
his bunk to make sitting room, but the dog
resented this interference and sampled the
senior’s leg. Several times they heard the
footsteps of a visiting bobby in the corridor,
and hailed vigorously—only to be told, “Make
less noise, ye dhrunken divils, or I’ll turn
the hose on yez ! ” At last, after eight solid
hours, a bobby, arriving with another arrest, per-
ceived the key in the door and let them out.

“ Les. O.” : Re snake-bitten dog and
water-cure (B. 15/6/’01). Isaac Ferris, of Smith-
field, saw a black snake bite his terrier on the
shoulder, and the dog at once lay down in Pros-
pect Creek, staying there all the afternoon with
only his head above water. After three days’
sickness he recovered.

Queensland Parliament is being moved to
destroy the prickly-pear on Crown lands. In
America they are coping with the pest _by
perfecting methods of extracting the spines
from the pear and using it for-fodder. If pear
can be as easily prepared for fodder as scrub it
would be a fine stand-by for drought seasons.

“ Wallaroo ”: Once knew a bronzed,
heavy-booted female who ran her farm solus, while
hubby—one of the “ born-tired ” genus—did the
pot-jostling. She used to hand-reap her wheat,
thrash with a flail, and clean by washing in creek.
In between crops, she took the old man tin-minmg,
but never let him near the sluice-box. Said “ the
old cove lost half the tin.”

N.W. squatters ere the fence can be started. tie
was recently caugbt 80 miles N.E. of Kurnalpi.

Someone has been “ pulling the leg of
the author of “Theßedemption of David Corson,
which deals thus with Australia’s bunny :
In Australia, when drought drives the rabbits south-
wards, tile ranchmen, terrified at their approach, bar e
only to erect a woven-vvire fence on the north side of their

against it, and die in droves, too stupid to go round,
climb over, or dig under.
Poor tired bunny ! Unfortunately this broken-
down type of rodent is not frequent.

“ N.D.VV.”: The breeding-rate of the
rabbit in Maranoa district (S.Q.) was under six
per litter, but since the change in the season and
the abundant growth of herbage, the rate n;is
much increased. One of the destruction-staff
mentions having found a burrow with 11 young
in it ; then trapping the doe in the burrow and
taking from her eight young to which she was on
the point of giving birth. Also, an inspector
states that he took 42 young from four does, a,nd
it is authentically reported from a Border-station
that 16 belly-young were taken from one doe. As
far as I know this is a record.
, * * *
John Egan, miner, late of Beech worthy
Vic., was a quaint suicide. He had been “bulling ”
some old workings at Silver Creek, and awoke the
other morning to the fact that he was tired of life.
Going below he took with him a blanket and a
tin of charcoal, established himself at the face in a
dry drive, pegged his blanket across the drive,
making the space in which he remained as air-tight
as possible, then lit the charcoal. After which he
lit his pipe, settled him down to a steady view of
his prospects in the near future, and so died. He
was found sitting against the side of the drive,
with his old familiar pipe still between his teeth,
and £lB in his pockets. Egan is described as “ a
great reader,” and was known locally as “The
Philospher.” The Philosopher’s stone is now
visible in Beech worth graveyard.

At Ballarat Oval, t'other day, during a
festival, James Morrissey was found flourishing
poisonous black snakes. He had four of the rep-
tiles, and was arrested, presumably, for being
drunk when in charge of a bunch of serpents. In
court, he declared that he had discovered an anti-
dote for snake-bite, and was permitting the rep-
tiles to bite him in order to advertise his cure and
establish confidence. He had allowed one snake
to strike home, and had been pretty bad in his
cell, but had pulled through all right. James
pleaded that his “drunk” was merely to assist
the antidote ; he was drunk on scientific prin-
ciples, and for the public good. But he was filled
ss. all the same, just as if the spirit of scientific
investigation was the same as an} 7 other tangle-
foot.

North Island Maoris are sad at heart over
the recent deaths of five notable natives. All
were recipients of medals from the Duke during
the Rotorua visit, and all sickened shortly after
his departure. Those medals were as deadly as
K.O.M.G.- ships.
THE SWAGMAN'S FAREWELL TO MATILDA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The swagman sat upon a log beside a waterhole
And eyed Matilda on the ground with anguish in his soul.
He drew his hand across his eyes as far across the plain
Came wafted down the forest aisles the whistle of a train.
Again he looked to where she lay insensate on the clay,
While slow the lengthened shadows barred the even of
the day.
He saw how shrunken was her form, how wrinkled, old,
and worn,
And felt a tugging at his heart as if its strings were torn.
He tried to speak and say “ Good-bye ” as darkness round
him fell ;
And but the night-winds heard him moan his husky-
voiced farewell.
“ Farewell, Matilda ! you have been my partner all these
years
Upon life’s journey through the West, with all its smiles
and tears ;
Together in the time of drought we faced the blighted
plains;
Together fought the roaring floods of stern, submerging
rains;
In unity the track we sped till at our resting-place
I found a solace from the world within your warm
embrace.
I fondly dreamed that ever thus ’twould be, till at the
last
Together we’d be found asleep when life for us was past.
But now, alas ! the times are changed, and this can never
be,
For where your form was welcome once you now meet
obloquy.
“ The shrieking train with thunder roar whirls through
the solitude,
While bikes fly like the wind along each line of latitude.
And now that auto-cars have come —a vision flashing by—
Soon flying ships on ether’s sea will speed across the sky 1
Marconi’s wireless telegraph will call from the bureau
Electric engineers—to shear, to reap, to plough or mow.
And he who would be in the van must lay Matilda down,
And with a neat rosella pack dwell in a postal town.”
The swagman paused and bared his head beneath the
darkened sky ;
Then raised Matilda in his arms and breathed a last good-
bye.
The brand of Cain was on his brow, but anguish in his
soul,
As, with a frenzied cry, he cast her in the waterhole.
A startled night-bird gave a scream and flapped across
his sight—
“ Matilda’s ghost!” he gasped, and fled, a madman,
through the night.
Now, when the summer drought came round and all the
holes had dried,
They found in one a nomad’s bones—Matilda by their
side.
The jury they empanelled there to solve the mystery
Have not upon a verdict yet been able to agree.
Although unanimous that one had thrown the other in—
Which was the one ? —God only knows, who knows the
Swagman’s Sin.
JACY HILL.

Harbinger of spring. Droning herald of
the warmer days to come. It sails gaily in at the
open window, bumps against the walls, buzzes
along the ceiling, circles the room a score of times,
finds the kitchen with unerring instinct, makes
several tours round it, and, finally, settles on the
meat for the family dinner —with ghastly conse-
quences. That is the common —the all-too-com-
mon—blow-fly. The best way to outwit it is put
your meat where it can’t be reached, i.e., in a
meat-safe. Plenty of air and no admission for
flies. Hanging meat-safes, from 12in., 4s. 9d. to
18in., Bs. 6d., 20in. 11s. 6d., at McLean Bros,
and Rigg, Ltd., 109 Elizabeth-street, Mel-
bourne*
Simpson’s factory, at Newtown, Sydney,
has been going for the past ten years, sending
vehicles all over Australia, Tas., N.Z., Fiji,
Nor fold Island, India, and California. Up to a
few days ago the firm was paying from 20 per
cent, to 110 per cent, to get its sulkies into
Australian States but now ! The firm expects
to do great business with the “ Patent Jump-Seat
Sulky,” a splendidly comfortable sulky which
does away with all “ horse and cart ” motion.
Another special vehicle is the “ Gilgai,” a cross
between a buck-board and a buggy, combining
the usefulness of the one and the comfort of the
other. Two ordinary men can easily carry a
“Gilgai,” but the vehicle itself will carry a half-
ton.*

“ Werrimee ” : The skippers of the little
Richmond River (N.S.W.) steamers have much to
put up with. Perhaps the boat is late and hurry-
ing to get to the wharf before night, when an old
woman stops her to say “ What’s the time ? Our
clock’s stopped.” A “ flag” (father’s old shirt or
mother’s apron) brings the vessel in to another
wharf, and a boy accosts the captain : “Please,
father says will yer ask Mr. Dougherty for ’is
corncracker, an’ fetch it with yer? ’’ At another
place a cargo of a dozen eggs or a bit of fresh pork
has to be taken in and landed further down, or
perhaps it’s a note to the storekeeper to send up a
pound of tobacco. At election-times there’s some-
body waiting at nearly every cookie-wharf to
know “who got in,” and it is common to see
kerosene-tins hung over the water on the end of
clothes-props. These are boat-hooked on board,
taken along, and brought back full of treacle. No
message required.

“P.X.”: Found alongside a track out
here (far west N.S.W.) lately, a wedge-tailed
eagle in a tight place. Apparently when swoop-
ing at prey he had got himself tangled in a fence,
and a projecting wire-end had hooked him
through the wing-sinews. He had been hanging
helpless for, I should judge, a couple of days, but
still showed plenty of -fight. Was 7ft. from tip to
tip. Have seen ’roos and emus caught in the
wires, but never before one of the hawks.

A huge landslip, lately destroyed the
beautiful Pohokura pah, at the mouth of the
Urenui river—one of the most charming bits of
scenery in Taranaki (M.L.). With a great roar
the hill broke away and slipped into the river,
sending large boulders and quantities of fish into
the paddocks on the other side.

A BLACK PAGE FROM THE NEVER NEVER.—
“AND HER HUSBAND AWAY DROVING!"
The Indian press is raising a howl against the
Federal Immigration Restriction Bill. First, the
howl declares that the legislation is unnecessary, as
only 41 Asiatics left India for Australia during
the last three months. Which argument kills itself,
for if Hindoos are not emigrating to Australia an
Exclusion-Bill will do them no injustice. Secondly,
it goes on to say that the Hindoo does not interfere
in the labor market, as he goes in for peddling
and domestic’ service, and marrying into the
families of Australian pro-black-labor legislators.
Which is partly true. The bye-ways of Victoria,
in particular, are broivn with the multitude of Ram
Chunder, the peddler, and it is hardly safe in bush
districts for any man to leave his home, because of
the way Ram curses his female belongings ivhen they
ivon’t buy his trash.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on lIS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
P.W.C.: Social science would be easy to master
if human nature and the complex processes of human
existence would only conform to the apparently axiomatic
theories of the political economist. The trouble is they
don’t. Theoretically’, if the f.o.b. value of an article is
Is. and a new duty of 3d. is put upon it, the retail price
should go up at least threepence. Practically, however,
it often doesn’t. The foreign producer of the article, in
many cases, recognises that if the consumer is called
upon to pay’ all the duty, the effect will be to force the
article out of use ; so, to keep his market, he abandons a
share of his profit and thus pays part of the duty....

The great success of the distribution of the
first edition of the “Library of Famous Litera-
ture,” edited by Dr. Richard Garnett, C. 8.,
will, no doubt, be repeated in Australia. This
work was first offered to the public by. The
Standard, in London, and over 10,000 copies
were subscribed for in a few months. With Ur.
Garnett were as ociated M. Leon Vallee, of the
National Library, Paris ; Dr. Alois Brandi, of the
Imperial University, Berlin ; and Mr. Donald
G. Mitchell, of America. To the first volume
is prefaced a fine faesmile from a manuscript of
Demosthenes. Among its illustrations are
portraits of ancient and modern personages and
scenes. The master-pieces, including translations,
are from Greek, Indian, Latin, Egyptian, and
other literatures, and include compositions by
modern writers and bearing on ancient themes.
Dr. Garnett himself writes an introduction on
“ The Use and Value of Anthologies.” Among
the modern authors, from whom selections are
taken, are Tennyson, Matthew Arnold, George
Smith, Charles Mackay, George Borrow, Professor
Jebb, Keats, Landor, Hawthorne, and Ruskin.
“On first looking into Chapman’s Homer,” is
selected as an example of Keats’ self-identification
with the classical spirit. “ Achilles and Helena,”
is an example from Landor. Mr. Ruskin’s
“ Greek Myths ”is also printed. The illustrations
are admirable, and the work is issued in the
highest style of the book-maker’s art, the paper,
printing, and binding being of the very best.
Announcement is m.-ide in our columns to-day
that a supply is in hand in Sydney, and that for
a short time immediate delivery of the work will
be made to prompt applicants.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A Federal Labor Senator to The Bulletin
on the censure debate : “I do not think the Govt,
need fear. I hope not, anyway. We don’t want
to give the pro Kanakas any more time to
organise. ”
The Government, by keeping strictly
silent while G. H. Reid was on his feet, quite
spoilt his want-of-confidence speeoh. And Reid
Tuliy admits it. To the Opposition leader hostile
interjections are as the breath of life.
Queensland Govt, has at last been goaded
into attempting the local suppression of John
Norton. Last week Minister Foxton introduced
a Bill to “ deal with ” Sunday papers—to prevent
such papers from being printed or sold.
Second reading of the Cloncurry syndicate
railway-grab Bill passed Queensland Assembly by
33 votes to 24 ;17 members were absent They
may wish to take credit for not having voted for
the Bill. The public will draw its own conclu-
sions.
One member of the Maoriland Federal
Commission makes no secret of the fact that his
opposition to joining the Australasian Common-
wealth was due to a fear that the new nation
would “cut the painter” shortly. Maoriland’s
present rulers are very anxious about that painter,
and even discourage “Natives’” associations lest
they should be seed-beds of anti-imperialism.
Queensland Government uses all its honey
on the Kanaka laborer; the white worker has none.
As a means of retrenchment, the Philp-Leahy
crowd proposes to discharge 25 per cent, of the
fettlers on the railways of the southern and
western districts. The step will seriously increase
the risks of railway-travelling, and throw many
deserving men out of work.
THE BLACK-DRAUGHT.
“Yet the House has declared against any subsidy being given to steamers
which employ colored labor even to the smallest extent. The Orient line is liable
to be cut off, for instance, because of certain colored stokers. What is most to be
deprecated is the idea of a collison with the British Govt, on this subject. It is
the British Govt, which enters into these postal contracts—because it has the
largest interest in them —and the British Govt, has repeatedly expressed its great
unwillingness to declare that the Hindoo subjects of the King are absolutely de-
barred from suitable employment in H.M.’s service. It does not wish them dis-
criminated against.”—ARGUS.
MEDICINE-MAN: “Here, take this!”
AUSTRALIA: “ Wha’ for? What price your doctoring, old man? You
gave India the Civil Service, and cooked him. You made China
take your opium, and cooked him. You’ve tried to doctor the
Boer; and now you leant me to take the nigger!”
George Turner dealt a home thrust at the
Opposition during the Federal tariff debate when
he quoted the calculations of Nash (Sydney D.T.),
the foreign-traders’ journalistic joss in N.S.W., to
show that a Reid tariff would have to be on almost
identical lines with the Turner-Kingston tariff so
far as the burden on the people was concerned.
The logic of the Q. Kanakapßile: “Twenty-
five years of experience has conclusively shown
that it is impossible to grow sugar without black-
labor.” Also, “Give us a few years more grace and
we will be able to do without the Kanaka.” They
have been blowing hot and cold for 25 years, and
if time is given them the infants of to-day, grown
old and gray, will hear the time-worn cry “A
few more years ! ”
If the Philp crowd is so sure that the
State is with it on the Kanaka question [vide
Philp’s public utterances) why doesn’t it go to
the country on the one question—Black or White?
Or, better still, why doesn’t it take a Referendum
on the Kanaka, and overwhelm the Federal
Parliament with the result ? Philp, when told
this, suddenly grows hard of hearing, and goes
on with a Syndicate Railway Bill.
Sydney Sunday Times cries bitterly
because England continues to buy butter, and
mules, and canned pork from countries where
pro-Boers abound, instead of sending all orders to
loyal Australia. Behold the working creed of the
Australian Jingo : to send his brother to the war
in return for a canned-goods order. So much (of
somebody else’s) blood poured out—so much
butter-money poured into the cash-box.
Now that the unspeakable Kitchener has
taken to flogging and hanging Boer prisoners, the
Transvaalers must begin to regret their misplaced
leniency to Dr. Jameson and his brigands of a
few years ago. Their lives were forfeited by law,
yet they were neither hanged, flogged, fined, nor
even imprisoned for any length of time—in fact,
though their crime ranked as murder, they were
treated as gently as if it had been a plain drunk.
The Senate’s present calm acceptance of
second place in the Federal legislature—as House
of review—is only on the surface. Against the
motion to adjourn over the no-confidence debate
several senators protested, while Higgs urged
that the Senate had as much right to censure the
Government as the Representatives, and that its
censure should have an equal effect. Some day,
perhaps, on this very point there will be a willing
fight between the two Houses,
N.S.W. freetraders taunt the Victorians
in the Federal House of Reps, with “blindly
following ” the Barton Government, and with the
next breath roar out that Barton studies Victoria
and Victoria alone. Thus, the Vic. member is
frightened off the Opposition benches: he would
have to credit his constituents with a very poor
estimate of the veracity of the Reidites to dare to
vote against a Government which is represented
as loving Victoria so exclusively.
Launceston Examiner, dishes up again a
good old Foreign-trade argument—that instead of
protecting our little tobacco industry it would be
cheaper to abolish it, pension off the employees,
and, by importing all our tobacco, get so much
more revenue in import duties. But, if so, why
not go a step further: abolish all industries, pen-
sion off everybody, import everything, and put a
10 per cent, duty on everything, pay the pensions
out of part of the huge customs revenue that
would ensue, and run the country with the
balance? When the chief end of a country is to
have lots of imports, the folly of producing any-
thing becomes apparent at once. Producing any-
thing helps to diminish imports.
“ Kiwi ” writes
from Maoriland :
The Farmers’ Union is
becoming a formidable
organisation in Maoriland.
Though professedly non-
political, its chief plank
seems to be uncompro-
mising opposition to
trades-unionism and labor
legislation, and it has
already begun clamoring
for the exemption of far-
mers from the operation
of the Workers’ Compensa-
tion Act. Meanwhile, the
organisation of labor is
proceeding apace, chiefly
to enable workers to take
advantage of the Concilia-
tion and Arbitration Act,
as, unless registered as a
union, they cannot come
under the law. There are
now about 30,000 labor
unionists in Maoriland,
and there is every likeli-
hood that at no distant
date there will be a politi-
cal struggle between them
and the rapidly develop-
ing forces of the new Tory-
ism as manifested in the
Farmers’Union. The first
tussle will begin if Premier
Seddon (who is placed in
an awkward fix between
the two parties), intro-
duces his promised Bill to
exempt landowners from
the Workers’ Compensa-
tion Act.
Much muddy mag-
niloquence has been
evoked by the Federal
flag business. The latest
from a Sydney news-
paper correspondent:
“ I therefore hope that
our Federal Parliament
will submit the whole of
the designs to the public,
and then to our father-
land, and let them say
what they think best for the new-born Empire’s future
flag.
By “ our father-land” the gentleman no doubt
means our Uncle-land. The Bulletin is in favor of
settling the flag controversy by adopting some
such chaste design as that of a rampant kangaroo
poking its tail into business which doesn’t concern
it, and being badly singed in consequence.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Crick recently
gave Dr. Ross some very specious reasons why
land was not thrown open in Walgett district,
Central Division, but ingeniously avoided stating
why several areas on Crown leases in Warren
district, which have been surveyed since July,
1901, and were then due for selection, were not
thrown open to the public. A certain, not very
long ago, predecessor to. Mr. Crick could give
ample information on that point.
The fate of the Womanhood Suffrage Bill
in N.S.W. Upper House was linked with that of
the Compulsory Arbitration Bill. If the old men
THE FRIENDS OF “ THE POOR.”
“ Mr. Reid explained that the necessaries of life were to be subject to
the tariff equally with luxuries , so that the tariff would piess very heavily
upon the poor.” Also , the Argus, the S.A. Register, and the importers:
“ The tariff will press heavily on the poor.”
had thrown out both measures Premier See
would have prorogued Parliament, and irrigated
the Council (swamping not necessary) with a
sprinkling of Radicals in favor of Womanhood
Suffrage and Compulsory Arbitration. The meek
acceptance of the Arbitration Bill has shelved the
female franchise question for a session—perhaps
for a Parliament.
London Times lets in light on Downing-
street dictation to Australia :
In the Post-office Bill it was proposed to forbid the
employment of Asiatics on board mail steamers. The
Secretary of State at once pointed out that this was ultra
vires, inasmuch as it violated all existing contracts, and
international law. The Government, therefore, dropped
it.
Now that Parliament has forced the Federal
Government to “ pick it up again,” what is
Chamberlain going to do ?
An attempt was lately made by Propsting,
M.H.R., to induce the Tasmanian Assembly to
repeal the Coupon Abolition Act of last session,
but Tasmania evidently knows when it is well off,
and Propsting only mustered seven votes.
Premier Jenkins, of 8.A,, now promises, in
response to a loud and general demand, to intro-
duce a Coupon Abolition Bill. The uprising
against the ingenious middleman, who lives by
coming between the man who gives discount and
the man who receives it, is becoming general.
The hoof of the syndicator is soon felt.
Stated in Q. Assembly that a Queensland railway
employee, who joined the Ohillagoe syndicate’s
service, was told to perform an unlawful action.
He refused, and was sacked. The Government
re-engaged him on the State railways, where-
upon the syndicate’s engineer wrote to the Q.
railway authorities, saying he “considered it a
very unfriendly act for the authorities to employ a
man his co. had sacked .” And the humble rail-
way department straightway sacked the man
again.
Said that one of the things which the
Premiers “ from dominions beyond the seas ” will
discuss during the coronation visit to London will
De Imperial trade-reciprocity on a basis of Pro-
tection. England has carried her Freetrade
burdens long enough, and well-informed poli-
ticians predict a tariff before the dissolution of
this British Parliament providing for reciprocity
with Australia, Canada, S’Africa, and India.
Since Barton knows no more about trade and
finance questions than a child, he ought to take
Kingston with him when he steps into Mr.
Chamberlain’s parlor.
Bruce Smith managed to work in a final
protest last week against the dreadful “education
test ” for undesirable immigrants. He was greatly
troubled lest a Government might start examining
Frenchmen in Italian or Russians in Spanish.
The poor man hasn't yet grasped the fact that no-
body proposes to examine any European immi-
grants, though the day may come when this
country will have to shut down on Russians or
Greeks or any other people. In fact, it may some
day be necessary to block all the Smiths, and then
the education test —in the Manx language—will
meet the case every time.
' During the second-reading debate of the
Industrial Arbitration Bill in N.S.W. Leg. Coun-
cil, Hawken, M.L.C., one of Reid’s awful crea-
tions, fired off this fine, vague jargon :
He wanted to know upon what basis the bill was built.
If they found that it was built upon a basis that had
caused a scourge and curse to the human race, then, as
an Australian community, they were bound to scout it.
In his opinion, it was built upon a socialism which bad
been a curse to the Eastern world, and it would result in
repression of trade, and prove inimical to the interests
not only of employers and employees, but the citizens
generally. That being so, he was bound to protest
against it in the interests of democracy and of true work-
men.
The gravamen of Premier Reid’s payment
of £350 to J. C. Neild was that the payment was
made without Parliamentary sanction. The
offence was certainly aggravated by Reid’s per-
sonal promise in the House that there would be
no payment. But the £46 Premier See paid to
reproduce copies of the likeness and proceedings
of R. Atkinson Price, M.P., was paid in direct
contradiction of an order of the Printing Com-
mittee, a body founded by Act of Parliament.
An example was, it was thought, made of Reid ;
but it would seem that the habit is inveterate in
N.S.W., and that several Ministries must be
wrecked before it will be broken.

to the N.S.W.

Poor old Senator Neddy Pulsford arises
to ask “ with all the earnestness I can com-
mand” what is to be done with all the
surplus cash that the State Treasurer of
N.S.W. will have when he gets the State’3
share of the proceeds of a huge and iniquitous
Federal tariff. He throws out a gentle suggestion
that half-a-million or so, more or less (a mere
trifle, of course) might be given to the Railway
Commissioners to reduce fares with. And, mean-
while, the “surplus” has been spent nearly twice
over already, and the Treasurer is getting grey-
headed wondering how he will make both ends
meet.
The Govt, cow kicked out vigorously at
those who tried to milk her over the Warburton
(Vic.) railway land resumptions. One gentleman
claimed £6OO for about naif an acre of agricul-
tural land. The trust offered him £75. He re-
fused ; went to arbitration, and was awarded the
£75, but has to pay all costs. Another asked for
£3OOO for acres; it took the arbitrators six days-
to settle his case ; awarded £260, and the bulk of
the costs to come out of that. A £2500 claim was-
settled for £5O ; and a man who claimed £364, and
refused £96, emerged from the arbitration with
£5O and all costs to pay. Out of a total of £9ooo'
claimed only £682 was awarded. In the good old
Gillies days, the Government hadn’t developed'
this quarrelsome habit. It was an affable body,
and just paid up.
When the Federal tariff rose on the
horizon Sydney Telegraph hustled round to
interview “leading citizens,” who advertise with
it, and collect opinions. Among others it col-
lected this one from W. H. Lever, the soap
monarch :
England is now the only freetrade country where we
have works. I see so many evils in a protectionist policy.
You begin to protect the manufacturers, and then you
have to protect the working-men. There are always
these difficulties in a protectionist country. Where our
working-men in England have gone to the United States,
they have never been tempted to remain. They always
want to come back. The wages may be a little higher,
but the man finds he is worse off. I believe that to-day
the'working-man in England is better off and happier
tiian is his fellow in protectionist countries, though his
wages may not seem so high.
Now, if they have “never been tempted to re-
main,” why on earth do they remain ? And if
they “always want to come back,” why don’t
they come ? The whole reason why there are now
more English-speaking people in the United
States than in Great Britain itself, is that that
emigrant Briton didn’t come back.
Reid’s sudden rush into holts with the
Barton Government over the tariff placed his
Sydney journalistic jackals at a considerable dis-
advantage. They had to rush things, and, of
course, overreached themselves and fell in. After
the first Saturday ■ under the tarift the alleged
country correspondent began to yelp like this
in S.M. Herald :
FEELING IN WINDSOR.
Windsor, Tuesday.
The feeling against the Federal tariff is very strong in
this district, and the duties came as a shook to the
working classes. The increased price on the commodities-
of life which have been charged by the storekeepers have
caused the cost of living to be fully 30 per cent, higher
than previous to the imposition of the duties.
The farmers, who in general were ardent protectionists,
cannot understand how they have not benefited, as their
produce, instead of increasing in price and giving them a
better return, has become cheaper, while their imple-
ments and the cost of living has greatly increased.
Feeling is very strong among them against the present
system of taxation, and will be the means of turning a lot
against Protection in favor of Freetrade.
The beginningand the enddon’thangtogether. The
commodities of life have become fully 3i) per cent,
dearer, and yet produce is cheaper. Presumably
in the Herald office 11 >ey don’t regard “produce”
such as grain, vegetables, meat, potatoes, butter,
milk, eggs, etc., etc., as commodities of life. The
Freetrade paper is always trying to prove that
Protection is bad because it makes prices go up
and also bad because it makes prices go down, and
this struggle to be in two places at once makes
it look ridiculous.
