VITADATIO
THE GREAT BLOOD HERBAL
REMEDY.
GRAVEL
CURED AFTER TREATMENT BY SEVEN
MELBOURNE DOCTORS.
9 Barkly Avenue, Malvern,
Victoria, Dec. 24, 1900
Mr. S. A. Palmer,
Dear Sir, —I consider it my bounden duty to let
Buffering humanity know what Webber’s VITADATIO
has done for me. For the last 10 years I have been a
terrible sufferer with that agonising- disease, gravel, and
of late years I have gone through almost unbearable
agony, which reduced me to a mere shadow. I was
completely helpless at times with weakness, and having
little or no sleep, I could do nothing for myself. I was
treated by (7) Melbourne doctors, and they all told me
that an operation was the only means of relief, they could
do nothing else for me. I kept lingering on, afraid of an
operation. At that time I was living at 44 Wilson-street,
South Yarra, and well-known. A lady living in the same
street sent me a message and advised me to try
VITADATIO, saying it had done wonders for her mother-
in-law, who suffered from gall stones, and that it would
cure me. I told my husband, and he advised me to get a
bottle at once. I did so, and after taking the third
bottle the disease began to come away like pieces of chalk,
and continued to come away for about two months. By
that time the pains had completely left me. I continued
taking VITADATIO until I was quite strong and well, and
it js now eighteen months since I took the last bottle, and
I have had no return of the old trouble since. I have
lived at the above address for the last twelve months, and
both m3' husband and myself have told everybody we
know about VITADATIO and my complaint. I am
thankful I have avoided a dangerous operation, and am
now in good health. I Shall be glad to give any further
information to anyone calling on me at my above
address.—Yours gratefully,
(Signed) JANE VINCENT SPENCER.
27/12/1900.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
184: Pitt Street, Sydney.

BMYTH & NICOLLE, 14 Hanter-#t., Sydney.
Newspaper envoys from all the States are
going to Melbourne in a body, to prove to the
Federal members and the Federal Ministry
that, in the tangled words of S.A. Regis-
ter, “in the interests of true democracy
and popular education, it is to be greatly
regretted to note the introduction of 10 per
cent, duty on printing paper—the raw material
of the newspaper and publishing establishments.
This will weigh heavily upon the rural press and
journals devoted to special public objects.” Again
the Poor Man ! It is always the poor man. Of
course, the rich city papers don’t mind a bit. It
will cost some of them up to (say) £2500 or so a
year for paper-duty ; but, bless you, they never
give a thought to themselves !
Writes a Vic. pressman who looks down
on the public chaos from the gallery :
The Victorian Opposition, under the leadership of the
dismal Irvine, has gone to pieces. The members have not
held a meeting since the opening of the session. No
control is exercised in any way over the speakers. On
important debates sometimes one member of the Opposi-
tion leads off, and sometimes another. During the
Convention debate Shiels really led the Opposition. On
several occasions, M‘Kenzie, the blind member for
Anglesey, has taken the place of the leader, much to the
annoyance of the ex-Ministers on that side of the House.
One of the evil results arising from this state of affairs is
that nearly all the Ministers are taking a day off. They
do little or nothing in their departments, and less in
Cabinet. Especially is this the case with Trenwith, who
has turned out one of the laziest Ministers that Victoria
has ever had. There is no cohesion among Peacock’s team,
and in more than one important debate on liberal pro-
posals, brought forward by private members, Attorney-
General Gillott has been violently opposed to his
colleagues. Indeed, when young man Robinson
introduced his Bill to permit municipalities to levy rates
on land values only, Gillott led the Tories of the House
who attacked the Bill with great vigor. But then it is
said that Gillott is not anxious to retain his portfolio—in
fact, that he is trying to lose it, and has great hopes that
he will succeed ere long.

“ Here is a story that ‘ outs ’ ‘ Dr. Jekyll
and Mr. Hyde,’” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “ on the ‘ point,’ and on all points.
It birthed, grew, and died ’mid.gore and shocking
savagery, in squalid Kentish Town (North Lon-
don). Enter ‘Dr. Jekyll’—a draper’s assistant,
20 years of age, earning 20s. a week. Temperate,
well-behaved, one of the Y.M.C.A., and who had
been half his brief life in the same billet. Exit
‘Jekyll’; enter ‘Hyde’—a dashing youth who
passed himself off as a man of means, and ex-
plained his parents away as merely the caretakers
of his (Hyde’s) house. He drank, billiardised at
a local pub. nightly, and courted the inns’
daughter. The publican jibbed at marriage, for
‘ Hyde ’ drank too deeply and often. Eighteen
months passed—then came the hideous crime-
climax. On a recent evening, 1 Hyde ’ took his
18-year-old sweetheart out to supper at his mythi-
cal sister’s mythical house. Next morning the
father of .Tekyll-Hyde burst his sitting-room door
to see a shambles. The girl’s clothing had been
torn off her with such inhuman violence that only
the sleeves of her bodice,' her necklace, and her
gloves were left on the otherwise naked form.
Her mouth gagged with her own linen, twenty-six
distinct wounds on her head, and a blood-bathed
hammer completed this tragedy ‘ not needing
words’ ! Medical evidence witnessed to six
separate fractures of the skull, nine smashes on
the forehead after death, and that the gagging
was done after absolute insensibility, when the
murderer had committed a still fouler crime. He
lay dead by a bowl of oxalic acid. An intelli-
gent jury ‘deliberately’ omitted the phrase ‘of
unsound mind ’ from its verdict of suicide !”

third edition of their pamphlet, explaining the action of
Eucrasy, and pointing out their method of curing those
addicted to the liquor habit.
The Eucrasy cure is one of the oldest, having been
before the public since ISB2, at which time san at or hires
were in successful operation in many cities in America,
and the succees of their remedy is shown by hundreds of
grateful patients all over the world.
A well-known merchant, residing at Newcastle, says :

qualities of Eucrasy. I felt even after the first dose a
different person, and may say now that lam cured. The
craving has entirely gone, and I can with full confidence
recommend it to anyone, no matter what hold the liquor

yours faithfully,

Burglars and Bottles.
.The man who has once driven a burglar out of
his house with a pistol is likely to keep the
weapon handy for use in the future.
On a similar principle Mrs Elizabeth Langmaid
is never without a bottle of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup where she can lay hands on it any day.
About four years ago she was taken bad with
what was called “a complication of complaints.”
The doctors said she had an abscess on one of her
lungs and also indigestion and heart troubles.
And, seeing how she looked and felt, we should
have believed him without a moment’s hesitation.
“ You can get an idea,” she says, “how bad I
was when I tell you I lay helpless in bed nearly
nine months.
(That does give us the idea, and no mistake.
Save for the hope of recovery—which seldom
quite perishes in the mind—l would as lief be
dead, and so have the trouble over and done
with.)
“Finally,” Mrs. Langmaid goes on to say,
“when I got out of bed, all of me that could
waste away was gone. I was just a skeleton
covered by a skin. In truth they wrapped me in
wadding—for appearance and for such comfort
and warmth as the protection might give me.
“ Whatever my complaint was I always had a
dreadful pain in my sides and under the shoulder-
blades ; but the medicines I took had no more
effect on it than so much sweetened water would
have had.
“ While in this miserable condition I remem-
bered how different friends of mine had spoken
of the virtues of Seigel’s Syrup for many kinds
of ailments that nothing else seemed able to
help,
“Anyway, I was sure it would be no mistake to
try it, and so I got a bottle from Campbell & Co.’s
store in this town. Up to that time I always had
a great feeling of weariness and drowsiness after
eating, and could not shake it off.
“But, to my delight, I soon discovered that a
doSe_ of the Syrup dispelled this almost im-
mediately, and by the time I had finished the
first bottle I was greatly improved.
“As you would suppose I persevered in taking
the remedy until by degrees I got strong again.
Gradually, too, I picked up my lost flesh, and
recovered my former good health.
“ Ever since then I keep a bottle of Seigel’s
Syrup in the house, and take a dose whenever I
feel out of sorts in any way.
“ You may publish this if you like, and I shall
always be glad to hear of Seigel’s Syrup doing
for others what it did for me.”—Elizabeth
Langmaid, Market-street, Muswellbrook, N.S. W.,
Sept. 26th, 1899.
At St. Helena, recently, a Boer prisoner,
boing attacked by a large shark while bathing,
wrestled with it, and though he had an arm and a
leg bitten off, suoceeded in dragging the fish into
3ft. of water. The man was got ashore, but soon
died.
In England there are 25 open-air sanatoria
for consumptives advertising in the current num-
ber of the British Medical Journal. Most of
them stand in sheltered wooded districts averag-
ing GOOft. above sea level. A few are on the
coast, near the sea; the majority are lighted by
electricity, and their weekly fees range .from 2 to
4 guineas.
T’other day some Fern Gully (Vic.) rate-
payers were summoned to appear before the local
court of injustice at 10 o’clock on a certain day.
The cheap beaks, however, opened the court at
nine ; and had the cases called. “No appearance,
y’r warships.” Verdicts for full amounts claimed,
with costs. At 9.45 the unlucky defendants began
to arrive—just in time to see the court disappear-
ing into the nearest pub. for refreshments.

London Tit-Bits :—

dences in Sydney, Australia, are on the top floor, and the

otherwise in Australia. Inoredible though it may appear,
they have already discussed the advisability of attaching
smoking-rooms to the schools.”
The contributors of this valuable information
do not appear to be aware that Sydney houses
have no roofs, or that in Australian schools
smoking-classes have been established, with
prizes graded according to proficiency—age for
age—with cigarette, cigar, and pipe respectively.
A paragraph for intending cohentingenters
(from Windsor Magazine) : —
If you go to the Nut and Bolt Works at Cwmbran, in
South Wales, you may to-day see a middle-aged man
(none too strong in health sometimes, from his exertions
on a certain fearful January night), working as a common
laborer, in order to buy enough to earn him his daily
bread ! A man of fair complexion he is, hard-working,
honest, respectable. When he is “ dressed-up ” there
appears on his coat that little Maltese Cross, with its
magic words, “ For Valour.”
This is John Williams, V.O., the man who saved the
patients in the hospital at Rorke’s Drift whilst Hook
guarded the door ! This is the man who stayed behind
in the burning place when surrounded by hordes of Zulus,
looking in the windows, watching with savage joy their
fiendish work ; the man who refused to leave the charnel-
house whilst a single sick or wounded soldier was left in
it; the man who, with the brave Hook, defied the whole
host of Zulus to enter that blazing building if they dared!
This is the man who at last smashed through the thick
wall and literally dragged the wounded soldiers after him
—Hook doing the same —one by one, till he had dropped
them all bodily into the yard just below, where the brave
Hitch was trying to keep the .savages at bay with his rifle-
fire, so as to make a clear passage for the patients to cross
into safety !
And to-day this man has to earn a scanty living as a
common laborer, and to toil as best he can at such hard
work as a nut-and-bolt factory affords, if he wishes to
have bread to eat ! We give the successful general a
splendid income, and in the end a pension, while to such
simple heroes as Williams we offer nothing better than
the workhouse !
The Bulletin always reckoned that it
would be a sight worth seeing when Reid and
Kingston collided, and so it was. The eye-glassed
one had sat down after eloquently denouncing the
educational test for the exclusion of Asiatics as a
weak, foolish subterfuge and calling wildly for
straight, honest exclusion. Then Kingston struck
the table with his huge fist and made the books
fly round his head like a halo while he told the
House how Reid arid three other Premiers, a few
years ago, agreed to stand shoulder to shoulder in
demanding straight-out exclusion, and then the fat
man sneaked back to Sydney and accepted the edu-
cational test —the same one that he now denounced
—without even giving notice of his intended
breach of faith. And then Kingston pointed his
great hand at the podgy backslider like an indig-
nant elephant scoffing at a caterpillar.
Cabled recently that the Boers still fight-
ing had been importing “ Krag-Jorgensen ” rifles
and ammunition. The K. J. is the 6 millimetre
(about Jin.) U.S.A. rifle, said by European experts
to be much superior to the 8m.in. Mauser. The
news has some local significance, seeing that Aus-
tralia, relying, as usual, on British military infal-
libility, has been in a great hurry to lay in a stock
of the now apparently obsolete Imperial rifles.
Now, if Australia buys a stock of obsolete rifles
and cartridges, it will soon have to throw them
on the scrap-heap and buy others which will, by
that time, be obsolescent. Indeed, the Krag-Jor-
gensen may, at any moment, be superseded by a
rifle with which the Swiss Confederation has been
experimenting, which has a barrel of an aluminium
alloy (not half so heavy as iron), a 5m.m. bore,
and the flattest trajectory yet obtained.


A GREAT LIBRARY.«heWORLD’S LITERATURE.
important Nows for Every Man who ever Buys a Book.
ft
a
1
ANDREW LANG
Writes on “The Progress of Litsiature
the Nineteenth Century.”
“The First Intellectual Task of the
Age.”
“And so, I say it most confidentially, the first
intellectual task of our age is to rightly order and
make serviceable the vast realm of printed material
which four centuries have swept across our path.
To organise our knowledge, to systematise our
reading, to save out of the relentless cataract of
ink the immortal thoughts of the greatest —this is
a necessity, unless the productive ingenuity of
man is to lead us at last to a measureless and
pathless chaos.”
Mr. Harrison goes on to indicate what would be
the high value of some collection, or “healthy
rational syllabus of essential books,” that would
present “ a working epitome of what is best and
most enduring in the literature of the world.”
And he adds
“Some such firm foothold in the vast and in-
creasing torrent of literature we certainly must
find, unless all that is great in literature is to be
borne away in the floods of books. With this we
may avoid an interminable wandering over the
pathless waste of waters. Without it we may read
everything and know nothing, wandering like un-
classed spirits round the outskirts only of those
Elysian fields where the great dead dwell and hold
high converse.”
w
FERDINAND BRUNETIERE
Writes on “ French Poetry.”
SIR WALTER BESANT
Writes on “Novels That Have Made History.”
The Misuse of Books.
In a memorable address delivered nearly a
quarter of a century ago, a distinguished critic of
books, Mr. Frederick Harrison, gave utterance to
these pregnant thoughts :
“Far be it from me,” said Mr. Harrison, “ to
gainsay the inestimable value of good books; but
I often think we forget that other side to this
glorious view of literature—the misuse of books,
the debilitating waste of life in aimless, promis-
cuous, vapid reading, or even it may be in the
poisonous inhalation of mere literary garbage.”
“Whether our neglect of the great books comes
from our not reading at all, or from an incorrigible
habit of reading the little books, it ends in just
the same tiling. And that thing is ignorance of
all the greater literature of the world.”
Tv"
f
r*
BRET HARTE
Writes on “ The Rise of the Short Story.”
Or. Garnett and His Distinguished
Associates.
The chief editor of “The Library” is Dr.
Richard Garnett, C. 8., who retired a few months
ago from the responsible and laborious post of
Keeper of Printed Books in the vast library of the
British Museum, after a half-century of dis-
tinguished service. His counsel has been sought
by almost every man of letters who has resorted
to our National Library for the purpose of
literary research ; he has been intimately associ-
ated with many of the great literary enterprises
of the last twenty years, and he has probably
answered more questions about books, and post ibly
knows the contents of mo?e books, than any other
living Englishman. He is more than a man of
books, however, as many volumes of the most
varied character —volumes of poetry, of humor
and biography, as well as history and libraria ship
—have amply revealed.
Though the work is in English throughout, with
Dr. Garnett have been associated M. Leon Yallee,
Librarian of the Bibliotheque Nationale of Paris,
the largest library, it is surprising to find, in the
world : Dr. Alois Brandi, Professor of Literature
in the Royal University of Berlin ; and Donald
G. Mitchell, of New York, known the world
over for his delightful volumes written under the
nom de plume of ‘‘ Ik Marvel ” ; not to speak of
many qualified assistants.
Three Hundred Copyrighted Works
Included.
The hearty interest with which the most popular
of modern authors have welcomed Dr. Garnett’s
great enterprise is shown by their readiness to
permit the inclusion of their writings—from, in
fact, above 300 copyrighted works. Their co-
operation is further evidenced in several novel
features of the “ Library.”
The chief office of the “Library” is to gather
together a thousand of the world’s masterpieces,
and to provide an endless store of the best reading.
But it goes much further than this. The plan of
the “Library” includes a series of delightful
original essays on the literature of different epochs
and different topics, all written especially for this
work by the most distinguished of living men of
letters.
These introductory studies afford just that
stimulating touch which is so often needed to put
the reader in a mood to enjoy the treasures which
the “ Library ” contains. With these aids he no
longer explores the wide waste of books at ran-
dom, but with accurate knowledge and with the
clearest possible insight.
♦
Reading for a Lifetime.
To sum up, this great work comprises in itself
a rich and marvellously varied library. It pro-
vides entertaining reading sufficient to last the
most of readers fora lifetime; while, on the other
hand, it will supplement and round out the best
private collection of books. As an introduction
to a wider acquaintance with the best literature,
or as a means for inculcating and developing a
taste for literature, especially among the young,
it must be welcomed in every home where books
are valued and read.
It is “infinite riches in a little room-”
Max Muller’s Ideal World Library.
Something of the same thought runs through
what Professor Max Muller wrote a few years ago,
when he said :
“Fewbooks I find supremely great from end to
end, while, on the other hand, there are parts and
passages, or whole poems, that I can read again
and again, wondering more and more every time
how one man could have written them.
“If I were to tell you,” the great philologist
goes on, “what I really think, I am afraid you
would call me the greatest literary heretic, or an
utter ignoramus. Take the greatest poet of
antiquity, and if I am to speak the truth, I must
say there are long passages even in Homer which
seem to me extremely tedious. Take the greatest,
or, at all events, one of the greatest, poets of our
century, and again I must confess that not a few
of Goethe’s writings seem to me not worth a second
reading. There are gems in the most famous,
there are gems in the least known of poets ; but
there is not a single poet, so far as I know, who
has not written too much, and who could claim a
place for all his work in what might be called a
Library of World Literature.”
The Bewilderment of Modern Days.
■ - I
“ But there is much more. Even those who are
resolved to read the better books are embarrassed
by the field of choice, practically boundless.
“The vast proportion of books we shall never
be able to read. A serious percentage of books
are not worth reading. There never was a time,
at least, during the last two hundred years, when
the difficulties of making an efficient use of books
were greater than they are to-day.
“So the question which weighs upon me with
such really crushing urgency is this : What are
the books that in our little remnant of reading
time it is most vital for us to know ? Every book
that we take up without a purpose is an oppor-
tunity lost of taking up a book with a purpose.
We know that books differ as diamonds and
sand on the seashore.”
HfiNRY JAMES &
Writes on “ The Rise of the Novel.” *-
Two Great Indexes.
It will at once give something of an idea of the
extraordinary range and variety of the “Library,”
and at the same time reveal how accessible is
every topic, section, or page, to note that the
“Library ” is double indexed, that is, that it has
, both a general and topical index, and that these
two indexes contain some 10,000 entries. Every
author, the subject on which he writes, the title of
his story,, history, sketch, or poem, the first lines
of every poem, every proper name or character,
and even the special and individual topics, re-
ferred to, it may be, only in a page or even a
paragraph, are all entered up, indexed and cross
referenced.
The Prophecy Brilliantly Realised.
These prophetic words, from two of England’s
most eminent men of letters, describe in very
essence the plan and purpose of the great
“LIBRARY OF FAMOUS LITERATURE,”
issued under the editorship of Dr. Richard
Garnett, 0.8. It is precisely such a “Library of
World Literature ” as Max Muller had so curiously
foreshadowed—twenty handsome volumes, bring-
ing together in a convenient and delightful form,
and still at a price within the purchasing power
of the great reading public, those supreme ex-
pressions of human genius, those single poems,
those famous chapters, those marvellous short
stories, or portions and descriptions from larger
works—in short, those great parts of great books,
“ the immortal part of their most mortal bodies,”
which, as the Oxford professor says, “make it
seem a very miracle that they could have been
composed by man.”
It goes almost without saying that such a work
would have been next to valueless if it had not
been done by men of the highest competence and
on the broadest scale. “The LIBRARY OF
FAMOUS LITERATURE” represents the best
judgment and most accomplished criticism of our
time.

Illustrations That Are Interesting.
The “Library” is richly embellished with
nearly 500 full page illustrations and colored
plates, printed separately from the text, on heavy
enamelled paper. There are portraits of the most
famous authors who ever lived ; there is a delight-
ful series of pictures of “Authors’ Homes” and
the still more novel series of “ Famous Authors
in their Homes.” So, too, their haunts are shown,
the scenes of memorable passages in their books,
and places celebrated in literature or in history.
Rare-Colored Plates
The colored plates, running through every
volume, form one of the most notable features of
the “Library,” They have been gathered from
rare sources, and comprise some of the most ex-
quisite specimens of the antique art of book illu-
mination which flourished centuries ago, when
books were made by hand, and the transcription
of a single volume often represented the labor of
years.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
V.A.T.C. meet (ended Saturday) produced a
profit of over £2OOO. Last year’s excess was £2720, but
stakes were £5BO less and weather much more favorable.
* * • *
«Ti l ' B year’s Caulfield Cup, contested by a field
Of and run in record time, was, in its purely sporting
asp( ci, a thing of beauty and a joy for ever. Followers of
form ask for nothing better than a race so honestly
fought out as this was. To begin with, the first favorite
was the brilliant mare Wakeful, weighted at 9st. Wake-
ful, after winning Newmarket Handicap from end to end,
had captured Doncaster Handicap over the Randwick
mile with ease, and had finished a good third in Sydney
Oup. Wakeful had run away with the w.f.a Caulfield
Stakes a week previously. Her speed and stamina looked
equal to winning the Cup in her own plain, straight-
forward way. The mare was ridden with that idea. Un-
fortuiiatdy for her backers, the gay Wakeful played up
at the barrier, and, being* placed at the wrong end of the
line, far away from the inner rail, she had to strike across
the course soon after the start to obtain the coveted posi-
tion. So, instead of getting a great “ break ” on the field,
as in Caulfield Stakes, she was only a length or so ahead
of Hymettus as they belted past the stand.
The race may be described as a chasing of
Wakeful by Blunderer, Hymettus, Bonnie Chiel, Cluster,
Caledonia and 17 others, of whom Flagship made a show
towards the finish, and Juggler ran respectably.
Blunderer and Bonnie Chiel appeared to drop out of their
positions through tiredness. The “ ruck ” horses never
had a chance. Probably Grasspan and three or four of
his companions would have got nearer to the front but
for being blocked ; all the same, they were fairly beaten
by the more speedy leaders. Some distance from
home it was evident that Hymettus, of all the
pursuing steeds, was the only one that could possibly
wear Wakeful down. Half-way down the straight the
big chestnut collared the flying mare, who had been
making pace for him, but even then she wasn’t beaten.
It was struggle for struggle between them —a pair of
heavy-weights, first and second favorites, fighting it out
to the bitter end. The judge gave the race to Hymettus
by half-a-head, which meant that their fore-legs were
level as they passed the post. Honors were easy between
the two bull-dog battlers, and the backers of Hymettus
had the luck to get full money instead of half-pay. There
never was a great handicap race in which a dead-heat
would have given more general satisfaction.
The Caulfield Cup amounted to what horse-
reporters call a splendid vindication of recent running.
Allowing for a little difference in the conditions, this
struggle was pretty true to the “ form ” of the previous
Saturday, when Wakeful and Hymettus finished ahead of
the crowd in the Stakes (10 furlongs), and Bonnie Chiel
and Blunderer filled the first places in the Toorak Handi-
cap (1 mile). No accident marred the race, and none of
its features had a nasty, evil smell. Grasspan disap-
pointed his backers, but then Grasspan had done nothing
on the previous two days of the meeting to justify his
backers’ confidence.
Caulfield Cup time was § sec. faster than pre-
vious record—the 2.36 of G’Naroo in 1891. Moreover, the
performance last Saturday was remarkable for the fact
that two heavy-weights made the record. Paris won with
9st. 41bs., but then the runner-up, Devon, carried less
than 88t. Cremorne, the ’96 winner, had Bst. 91bs. on his
back when Straightfire (7st. 31b.) followed him home.
And both Paris and Cremorne took 2.38 sec. or longer in
doing the trick. .
* • » * *
Hymettus belongs to Mr. Jas. Paterson,
wealthy ship-owner, coal-merchant, and prominent sport,
and is by imported Eiridspord (now dead) from Busy Bee,
by The Marquis (imp.) from Beeswing, by Lady Kirk
(imp.) from Spa (imp.), by Leamington. At two years
Hymettus got home twice in 12 starts, winning V.A.T.C.
Nursery Handicap (7st.) and V.A.T.C. Juvenile Stakes
(8.5). Next season saw him line up 18 times, and score
thrice, securing Vic. Club Flying Handicap (9st.), V.A.T.C.
Welter (9st.), and V.A.T.C. South Yarra Handicap
(8.2). Prior to his annexing his first Caulfield Cup (in ’9B)
he got away with the Three and Four-year-old Handicap
at Flemington (8.12), V.A.T.C. Heatherlie Handicap (8.9),
and V.A.T.C. Coongy Handicap (8.12). Subsequently,
Hymettus ran second to Stand Off (7.1) in the Oakleigh
Plate with 9.2 in the saddle. Twice this season he shaped
unsuccessfully, but appropriated Show Handicap (6 fur.)—
8.1 up—at Flemington, and the Vic. Club Oup (lm.) at
Caulfield (in Aug.), carrying 9.5. Then Memsie Stakes
(lm. lfur., w.f.a.) fell to him in Sept.
* » * *
Jock Powell, up on Hymettus, had luck. The
day before, it appeared unlikely he would be able to ride
through illness. He is 25 years old, and has been in the game
15 years. Adelaide Cup, Birthday Cup, Onkaparinga Cup
(twice), Alderman Cup, Goodwood Handicap, Kalgoorlie
Cup, G.N. Hurdles, and other big helpings of turf plums
have been closed on by him at various times. Talking
afterwards, Powell said ’twas impossible for any nag to
have had a clearer run than Hymettus.
• * * *
Said that there was nothing in the stable up to
trying Wakeful. She was backed for the Cup on what
she did on her own. Caulfield Stakes was merely a test
of the estimate formed on her single-handed trials.
Wakeful is the handsomest mare seen this side of the line
for many a year.
* • * *
Interesting facts of Monday’s settling over
Caulfield Cup were that Hymettus’ owner won less than
£2OOO, a Melbourne tobacconist collected close on £3OOO,
a Oollins-street stock-broker had 2000 to 20 of “ Oxy’s ”
money, and still holds 2000 to 10 from same man about
same nag for Melb. Cup. Double books are in a bad way
as everything having a chance has been coupled with
Hymettus. Still there may be a “ turn-up.” Mo. has had
many of them before.
* » * *
Up to now there has been little encouragement
for the Sydney division to estimate their Flemington
chances by the results of the A.J.C. Spring meeting.
Recent heroes—Sequence, Lucknow, Grasspan, and
Hautvillers—have diminished in importance. They
looked brilliant in comparison with the animals they met
at Randwick, but at Caulfield they made a poor show.
Hautvillers, who is a Victorian, anyhow, may win the
V.R.C. Derby, though Grasspan and Haymaker look more
dangerous, but the three-year-olds are a moderate lot.
If anything that showed up at Randwick last September
is to win the Melbourne Oup it will probably be - Aus-
tralian Colors. Granting that the four-year-old son of
Australian Peer finished only a bad third to Wakeful and
Hymettus in the Caulfield Stakes, his effort at the finish
looks more creditable on paper to-day than it seemed at
the time.
Will Hymettus win Melbourne Cup? His
Caulfield victory left the big ’un uncommonly shaky on
his pins. Present scribe would like to bet 5 to 1 against
Hymettus for the two-mile race, whilst taking 5 to 1 that
he doesn’t start for it. No punter who saw the brave old
’orse nibbling away at the grass after he had returned to
the birdcage last Saturday can confidently predict his
victory for Melbourne Cup. There are 17 days of final
preparation on the track to be put in between the two
events, and if Hymettus can stand a thorough Cup
“winding-up” his legs must be artful dissemblers.
“ Don’t back Hymettus until he goes to the post ” is good
advice. In any case he doen’t look quite hard enough
to pull through a severe two-mile race, although he is
much more fit than was that other great generous horse
Trenton when he ran second to Arsenal in the Melbourne
Oup of ’86.
A lot of common horses that can’t possibly affect
the issue, save by causing an accident, will be started in
Melbourne Oup, more’s the pity. It was so in Caulfield
Cup. The Bulletin last week named 10 as the most
likely lot to provide the winner and runner-up. Eight of
those 10 started in a field of 23, five of them finished in
the first seven, and three of these scooped the place-money
among them. Eleventh-hour tipsters, as a rule, got
nearer to the mark. Moral: The supply of “ rough out-
siders ” with a reasonable show of winning big handicaps
is very limited just now. With two or three exceptions,
the long-priced ones for Melb. Oup are not worth talking
about as long as Wakeful, Revenue, Flagship, Australian
Colours, and Hymettus gallop well in the market.
• * * *
Latest well-backed one for Melbourne Cup is
Revenue. _ A big commission from the right quarter
brought him down to short figures, and best price going
Monday last was sevens. Same sort of move was made
on same nag last year, but he never looked a possible
winner at any stage. Inference from the manner in
which those who should know best came at him is that he
does well in private. With silk up, however, it’s quite a
different thing. A five-year-old, bred to stay, and having
only 7.7 to bring home, Revenue should be good goods,
but he is a long way from it on public form.
* • • *
The wail of the owner and the punter is loud
over the disinclination of Ikey Mo. to bet to any extent
on either Caulfield or Melbourne Cups. The slightest
smell of a commission causes a terrible shrinkage of the
odds. Asserted positively not a single stable had £5000
™ an y horse for Saturday. A few years back
£10,000 could have been secured easily. Wakeful
stable only gathered up £3000 in all' the States
before the price fell to eights. In Carbine’s
year all the public were on him, and yet owner
Wallace secured £30,000. Hymettus went down to tens,
with even less money than Wakeful. A Sydney Mo in the
Vic. Club rooms last Friday night laid a few wagers at 90
to 20 against Wakeful, and, later, seeing another Mo
backing the mare, fell over himself in an effort to grab all
available at 85 to 20. Immediately the Chosen lay a
tenner or a pony their satellites are sent all over the
place to back it so that the Children can’t lose. There’s
nothing in owning horses now, and won’t be till the tote
comes along with bigger prizes and more equitable
betting.
* • «
Ikey Mo. has reason to perpetually wail his
‘losses.’’ After winning Caulfield Stakes, Wakeful
"5°PP® d 9 to 1 to 5 to 1 and at the Victorian Club on
the Saturday evening an investment of a solitary £100
brought her down to fours. At this rate the punting of
a thousand or so would foreshadow ruin to the whole
tribe of Ikey.

28 starters for the Gwyn Nursery at Caulfield
last Saturday, and Fishery, a handsome daughter of Bill
of Portland and Fishwife, was backed at 3 to 1 to beat the
fieW Given a fair run for all of them the odds against
the favorite were absurdly short, but the foolishness of
punters was emphasised by an accident. Three of the
mob toppled over, and the 3 to 1 chance was among them.
Kinglike, the colt that scored a brilliant win in
the Eclipse Stakes at Caulfield last year, and has never
done anything since, was a starter for the Ooongy Handi-
cap on the Wednesday of the recent Caulfield meeting.
He whipped in the big field. The story is that the
severe race of 12 months ago broke Kinglike’s heart, or
ruined his constitution—but Time is a great healer of
hearts, anyhow. Also, it is unlikely that Leslie M'Donald
would pay for the privilege of galloping the horse in
pubhc if there were no hope of getting him back to
form.

* • • *
Forty-second gathering of N.S.W. National
Rifle Association began Monday with some remarkable
shooting First stage of the King’s match provided a
record—36 shooters made the “ possible ” at 500yds and
a big number notched 34 and 35. Previously the Associa-
tion gave £1 each for “ possibles,” but they’ve dropped
that since the introduction of the -303 rifle rendered
scoring' of seven consecutive “bulls” easier than with
other weapons. Of the possible 105 in the first stage of
“ the King’s,” two competitors—Mr. A. Carter (Vic.) and
Const. D. Scott—totted up 100. High merit of the shoot-
ing generally may be gauged by the fact that 125 scored
90 or over.
* * * *
The finals of N.S.W. Sports Club amateur
boxing championship competitions were slogged off at
Queen’s Hall, Sydney, Saturday night, in a decidedly
tame manner. Boxing finals are always unsatisfactory,
and there seems to be no way of avoiding it. A certain
number of men draw byes. If they are good ’uns, they
romp right over the bashed and bruised victors who
struggled through a number of gruelling rounds the night
before. If they are wastrels who would have been slugged-
out in the first scrap, they have no chance against the har-
dened veterans who have slogged their way right through
by sheer grit. Bantam Post was belted right out in
one act by Harry Clifton. Featherweight Simpson,
Otto Cribb’s brother, made a rather miserable exhibition
against the bull-necked and deep-chested Clifford. His
one redeeming point was skilful ducking, but Clifford
followed him ruthlessly till Simpson took refuge on the
floor till the referee counted nine, when he climbed on to
two unsteady legs, and for the rest of the time hung
round Clifford’s legs till he was knocked down and
counted out. The fight between M’Tigue and Welland
began promisingly, notwithstanding that Mac. had one
arm in bandages. Things were humming nicely when
Mae. accidentally placed one arm round his opponent’s
leg, and was promptly disqualified. The intervals between
the contests were filled in with rapier and sword exhi-
bitions, which were by far the best parts of the show.
m m * *
Yankee bruiser, Scanlan, opened business with
local negro, Ike Stewart (11.4), at Sydney Golden Gate
A.O. on Saturday night to a crowded house. Scanlan
was taller, at least a stone heavier, and had the advantage
of several inches reach, but all the same he had far from
a walk over, and, although Ike was beaten, he managed
to tear away the great funk that wrapped the stranger
round and flapped out over the souls of Sydney pugdom.
The first round was very willing, and Ike ladled out bruise
and bash just as effectively as the other fellow from Murka.
Bounds two and three were absolutely the black’s, and,
had the fight ended there, Ike would have adhered to the
shekels. In round four Stewart began to give way, but
the fighting was still fierce enough to move the case-
hardened audience to roars and howls. Sydney audiences
always howl when hair, and blood, and hunks of pug are
flying about. In round four Scanlan got one home and
laid the Austral right out, but he came up again and gave
his opponent a couple of trimmers. The Yank, finished
artistically by planting a left on one section of the jaw,
and before the reeling Stewart could recover, a weighty
right on the other side stretched him out as one in a
dream, while a droning voice counted ten. If Saturday
night’s exhibition was Scanlan’s true form, and writer is
very suspicious it was not, M'Ooll and his backers are going
to win a lot of money comparatively easily on November 18
at Newcastle. There were flashes of science and spurts
of power that indicated that the Yankee had banked his
fires till the market was fairly rigged.
* • * *
A first-class bruiser, when he is at its best, is
like a first contingent when it is departing—simply loaded
down with assurances that he shall never, never want for
anything. When he slumps ! One-time Victorian
sterling bruiser, Peter Newton, proceeds to address those
who promised wildly in the days of long ago : “ During my
travels many friends invited me to stay at their places for a
while. If they feel that way now, I shall be only too
happy to go to town or country, as things are not too
good with me at present, and it would do me a lot of
good.” Painful trouble in the legs keeps Peter down,
and has reduced him to poverty. Will some of the
“ many friends ” of his days of affluence kindly send along
their addresses ? That other great Peter (Jackson) found
about three friends, out of all the armies of admirers, to
stand to him in his day of broken health, and they were
the ones who could afford it least.

French dairy-farmers threaten to invade
England with their milk, and, alarmed at this
new blessing of Freetrade, the onee-faithful Cob-
denite papers cry out “ that the impoverishment
of British agriculture is attributable to foreign
competition.”
Seven pounds seven shillings is recorded in
the first year’s Commonwealth expenses as having
been spent on the funeral of the late Minister
Dickson. That is certainly modest—so modest
that if it was worth spending it was not worth
entering up as an historical record. It has been
the custom in the U.S. that members who died
during the currency of a session were buried
wholly at public expense.
On Tuesday last the Bank of N.S.W.
proceeded to do honor to Messrs. Lamport
and Hazel ton, the manager and assistant of
the Henty branch bank that was attacked by
armed robbers some time ago. Inside the
head banking chamber was a crowd of faces,
most of them with shaven chins and a grey-
ish tinge in the hair. The counters were covered
with red cloth and draped with Union Jacks. Dr.
M'Kellar made a speech, in which he related
many successful attempts on the bank’s wealth,
which went to show that men like Lamport were
somewhat scarce. They dated as far back as
1859, when the Deniliqum branch was robbed by
way of setting a good example. Then he
went on to say he wouldn’t have censured
Lamport very severely if he had surrendered,
and dwelt upon the value of the example
set by the square-headed, heavy-jowled young
man with the mousey hair and moustache, to all
the officers to risk their lives in “ our service.”
Next he enumerated the gifts: 12 months’ leave
of absence on full pay, a gold watch and chain,
suitably inscribed, and a cheque for £5OO (wild ap-
plause at the mention of the cheque for £500),
which he handed over, and which Lamport put in
his waistcoat pocket. He then presented assistant
Hazelton, who yelled loudly and threw a chair at
the retreating robbers, with three months’ leave of
absence on full pay, a gold watch and chain, suit-
ably inscribed, and a cheque for £lOO (more
and proportionate cheers at mention of cheque
for £100). General manager Russell French, on
behalf of the staff, presented Lamport with a
dress travelling case, gold sovereign-purse, field-
glasses, and gold sleeve-links. To Hazelton
the staff gave a dress travelling case and a gold
sovereign-purse. Then Inspector-General Fos-
bery, in a bob-tailed coat and grey pants,
announced that the Government would award
gold medals for valor. The statuesque DJbbs of
the Commercial Bank passed over a cheque for
£25, and was followed by Manager Adams of the
A. J.S. with a similar cheque, which Lamport put
into the same pocket. Then the recipient re-
marked that he thought some people were taking
an exalted view of things. After this there were
songs and champagne and flashlight photographs.
After declaring for weeks that the Federal
tariff was the outcome of a dark Victorian con-
spiracy to loot the other States, and a “sop” to
Melbourne, and the device of a Victorian-bossed
Ministry to prop up the “ricketty ” industries of a
bankrupt State, the Sydney Freetrade papers
suddenly broke out last Tuesday with the
news that Victoria had arisen unanimously
against the accursed thing, and had repu-
diated it at a great public meeting. And
then they forgot all about Victoria’s alleged indig-
nant repudiation of the tariff again, and went
right on talking of it as a hideous Victorian con-
spiracy once more. As a matter of fact, Victoria
is showing very little enthusiasm either
way. . There certainly was a quiet sort of
meeting in Melbourne, allegedly to protest
against the tariff, but only one or two
tenth-rate Victorian politicians attended, and
the speakers were almost all from other States.
It was mainly a few extraneous politicians pro-
testing to a Victorian audience. On the other
hand, nobody seems to be boiling with joy over
the tariff down in the Cabbage Garden. Not
that that makes any difference to the Calico
Jimmy party’s argument. The Sydney Free-
trade papers said that Victoria was over-
whelmed with joy at the tariff and that proved its
utter badness, as being a Victorian conspiracy
against the peace and happiness of Australia.
Then they said, on the strength of last Monday’s
meeting, that Victoria hated the tariff, and that
also proved its utter badness, for even Victoria
couldn’t stand it. The Sydney Freetrade press
has swelled Victoria up to such artificial import-
ance that that State can’t sneeze now without
thereby proving something. Victoria, in fact, is
the Tom Slattery of the States. It was the Free-
trade Sydney D.T., by the way, which set up
Tom Slattery as a bogey-Machiavelli in N.S.W.

N.S. W. ’s allegedly well-meaning Treasurer
is said to have another good resolution on hand :
he proposes introducing a Bill to define what
works are fairly payable out of loans and what are
not. At present, everything is payable out of
loans when the Treasurer feels that way, which
he always does. All the same, it is one thing to in-
clude gaols and lunatic asylums as “permanent
and reproductive works/’ while the House, by tacit
agreement, looks the other way, and another thing
to declare by Act of Parliament that they are
“ permanent and reproductive.” And, just for
that reason, Waddell’s alleged hazy good intention
is not likely to be further heard of.
A Richmond resident sued Vic. Railway
Commissioner for £l5, for damages sustained
through stepping out of a carriage that had over-
shot the station-platform, and falling 7ft. The
accident happened at night. The defence was
that “there was no negligence and no invitation
to alight.” Defendant got a verdict, with costs.
It is to be assumed from this that passengers are
not to leave railway - carriages till expressly
invited by the Commissioner or his official repre-
sentative, and any man neglecting to wait for the
invitation leaves on his own responsibility, if he
leaves on his ear.

Singing-teacher Steffani, who, for practically the past
20 years, has been established in Sydney, sailed t’other
day for Europe, accompanied by Mrs. Steffani and Miss
Hetty Molroyd. Mr. S. intends to get an engagement
for the latter lady during the forthcoming Italian carnival
season. Steffani has, in Sydney, turned out some notably
good vocalists, such as Mrs. W. A. M'Donald, n&e Slattery
(one of the few true dramatic sopranos that Australia
has produced), Miss Florence Schmidt (at present doing
well in England), and Miss Hetty Holroyd (of whom much
may be reasonably expected). Miss Nellie Cooke, of
whose capabilities Steffani has a high opinion, will follow
in a month or so, and study with him in Europe. .Steffani
was originally in the Admiralty Office, which he left to
join Mapleson’s Italian opera co. at Covent Garden, and
it was there that he Italianised his patronymic of Stevens.
He afterwards played the principal bass part, “Daland,” in
the first English production of Wagner’s “ Flying Dutch-
man,” at London Lyceum, with Santley as the F.D. About
’77 he came to Australia, for Sam Lazar’s opera co., and has
remained here pretty well ever since Being possessed of a
“ long voice, he was able to tackle a great variety of
rdles, including Marcel (“Huguenots”), Oroveso
(“Norma”), the Commendatore (Don Giovanni), Alfonso
(“ Lucrezia Borgia ”), Don Basilio (“ Barber of Seville ”),
etc. Steffani is also a painter of no mean capacity, and
has frequently “exhibited.” He is undecided as to his
s>,dnei wm vmS,My him
: ' , . . ...
Apropos that orchestra strike. To the musicians toil-
ing immediately under the stage margin, unhonored and
unpaid, an impecunious actor-manager appeals in vain for
assistance. They lack the motive of personal vanity that
impels mummers to go on hoping against hope as long
as they can go on mumming They rightly regard a
theatrical show as a business to which they are bound
only by bonds of cash remuneration in accordance with
contract, and for the new manager, who has established
no claim to their present sympathy by a record of prompt
payments in the past, those sane and sordid bandsmen
care no more than if he were an hotelkeeper who had en-
gaged them to play in the vestibule of his pub. United
they perform, and together they “ strike” unless it suits
them to continue the music on spec. Why shouldn’t
they strike if the spec, looks unpromising to them ?
They know their business. For a just appreciation of
things theatrical one can’t do better than apply to a
trombone, a cornet, or a big drum, not that a first violin
can’t also be a very practical person where his salary’s
concerned.
S.S. : The cruellest thing I have seen on the stage has
been happening nightly at Melb. Majesty’s, where Mrs.
Edwin Bryer plays Mrs. Malaprop Rocks in a summer
atmosphere several deg. above human comfort. Mrs. B. is a
veteran, having reached an age at which it is no longer
necessary to repudiate past birthdays. She is also an
actress of liberal proportions, and carries more weight
than any other actress at present in active operation in
Australia ; and yet she is called upon to do her part with
a lively quartette in a breathless song-and-dance and an
energetic knock-about. The audience thoughtlessly
insists upon encores, and when the substantial old actress
resumes operations that particular turn becomes a
painful event, and provides the judicious with a burning
grievance.
Edith Crane’s dangerous roll down a flight of stairs in
“Nadjezda,” at Melb. Princess’s, was a very Yankee
novelty. Some critics have complained that Sarah Bern-
hardt’s death-scenes are too violently sensational to be
admirable. But if the startling back-fall of Divine Sarah
is “ playing it rather low ” on her enthralled audience,
what shall be said of a lady’s attempt to score a high-
tragedy effect by rolling downstairs? Miss Crane’s route
was carefully padded, of course, for the sake of her
cranium ; all the same, the advt. spoke truth when it
said that “ very few gentlemen ” would care to risk the
ea^-
A fasting man has been putting up a gruesome exhibi-
tion in M.L. country districts—a life-like representation
of a hanging. The scaffold is 16ft. high, and similar to
the legal neck-breaking apparatus, with the exception
of an “ improved trap ” (which means a lot to the person
to be dispatched). The usual formalities are gone
through, and the fasting man signs his own death-warrant,
Why a man should sign his own death-warrant isn’t ap-
parent, but the incident is stuck in on general principles,
An executioner pulls the lever, the condemned man
shoots down an incline, and dangles 7ft. from the ground.
The horrid show would be stopped by any sensible police-
man, and the people who go to see it, in the dim hope
that some day the man will make a mistake and kick
convulsively and become a blue and green corpse, should
a i B o be arrested on suspicion of a tendency to brutal
crime, and punished accordingly.

“ Melb.” : Mr. Tyrone lower has been reading tne
Scriptures for two Sunday nights at Pastor Hughes,
St. Peters, with a chorus of delight from the lady
fashion-reporters. I regard this sort of thing as rather
nauseous. It is to be regretted that Mrs. I otter should
have been cajoled into this figuring at an English Church
service. Her style of living in Australia, and that of Miss
Nance O’Neil, showed a proper appreciation of the dignity
of the profession. It is remarkable that Coppin has
never played off the church-and-stage act. His idea of
church-and-stage was co-operating with Parson Perks, at
Richmond, Melb., in the establishment of the local dis-
pensary. Perks was before that his bitterest foe, but
came round on finding out that this play-actor was quite
a Christian,
Mr. Burns-Walker debated a new pupil at the Young
Men’s Christian Association, last Monday, when Miss
Emily Hutchinson-Cooper displayed a voice that has been
trained down so effectively that, whereas she once pro-
bably shrieked, she now certainly sings. Miss Cooper sang
the Patti valse-song and other melodies, and also duetted
with Mr. Burns-Walker in something from ‘Lucia di
Lammermoor.” She didn’t always rise to the occasion,
nevertheless the audience frequently rose to her ana
gave her enough floral decorations to stock a flower-
shop. Burns-Walker was encored several times, but
sternly refused to respond.
Died in Melbourne, recently, Adolph Gonzales, an old
Australian circus identity. Not so very old in years—
only 52—but a veteran performer in the ring.
A Melb. merry-go-round proprietor has fixed up a new
and highly-popular form of amusement called the Flying
Angels. The cherub mounts a scaffold, catches on to a
pulley running on a taut wire rope, and slides down to
the far end, bumping into a well-stuffed hay-bag at the
finish. T’other night a well-nourished angel of 18 stone,
from the neighborhood of Lonsdale-street, started on the
heavenly tour, but her avoirdupois was too much for her
muscles and she hastily descended into the mud—with
some broken ribs.
Chance or coincidence, or what? Some years ago, a
man named Thomas Peel was killed at Chiltern (Vic.),
On a recent Monday Madame Heller was giving a clair-
voyant exhibition at the hall in that town, and in reply
to the question, “ How did Thomas Peel die ? ” she gave
a detailed and accurate description of the accident,
Then she added that Peel’s mate, Andrew M'Millan,
would shortly be killed in somewhat similar fashion. One
week later something heavy fell on poor M'Millan,
and the prediction was verified. The local correspondent
of Melbourne A<jb swears to this.

The publication of crowds of symptoms, testimonials and puffs of one’s skill and attainments savours too much of self-praise ; so the public
should look with a doubtful eye upon all those who “ doth protest too much.”
Dr. Watson does not pretend to say that “ One Pound is sufficient to send,” as stated in certain advertisements, for he considers that the
public are quite satisfied to pay a fair price for fair and legitimate treatment.

The prospects of Lager Bier fife wing in N.S.W.
looked a case for the coroner until, on 22nd June last, Mr.
Edmund Reach bought the extensive plant and buildings
of the N.S.W. Lager Bier Company. Then, regardless of
expense, he entirely remodelled the brewery, and placed
it under the management of his sons, Mr. Edmund Reach,
Jun., and Mr. Arnold Resch. These two sons have spent
several years in Europe, mastering every detail of
scientific brewing. Reach’s Lager, therefore, should
quickly become the national drink of Australia.*
The V. V. briar pipe is an old and acknowledged
favorite, through its unvaried excellence of quality. Its
manufacturers spare neither time, trouble, nor expense
in bringing their pipes to the standard of perfection, with
the gratifying result that the pipes have not only caught
the Australian smoker, but have also secured the appro-
bation of the seasoned smokers of Europe. The prize
medal at the Paris Exhibition of 1900, where medals are
earned only by top merit, was taken by V.V. pipes.*
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
for week ended 19th October that marsupial skins were in
only moderate supply, and slightly higher rates ruled.
But the market, though very firm, cannot be expected to
remain at that level. In opossum skins there is no
change of price, but quantities offering are diminishing.
The demand for tallow in London last week was very
poor, only one-fourth of the catalogues clearing. Local
shipping buyers are consequently looking sideways, and
out of 644 casks offered, only 47 were taken by local users.
Lower rates are impending, and the co. is therefore
selling all available before the slump gets any worse. At
present the slump is about 30s. per ton. Hides were
irregular. Sheep-skins were also irregular, buyers having
assumed a cautious attitude owing to certain news. At
the wool-sales at Melbourne and Adelaide the market was
not quite so good as a week ago. Sydney sales begin
next week, but there are signs that the prices of last
week will not be maintained. Owing to beneficial rains,
there is reason to hope for a record wheat-harvest.

Obit. : At Rookwood Asylum, Sydney,
Patrick O’Hara, a native of Sligo, Ireland, un-
married, aged 101 years. There is another old
man now living at the asylum who is well into the
nineties.
S.A. Assembly has been discussing for the
last three months various schemes of Parliamentary
reform. The Jenkins Ministry wants to reduce
both Houses by one-third, but every other party
unanimously opposes this and submits its own
scheme. The country crowd, which forms a
majority, wants to re-distribute seats giving pro-
vincial districts more representation, and the
Laborites favor leaving the Assembly alone and
abolishing the Tory Council altogether. Holder
knew what he was doing when he proposed, last
year, that the whole matter should be settled by
an independent convention elected by the people,
for Parliament is evidently determined not to
reform itself.
South Australia is considering four dif-
ferent harbor schemes. Lindon Bates, eminent
engineer, lately recommended a million-and-a-half
scheme at the entrance to Port Adelaide river.
Merchants and wharf-owners with big vested
interests—including the wealthy S.A. Co.—want
the river deepened. Marino, the best site of all,
is 10 miles from Port Adelaide, and has the united
opposition of all other “ vested-interest ” parties.
The latest scheme is a harbor at Port Victor, with
a canal to Goolwa to tap the Murray and secure
much valuable river trade. This latter scheme is
very old, for one S.A. Ministry spent over a
million in building a breakwater and left it but
half-finished. Strange that, though different
crowds are now shrieking for Govt, construction
of an outer harbor at the cost of the taxpayer, and
the State is considered quite good enough to take
charge of this unremunerative job, it is considered
unfit to manage the profitable tramways. Tucker,
M.P., who runs the Port Victor scheme, is one of
Snow’s chief tramway barrackers.
“ Ormond ” to The Bulletin :—
In yours of 12th inst. a par. states that Yic. Assembly
rushed the Coupon-abolition Bill through; “killing the
co. by ambush.” Permit me to deny the statement. Not
long ago Premier Peacock received a deputation from the
monopolistic co., when the latter made out the best case
it could in favor of its pernicious Yankee system. The
co. also knew that during the last State election nearly
every member pledged himself to vote for the abolition of
coupons ; while some time ago a committee of Yic. Leg.
Council strongly urged that the rotten idea should be
stamped out. Both Democrats and Tories agreed that the
public should be no longer gulled by such a questionable
and mysterious snare. The co. also had the opportunity
of replying to the large deputations of tradesmen and the
public, who demanded that the unwholesome trade
should be abolished, and the Premier says he never
before listened to arguments so weak and ridiculous. All
the co. could prove was that they (the shareholders)
would lose their tremendous, and unearned, divs. if Govt,
abolished the coupons. The system had scarcely a friend
in the whole Vic. Assembly, and few solid busi-
ness men really favor the coupon system. They prefer to
trade on the merits and cheapness of their goods.

INVALIDS
AND
THEACED.


SMASHED UP IN HEALTH
THREE WEEKS IN BED.
UNABLE TO STIR.
The Case of Mr. W. CONNELL.
(By at Dunedin Reporter).
When an occurrence of an unusual
character takes place, such as a high-
way robbery, the demolition of build-
ings by fire, the landing of a new Go-
vernor, the arrival of troops from the
seat of war, the unannounced departure
of an absconding bank clerk, or any-
thing else calculated to provoke a gene-
ral simmer of excitement, the patient
scribe is always ready to do his duty in
the interests of the world’s enlighten-
ment. by this desire a re-
porter recently visited Mr. William
Connell, of No. 122 Forth-street, Dune-
din, whose remarks are particularly
lucid. In answer to a. question Mr.
Connell smiled complacently, and said :
“ Yes; lam the man you are enquiring
about; but you look surprised. I sup-
pose you did not expect to spe me look-
ing so well. Thank God, I any f ound
and hearty now, and have been dur-
ing the past fifteen years. It is about
that time since I was taken very ill,
ancl I must say that my condition be-
came so critical that I despaired of ever
getting well again. I was working in
the tramway service, my duties consist-
ing in cleaning the tram-cars. Although
I invariably wore overalls and leggings,
I was many a time wet through to the
skin on cold, bleak nights'. Having no
opportunity to change my clothes, I was
obliged to go through my work as I
was—wet and chilled to the marrow.
No wonder, then, that my constitution
was completely broken down in time.
The whole of my system seemed petri-
fied with CQld, which I felt taking hold
of me for days before I was laid up.
The collapse came suddenly, for I tried
to get up to go to my work and found my
limbs refuse to move. My legs and arms
were useless, all my muscles and sinews
being contracted, My head ached and
throbbed in a dull, depressing manner,
making me feel more dead than alive.
Terrific'pains appeared right along my
back, and my shoulders felt as sore as
though they had been battered with a,
hammer. I became so helpless that I
could not pull myself off the sofa, and
t is no exaggeration to say that I used
to cry like a child with excruciating
agony in my loins. Even when sitting
in a chair’ I could not move, and for
three weeks I laid in bed unable to stir.
I was literally smashed up in health, be-
ing so weak that a child could have
pushed me down. When urinating I
noticed the water was thick and dis-
co' oured, a sure sign that I was also
suffering from kidney complaint.”
“ Were you able to take food, Mr.
Connell ?”
“Very little satisfied me, for it .only
required a mouthful or two of food to
produce pains emanating from a gaseous
fulness in my stomach. Then my chest
troubled me greatly. Quantities of gas
arose to my chest in such that
I thought it would burst/ besides ex-
tending along the breathing passages
and almost choking me. A very pecu-
liar thing happened every time I ate
anything; my head used to get com-
pletely covered with a cold sweat—a
probable result of the agonising tor-
tures I felt. If I had an inclination to
read I was prevented in two ways, the
first being that my sight was too bad,
and, secondly, my fingers were numbed
and powerless. Even my hearing was
affected, for I became as deaf as the
proverbial door-post. I was rather glad
of this latter affliction, as, whilst I was
capable of hearing, noise of any sort
was extremely irritating, and the sound
of children’s voices were intolerable.
I sank lower and lower, and
was very snappish and murky.
Previous to my illness I always
( had a disposition to be agreeahle, but
now I was quite the reverse. I was so
low-spirited and morose that I even be-
came suspicious of my surroundings,
having an inward dread of impending
d’sasder. My appetite soon fell away al-
tcge/her, my face was pale and shrunk-
en, and I had every appearance of a
person going into consumption. This
caused me no end of anxiety and worry,
especially as it was my first sickness
and there seemed every probability that
it would have a fatal termination. Even
the blessing of sleep was a stranger to
me, my aching limbs and bodily tqrtures
keeping me in a constant state of wake-
fulness. ' .both day and night I lay in a
state of stupidity, being only halt con-
scious of what was going on around me.
My lungs and chest were so stuffed up
with phlegm that I could scarcely catch
my breath sometimes, and I was often
afraid that I might fall off to sleep and
suffocate whilst in my slumber..' My
nerves were just like a fiddle unstrung
They were the only portions of my sys-
tem which showed any signs of life at
all. Try as much as I liked, I could not
keep them 'still, and if any unusual
sound struck my ears my nerves vibrat-
ed and shook for hours after. But it
was not only from the severe cold and
kidney complaint I suffered. My liver
! also went wrong, there being a fright-
fully bitter taste in my mouth, com-
bined with a constipated condition of
my bowels. My "tongue was covered
with a yellowish, moisture, the same
colour being noticeable in the matter
which I used to vomit so frequently.
Death would have been a source of re-
lief had it come, and I must confess to
wishing to die on many occasions. I
could not see any good in living with
my emaciated frame full of pam, es-
pecially as there was no apparent relief
at hand. Ail these violent and mad-
dening pains came through neglecting
my complaint in time. I was like a
man walking on and on without taking
a rest until at last I dropped. I then
thought what a fool I was not to have
tried to check the advance of my disease
when I felt it coming on.”
“Had you done so you would have
undoubtedly have saved yourself a deal
of suffering.”
“ i am sure of it. But it was not
until I was nearly dead that I consulted
a doctor. I shall never forget one day
trying to move my chair, and the conse-
quent unpleasant results of the at-
tempt. As I leaned forward, intending
to drag the chair under me, my hands-
slipped, and I fell to the floor. My wife
was out at the time, and I was so
frightfully weak and powerless that I
had to lie there till she returned and
picked me up.”
“ How did you get on when you
started- under medical treatment ?”
“Very badly. I did not make any
progress at all. All the medicine I took
were valueless. I was like a man
stretching his hand out in the dark in
the hope of reaching something for sup-
port, without any definite knowledge
of what was required. Eventually 1 was
told what I wanted to cure me. ‘ Cle-
ments Tonic will do it,’ said a friend,
and, sure enough, he was right. Aftei
everything failed, Clements Tonic drove
my cold away and restored the use of
my limbs, besides settling my nerves
and bringing back my appetite. My
lungs were cleared of phlegm, and every
ache and pain gave way to Clements
Tonic. I was soon strong enough to
work, by which time my kidney -and
liver diseases were eradicated. Clements
Tomio was my doctor, hospital, and
medicine all combined, and since T was
cured by that great remedy I have not
had a day’s sickness, Publish in any
way these statements.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, William Connell, of 142 Forth Street, Dunedin,
in the - Colony of New Zealand, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of three folios, and
consecutively numbered from one to three, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account, of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish In any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiv-
ing any payment ; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act of the
General Assembly of New Zealand, intituled “The
Justices of Peace Act, 1882."
Declared at Dunedin, this twenty-ninth day of
December, one thousand nine hundred,'before me,
FREDK. MALLARD, J.P.

A Question
Answered.
If the Pianola ent,
bles those who
have absolutely no
musical knowledge
to play the piano,
of what interest is
it to those who are
able to play ?
This is often asked, and when asked in our showrooms, generally resu’ts in the
purchase of a Pianola ; but more often the question finds expression only in
thought, and offers no opportunity for an answer.
PADEREWSKI HAS A PIANOLA in his home.
ROSENTHAL HAS ONE.
SAUER HAS ONE.
and only recently has ordered two more for friends in Germany, who are themselves
eminent musical critics. This certainly disposes of the question, although it fails to
give the reasou The reason is equally clear.
The Pianola increases the

repertory of even these musi-
cians from twenty-five to fifty
compositions which they can
play in concert form, to prac-

The Pianola does not mar

receives full opportunity for
expression to which the player

The Pianola offers relaxa-

enjoyment of the musical.
It is true, since the player
Pianola being placed in position to play Upright Piano.
controls the expression, the great musician obtains a more artistic result from the
Pianola than the novice, because he is more critical, but in this very point lies the
secret of the Pianola’s fascination.
The player, whoever he may be, gives his own individuality to his rendition.
This causes the musical taste to develop, and it is not long before the veriest novice
finds his own playing preferable to many of the pianistic efforts in the average concert.
Expression-marks appear on the music as it unrolls, for the guidance of those who
may be unfamiliar with the selection.
In this article there has been little attempt to explain how the Pianola is manipu-
lated, the principal object being to show the desirability of the Pianola for every one
who owns a piano, whether he be skilled in its use or not.

20 HIGHEST AWARDS
