His Friend, Charles Brown.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Charles Brown received a crushing blow. Un-
consciously, without any motive whatever, he had
peeped through the window of his sitting-room
when he returned home one evening, and had seen
what came to him with all the shock of absolute
surprise. He staggered, as though he had been
struck by a strong arm, and then rushed from the
place. As he tore madly along the street, away
from his home, the fresh, cool air soothed him.
Gradually he became calm, and so completely
mastered his feelings that, when he walked into
his house, his wife noticed nothing.
Days, weeks, months passed, and Charles
Brown made no sign. The shock that he had
received did not appear to have affected him.
But, after four mouths, there happened that which
sent the blood dancing through his veins with
delightful, intoxicating pulsation. The office-boy,
scampering home an hour earlier than usual, marked
the day with white chalk, for the ‘ ‘ boss ” had
told him to keep the change when purchasing
some cigars.
At 5 o’clock that day Charles Brown had a
visitor— a man in great trouble. With much
hesitation, he told of what haunted him. He was
heavily involved. Dishonor stared him in the face.
It meant ruin, exile. There was no hint of any dis-
honesty. Misfortune—carelessness, not fraud—
was the cause of the trouble.
Charles Brown closed the door of the outer-
office and turned the key. He came back with a
mild glow of concern and sympathy.
“Would not time brighten things?” he asked,
softly.
“Yes, time would brighten things,” the visitor
replied.
“Well, just now, I have not the money avail-
able to tide you over your little difficulties, but if
for a month the money could be obtained—only a
month —I, Charles Brown, your friend, will lend
you the money to pay back the loan, and all will
be well.”
The visitor breathed more freely ; hope leaped
into his eyes. “ But where can I obtain the
loan ? ” he asked, bitterly, hope drowned in
despondency.
“For a month—only for a month.” Charles
Brown bent forward till his mouth almost touched
the ear of the visitor, speaking very softly—“A
secret loan, perhaps not a month; a loan that
none need know about.”
The fear that shook the nerves of his visitor
was not unnoticed by Charles Brown, who sum-
moned all his mind to urge the point. “Is there
such a sum that you know of—money that is not
needed ; that will not be missed for a little time,
a month ? When it is wanted, I, your friend,
will have it for you.”
Fear faded from the visitor’s face, and hope
shone again. Money he must have. There was
money he knew of, money lying idle, money that
had not been touched for months, and doubtless
would not be required for as many more. If he
took it as a loan, only as a loan, he could tide
over his trouble. What things might turn up in
the meantime ? Perhaps fortune would smile on
him, after all. Whether or no, his friend, Charles
Brown, a man known throughout all the city as a
true man, would come to his rescue. There was
no risk, absolutely none.
The Post-Office clock struck six when Ernest
Leek, a civil servant,- given to horse-racing and
gambling in general, left the office with Charles
Brown’s words of encouragement in his ears.
At four o’clock next day he met his friend :
“Charles,! have done it!” —he trembled as he
spoke; “in my hour of need I must look to you.”
“You have never had, and never will have,
a truer friend than I shall try to be to you,” said
Charles Brown.
The month had almost passed and fortune had
not smiled upon Ernest Leek. “Wait,” his
friend, Charles Brown, had replied to an uneasy
reminder. “Wait. Have no fear, I have pro-
mised.”
The full month had passed when Ernest again
called upon his friend. “The hour is come,” he
said, trembling ; “I can’t hide the shortage any
longer.”
“ The time up ? ”
“Yes, it has come,” replied Leek. “You will
lend the money ? ”
“In a day or two. lam not ready yet.”
“In a day or two ! Man, it is a matter of
hours. In a day or two ! To-morrow at eleven
o’clock will be too late ! ”
“Is that so ? Then, my boy, it must be done.
Meet me here to-morrow at nine.”
“Charles, my friend, my saviour ! give me the
money now ! Let me feel that I have it. Let me
now, at this moment, put it back ! I will not feel
safe till it is there. My friend, my saviour ! do,
do give it to me now—now ! ” The distracted man
tugged at the arm of the other.
It may have been the hurry he was in that made
Charles Brown gaze unmoved on the face before
him. “Nine o’clock to-morrow. Train to catch,”
and he rushed away.
At a quarter to nine Ernest Leek stood at the
door of Charles Brown’s office, and his eyes
searched the street for the coming of his friend.
Nine o’clock. The Post-Office bell struck the
hour. But there was no appearance of Brown.
The minutes slipped by. Quarter-past—half-past
—ten. The great bell, with slow persistency,
tolled the hour. Still Leek waited. What could
it mean? He never asked himself the reason. All
thoughts were banished by the fear that had
possession of him. Quarter-past ten. Oh! the
agony of the time. The waiting man scanned every
face as the city’s slaves hurried on. The hands
seemed to race round the clock. In a moment of
fantasy he thought of some pony race of the past
at Kensington.
Half-past. It seemed but a minute or two, so
rapidly the hands moved round, when a quarter
to eleven struck. For two hours Ernest Leek had
waited. That time had been filled with the
greatest anguish of his life. With a strange fas-
cination he gazed at the clock. The chimes had
rung. The great bell began to knell the hour.
Leek counted the strokes—Eleven. 'All was now
lost.
As he turned away, dead hope stirred again, for
Charles Brown appeared. Was it yet possible to get
back to the office before his absence was noticed ?
If so, all was saved. “Quick, man—quick; the
money ! You are late, awfully late—l am afraid
too late ! ” and he followed Brown into the narrow
passage that led to the office. He grasped him
by the arm—“ The money, man! the money!
Every moment is risk. Quick, Charles, the
money ! ”
Charles Brown did not answer.
“Good God! What is the matter ? Can’t you
understand ? The inspector is there, waiting for
me. Give me the money. Quick ! Quick, and
let me get back ! ”
“ I have not got it ” The voice was even, and
free from emotion.
Leek looked at him dully ; he did not at first
comprehend. “Not got it?” Then the full
meaning of that fact came to him; and he was
calm with the calmness of despair. “Then I am
lost. I depended upon you.”
“ Get back to the office. Don’t wait, get back.”
“ But the ”
“Don’t wait, get back. I’ll follow,” and he
pushed him into the street.
* * 9 m
Six weeks later, Ernest Leek became a prisoner
in Bathurst gaol. A week after, Charles Brown,
returning from Melbourne with his wife, who had
spent the previous six months there, crossed over
from Harden on to the Western line to stay a
night at Bathurst. The next morning, accom-
panied by his wife, he presented himself at the
door of the gaol. An official order gained them
admittance.
Ernest Leek was in deep, puzzled thought when
the warder told him visitors had called. It was
the first time he had had any. So many regulations
were dinned into his ears by the warder that he
felt dazed and confused. He was led down a
narrow passage, then ordered to stop, and, turn-
ing, found himself facing a barred window which
looked into another, but wider, corridor. The
man standing in front, a little distance from him,
was his friend, Charles Brown.
“Charles Brown!” The voice was so altered
that Brown almost failed to recognise it.
“Yes, Ernest Leek, I have come to see you,
to see you here, here in your new home. And I
have brought someone else to see you —to see you
here. ”
Brown stepped back and revealed his wife. To
the woman and to the prisoner it was unexpected.
She, with a piteous effort, thrust a trembling hand
through the bars. The warder stepped forward
just in time to catch her as she fell.
They put her upon a bench, the prisoner looking
through the bars at them. The swoon soon
passed.
“Charles Brown,” the prisoner called out.
Brown walked towards the grating, a smile
on his face.
“Charles Brown,” said the prisoner, “you
know ”
“All! I left the banquet early on the 24th of
May. Some Providence that watches over the
injured prompted me to look through the window
of my sitting-room before knocking. I saw all.”
“ And this ? ” asked the prisoner.
“ Is my revenge—my revenge on both of you ! ”
ELLIS WALTER.
How They Part—in the Bush.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Laden with heavy swags, two men dragged their
way through the droughty country.
They possessed but one billy; it might, there-
fore, be safely augured that they were mates of
some standing. The one who carried the billy
led —he always does in the bush —and each one, as
he brought a boot forward and implanted it in
the flour-like dust, raised a small halo around
that foot; the men were also surrounded with a
general halo of the same material.
In consequence of the dust, and of having
tramped about a thousand miles in each other’s
company, their mouths had been fast closed since
breakfast. It was now mid-day, and they were
walking over a ridge which was dustless.
“The tank should be at the bottom of this rise,”
said he of the billy.
“I hope it’s better than the last water,” re-
turned the other.
“Well, in another two days we’ll be at the
river—that’s water, if you like.”
“ And bathing ? ”
“Yes, and fishing, and tucker of all sorts, and
oranges, and grapes ”
. “ And peaches, you said, didn’t you ? I’m par-
ticularly fond of peaches.”
“Oh, yes ! —plenty of peaches—and we’re
pretty sure to get that job, I’m thinking.”
“Don’t see what anyone wants with a job in a
country like that.”
This was meant for a joke, and the grimmest of
small smiles momentarily showed itself on the
faces of the two men—the first for a thousand
miles.
They had now arrived on the top of the ridge.
“See that hill there?” said the leader— “ it’s
behind that—the river.”
They passed down the decline. The tank was
fairly full, and they proceeded to unroll their
swags and boil the water for tea. Their eyes
were fixed on the billy, but their thoughts were in
the land of promise. At last it boiled, and the
leader of the expedition measured in the tea with
the lid of a small, battered canister.
“Why ! ” exclaimed the other, in an indignant
and pained voice, “you’ve put in two and a half
lids instead of two, and you know it makes it too
strong for me. I only told you this morning.”
The tea-maker muttered something unintelli-
gible. The other looked at him resentfully for a
short space, then pressed his lips together and
walked to the tank. He took a long, persistent
drink, then returned, and with the same deter-
mined look, and in silence, carefully rolled up his
swag, and as carefully fixed it on his back in the
orthodox manner, and, still without a word,
started back in the direction from which they had
come.
His mate watched him till his figure was
definitely outlined against the evening sky, and
the well-known old hat had sunk from sight on
the other side. He had not looked back once.
Something like a sigh escaped the man sitting
by the fire. He threw another half-lid full of tea
into the still gently bubbling billy—and added the
sugar.

J. F. MILLINGTON.
A collection of genuine “ spoonerisms ”
would be amusing. A Ballarat (Vic.) verger was
accosted, one Sunday morning recently, by a visitor
to church with the startling request, “ Would you
kindly sew me into the sheet ? ” It was a parson
in the same city who affirmed that Jonah was three
days and nights in the “welly’s bale”; but,
becoming conscious of the error, immediately cor-
rected it by saying, “ I mean the bale’s welly ” !
John Bull is so self-consciously upright
that when he wishes to do a crooked thing for the
sake of the “ higher good,” he always takes pains
to give it a fair name. You never hear of slavery
in a British possession ; it is always “indentured”
labor, or “contract labor,” or “an effort to instil
into the minds of the natives some sense of the
blessings of settled industry.” In Rhodesia the
old Kaffir custom of ‘ ‘ lobola ” —the paying of a
price to the father for his daughter—has been
revived as a civilised ordinance, ostensibly for the
moral good of the natives, but really to force the
young men to hire themselves as contract
laborers to earn the “lobola.”

Love-Bidden.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was sweated along in the heat all day,
He was tied to the fence for hours,
Just because someone had something to say
To a girl in the burnt sunflowers.
But the heart of a maiden is hard to change,
However so strongly tried ;
And a man rode out to the Blue-Cap Range
As a sane man would not ride.
With a drifting rein, and a biting heel,
And a blistering greenhide thong—
For even a favorite steed must feel
When the Battle of Love goes wrong !
He has ridden the flanks of the chestnut red,
And his shoulder white with foam ;
And out on the ranges he turned his head
And punished a good horse home.
Oh ! he’s won some races, has Diomed,
In time that was none too slow ;
But the fastest gallop the old horse did
Was the night that a girl said “No !”
V.K.
WILL OGILVIE.
A Government Tubercle-Farm.
The Liverpool (N.S.W.) concentration-camp for
the reception of the aged and sick poor is variously
described as an hospital and an asylum. In regard
to its two huge consumption wards, at any rate, it
is neither, but rather a culture-broth for tubercle,
into which human beings are thrown, alive and
strong, and, after a couple of years’ misery,
dragged out dead.
* * ♦ •
This great and most successful State experi-
mental farm, where phthisis is, by intense culture,
brought to the highest pitch of perfected deadli-
ness, has, on the average of the last five years,
generally 165 prisoners under sentence of death,
and it kills them off at the average rate of 67 a
year. The probable life duration of any person
thrown into the Liverpool Culture Cauldron is,
therefore, a little over two yeai-s. Since that
space of time is mostly spent in pestilent air, amid
gloomy surroundings of death and decay, chained
for most of the time to one room, it would be, on
the whole, far more humane to chloroform the
consumptives to death on admittance, than
leave them to slowly and painfully cough their
lives up during the weary months. Death then
would be more merciful, and not more sure, for
one never hears of a cured patient in this
“hospital.”
“ I should not advise you to go,” a well-known
Sydney doctor said to a Bulletin man, who, hav-
ing seen the consumptive wards at Liverpool by
day, announced that he intended to pay a night
visit to see how things looked when all the inmates
were sleeping. “And why?” “Well, I won’t
guarantee your freedom from tubercle when you
come back.” That was a serious expression of
opinion on the doctor’s part. For himself, he
said, lie would not spend half-an-hour in one of
the consumptive wards at night unless at the call
of absolutely urgent duty. Yet to these wards
men, young and old, are admitted, almost daily—
at first with power to creep out of a day to a
narrow balcony, or a little paddock of the grounds,
but soon, however, to be confined to the one room
and the one bed, day and night, until merciful
Death brings freedom.
« * * *
Entering one of the consumptive wards, from a
passage-way whose musty, stale, warm odour
told of crowded humanity near, the writer
was not tempted to linger. A large room,
quite as large as a rich man’s dining-hall, fairly
lofty, dimly lit by gas-jets, was plotted neatly
into mattress-graves. There were 36 beds in all,
arranged so closely that one had just room to pass
between. A coal fire at one end burned brightly ;
the roof was patched with ventilators; and of the
long windows many were open a little at the top.
The air was hot, moist, sickly. It lay like a
dank mist over the beds, and the lights shone
through a faint nimbus of fog. The coughing,
violent as it sounded, of the inmates, did not dis-
turb the thick body of the atmosphere or affect its
sluggish currents. The tubercle bacilli, swarming
in colonies from the diseased lungs, met no unwel-
come breaths of sharp oxygenous air, but floated
peacefully, laved in this warm, still, moist medium,
living their lives, reproducing their species, grate-
ful in their bacillic consciousness for the kindness
of Providence to their little lives. It is a fine
Home for them.
* * * *
The occupants of the beds, the food of the
bacilli, steeped in the enervating, poisoned air,
filled in weary resignation the role which had
been allotted them. From nearly every bed came
the sound of cutting coughs, but generally patient,
smothered and restrained. Once a note, queru-
lous, sharp, angry, sounded—probably the cough
of a new arrival. Over the others brooded in the
thick air the gloom of hopeless dejection.
* . * * *
It is not all old men whom the State kills in the
Liverpool Consumptive “ Hospital.” Ihe bacilli
get much young food. There has just recently
been admitted a poor lad of 19 or so—a fine-looking
fellow. Before 21 he will probably be dead, before
25 certainly. Only a miracle could keep a consump-
tive alive longer under such conditions of existence.
During the last five years nine were admitted
under the age of 20 ; 141 under the age of 30, but
over 20; 252 over 30 and under 40 ; 241 over 40
and under 50 ; 119 over 50 and under 60 ; 60 over
60 and under 70 ; 4 over 70 and under 80. The
deaths in five years of those under 40 were 125;
of those under 50, 225. So it is not the aged poor
chiefly whose remnants of lives are sacrificed at
Liverpool for the culture of tubercle, but the
young men who should be, and could be vigorous—
the breadwinners whom carelessness after in-
fluenza, or pleurisy, laid open to infection, and
who, contracting consumption, are hurried there
to die.
* * * *
Mark that the lexicon of the modern scientific
medical practitioner knows no such word as fail in
the treatment of phthisis cases. No matter how
far advanced the disease, there is generally some
hope, if not of absolute cure, at any rate of
materially prolonging life. But tothe con-
sumptive who is poor in N.S.W. the State issues
a death warrant, dated perhaps two, perhaps
three years ahead, but as sure in its execution as
when a capital criminal is pinioned and noosed on
the scaffold. The bacilli of the Liverpool wards
may bungle their cases a little—may give to one
man a few more months of coughing than is
strictly necessary ; but they ahvays kill.
So far as the conduct of the institution is
concerned there is no reason for complaint. The
matron-superintendent is kindly and capable
All that can be done, under the system, for patients
is done. The System is the murderer. The place,
in spite of having so many phthisis, cancer, and
other cases, is not conducted as a hospital, but as
a poorhouse. There are five trained female nurses
on duty during the day : two trained male
attendants at night—these not for the consump-
tives alone, but for the whole institution ! There
is no resident doctor —the medical superintendent
lives some distance away in the town ! Inmates
have two hours’ liberty per week. Recently,
as a, concession to civilised ideas, the consumptive
patients have been allowed the use of a small
paddock during the day. Those who can get there
probably receive some momentary benefit; per-
manent benefit is impossible when the nights are
spent in the foetid wards. Every consumptive
admitted must quickly die.
* * * *
These facts are familiar commonplaces in
N.S.W. Government departments. Some weeks
ago they were again brought before Parliament by
Dr. Nash, M. L.C., whose interest in public health
affairs is untiring. A humanitarian with any
share of the responsibility for such a state of
things would not be able to rest a single night
without doing something by way of remedy. But
N.S.W. Governments seem to be trained in the
school of Kitchener, and nothing has since been
done. ■
Private Brigalow Jones on the
British Army.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Seems to me,” said Private Brigalow Jones, of
the North Australian Mounted Infantry (column
on trek in the Transvaal), “the English nation
didn’t do a wise thing in letting us come here.
We put them down at cricket, and rowing, and
pretty well everything like that, and th’ only
thing left up their sleeves was their glorious
British Army. So they filled us up with yarns
about their soldiers and the wonderful things they
did, and the nations they had walloped out. You
couldn’t help reading about them, from the time
you began to read your school-books. And they
didn’t forget it when we were being drilled to
come out here. 1 You’re a fine body of men,’ said
they, ‘the pick of North Australia. But, of
course, you must not expect to be classed with the
regular British Army.’
“ But how is it out here ? I give-in that the
British officer is a marvel. You have to wait on
him hand and foot, and carry round cartloads of
beds and tables and chairs and whisky, soda, bis-
cuits, chocolate and tinned stuff for him, and just
when the time comes for him to put his military
genius to work and wipe the Boers out, like he
was shown at Sandhurst, perhaps he has mislaid
his eyeglass, and the battle goes all wrong. That’s
what they call a ‘regrettable occurrence.’
“And the British soldier! Most of them are
about 5ft. 4in. high, and they all want to be
orderlies or cooks. The British Cavalry can’t ride ;
and, as for De Wet’s Own*, they’re just remount-
agents for the Boers.
“The only thing I know of that’s left like you
read about in the school-books and histories is
their fancy names. There’s ‘the Death or Glory
Boys,’ for one. And there’s the Fighting this
and the Galloping that. Whenever a new
mounted-infantry corps is formed you will notice
they are bound to call themselves the Furious
Fourth, or the Galloping Sixth. The Galloping
Sixth passed us in single file one day on the march,
after they had stayed behind to water their horses,
and as they passed I tapped a few mounts with a
sjambock. They galloped all right. There were
horses galloping over the plain for an hour after-
wards. The Galloping Sixth were after them on
foot.
“They don’t want to stop out here fighting.
‘It’s all very well for you Australians,’ they say ;
‘ you’re used to this kind of life, and you only
came out here for amusement, anyway; but this
ain’t soldiering.’ Living in a barracks, and doing
your regular round of guards, and being able to
have a look round town, with a giri to walk out
with, and the canteen handy—that’s what they
call soldiering. There wouldn’t be any war if
they had a say in it.
“They used to tell us that one Britisher was
worth three of any other nation. I haven’t seen
much of it. It would be a poor hundred Boers I
wouldn’t back against a hundred of them.
“There’s one thing would be worth knowing:
whether all the other nations are the same as the
British. I used to think that if any Russians
came along to Australia, it would pay us to buy
them off, rather than hurt ourselves trying to fight
them. I reckon now that they will very likely be
as bad as the British, if not worse. We could
turn out about 500,000 fighting men in Australia,
which would be equal to as many Boers, so if
250,000'British can’t wipe out 50,000 odd Boers in
all this time, I reckon it would take at least
2,500,000 men to put us down. They couldn’t well
carry that many to Australia, and feed them too.
I reckon now we’re quite safe.
“I suppose before they get through with the
war now it will run them into at least three
hundred millions, perhaps four or five, and the
Boers and disease have already put about 17,000
Britishers underground, too. Now, with ordinary
luck, we could have put the job through in less
than six months, and if they had paid us a
hundred and fifty millions for it, it would have
been dirt-cheap. That would have given every
man of us a good dividend, besides the profit to
every colony that provided men. The English
would have saved a tidy bit over it, and would
have had their 17,000 men left alive, and they
could have put them to work at some other game
than soldiering, for which they might be better
suited. Of course we should have contracted to
supply only the fighting men. The English would
have had to provide the cooks and Army Service
Corps, and all that, and men for lines of communi-
cation, we only wiping the Boers out for them.
“Next war, if Toby Barton’s got anjr sense,
,he’ll put in a tender. I reckon fay the time the
British have caught De Wet, and knocked out
Botha, they’ll be real tired, and when they see
our tender, they’ll say ‘Toby, old man, we’ve had
enough of day-labor, the contract’s yours.’ ”
Then Private Brigalow Jones, modest man,
turned into his tent and wrote the account of
another heroic exploit by an Australian, for a
Sabbath paper.
J.
The Imperial Yeomanry are known throughout South
Africa as “De Wet’s Own.”
